The Red Page
The Modest Muse
Sydney Partrige’s is a modest little muse,
clothed demurely iu sixpennyworth of book-
let, manufactured by the lvoolinda Press, N.
Kensington, Adelaide. The Lie and other
Lines is written by a good trades-unionist
without much help from that free-laborer,
Inspiration. She writes of “Traitors”—the
farmers w'ho foolishly became special con-
stables against the Wellington watersiders,
of “Capitalism”
Ah, the song of the terrible bush,
Twelve sheep bogged where the grass grows
lush,
Five crows caw on a ring-barked tree,
Droning their grace out thankfully....
Capitalist ('row from his place on high
Husoops down to pick out each worker’s eye,
Gorging his fill on the life-blood red.
Pitiless, heartless, unshamed by the dead.
Yet here is a little picture that, poetically,
is worth all her trades-unionism:—
SUND’Y.
Hund’y out here on th ’ s’lection,
With nawthin’ at all to do,
r Cept feed, th’ pigs an' th’ chickens,
An' pen up a poddy or two.
All right out here on th’ s’lection,
With nawthin ’ at all to do.
Th’ sky seems ’sif you’d jest passed it
Through a tub’ve mum’s washin’ blue.
Th' little clouds is like soap suds,
'Tween us an’ th' shiny sky.
There’s a line’ve long-bealced ivers
Flyin' lazy beyond our eye.
An’ down to th’ Cultivation
Dad, Mum, an' th’ baby've gone,
To lean on th’ sliprails admirin’
Th’ growth 've th’ tasselin’ (sawn.
Blossom ’n’ Gipsy ’re standin’
In th’ shade ’ve th' oV apple-gum,
An' th’ noise ’ve th’ leeks in th’ branches
Ts mixed with th’ honey-bees’ hum.
An’ me an’ th’ daivgs ’n’ our Billy,
’N’ a friendly neighbor or two.
Have d dead-ripe time on a Hund’y
With nawthin’ at all to do.
Another modest little muse is M. Hookey’s,
also featly dressed (in green and white)
by The Bookfhllow, charmingly deco-
rated by L. Decnainenx, and presumably
priced at 6d. In The Edge of the Field M.
Hookey gives us only six poems—the I’est
are neatly done translations. Inspiration,
here, too, is wanting; but technique and
much talent take its place. Two of the six
poems adequately express M. Hookey’s in-
tent :
THE UNNAMED FLOWERS OF THE TASMANIAN
BUSH.
Siceet nameless flowers that blossom all un-
known
In woqdland places—whose bright, starry
eyes
Bee waving shadows and blue sunlit skies
But never face of man. The forest’s moan
Echoes about you, and the silver tone
Of dripping water, and the myriad sighs
And voices of the bush, and all the wise
Glad speech of Nature. These are all your
own.
But unto you no pobt lifts his ?ong,
Dowering your silent sweetness with a name
That you may live by in the deathless fame
Of fond remembrance. You are far away,
Swinging sweet censers ever, where the long
Hlant sunbeams crown you with the gold of
day.
THE HOUN BELLE. (A mountain in
SOUTHERN TASMANIA.)
Spellbound amid her sleeping sentinels,
Through tn& long ages dreams the Huon
I {(‘lie. —
A silent face amid the mountains grey.
Lying with stony features upward turned.
Heedless of heat or cold or the white snow
That every winter makes her forehead hoar.
Long hath she slept, so long that wind-blown
trees
Grown bolder in the stillness, have up-
climbed
O’er the wan cheek and, in wild whispers
low.
Breathe their strange music to the silent Ups
That answer not but guard their secret well,
j Lost in the solitude of silver clouds.
Scott’s South Pole
It will always be that. Amundsen got
there first; but Scott made it his. And
Scott’s Bast Expedition (Smith, Elder, per
Dymock’s Library), in tw r o big. finely illus-
trated volumes, vividly tells the story of an
expedition equally famous for the heroism
of its members, for the almost incredible
hardships they underwent, for the enormous
amount of exploration they achieved, for the
scientific results they attained, and for the
pathos of the tragedy of the Polar party.
The first volume is made up entirely of
Scott’s own journals—that wonderful de-
tailed record of misfortune met with
the white man’s bravery. Yet the second
volume contains stories of expeditions as
hazardous, as wonderful and as exciting,
and of misfortunes as bravely and as brightly
borne. There is that winter journey to
Cape Crozier with the telling extracts from
Cherry-Garrard’s diary, fully confirming
Scott’s opinion that it was “the hardest
journey ever done.” There is the equally
thrilling narrative of the northern party,
under Campbell, compelled to winter under
conditions (hat have never been met with nor
conquered before; the interesting western
journeys by Australian members among
glaciers, the long wait at the winter quar-
ters without news of Scott, tiie spring
search-party, and the discovery of the
bodies. Added are the account of the voy-
ages of the Terra Nova, and summaries of
the scientific results achieved. And through-
out there are the marvellous photographs of
Herbert Pouting.
&
Necessarily this work will be Compared
with Shackleton’s and Amundsen's. First,
it must be admitted that, had Scott lived,
he would have made a better book of it. Both
Shackleton and Amundsen w’rote their stories
at ease, building on the bare records of their
diaries a consecutive and well-integrated
story. They were able to amplify brief
notes with dramatic effect. Thus Shackleton
in his narrative of his return almost con-
sciously heightens the dramatic quality of its
climax; while Amundsen, 1 , the conqueror,
writing with lightness and ease, looks back,
r.:d sees hardly any hardships on his track.
His description of his winter quarters, too,
is much more brilliantly done than Scott’s.
Amundsen was a vmter as well as a doer:
there is a delightful literary touch in all his
work: Scott was a doer all the time, a
plugger even in his diary. Yet, to the reader,
the bare records of his diary are more than
literature: one must read between the lines.
And comparing Amundsen and Scott one
sees the former as the man born for his job,
at home in his environment, a master of his
materials, a homely man with an hereditary
flair for polar travel, a man of infinite care
and caution and ingenuity: while Scott
shows as the dogged naval man, a born
leader, depending more for his success on the
ingenuity of his devoted followers, not really
at home with the conditions, making mis-
takes and bravely facing the consequences,
taking risks before which Amundsen might
have conceivably almost lightlieartedly turned
back, and going to his death without a word
of Complaint, taking the full blame for what
no man could be blamed, and perishing a
hero. Yet a man who lacked nothing in his
make-up except the infinite capacity for
taking pains that Polar exploration demands.
His diaries, carefully read, indicate this side
of his character. He chanced too much, ex-
pecting somehow to muddle through. He met
a run of bad luck —inconceivably ahd shat-
teringly bad. That was one of the chances
he did not sufficiently consider. He took
chances, too, in his transport. He experi-
mented with ponies, motors and dogs.
Amundsen took no chances ; be kept to the
proven merits of dogs; he knew how to
handle dogs; and he took them to the Pole
and back agaj'n—rather, he made them take
him fo the Pole and back again. Amundsen
was the professional; Scott, despite his ex-
perience, remained the amateur. Yet....
yet... .which, ethically, is the finer man?
The world needs Amundsens to do its w T ork ;
Humanity needs Seotts for her nobler ideals.
A man of singularly equable temperament,
a lovable man. Always making allowances,
always ready with encouragement and ex-
cuse for his companions, yet quick to cor-
rect and guide. He must have commanded
the uttermost loyalty of his party. (And he
was proud of his party.
I am very much impressed with the extra-
ordinary and general cordiality of the rela-
tions which exist among our people. I do
not suppose that a statement of the real
truth, namely, that there is no friction at
all, will be credited—it is so generally
thought that the mhny rubs of such a life as
this are quietly and purposely sunk in
oblivion. With me there is no need to draw
a veil; there is nothing to cover. There are
no strained relations in this hot.... Such a
state of affairs would he delightfully surpris-
ing under any conditions, but it is much
more so when one remembers the diverse
assortment of our company.... To-night
Oates, captain in a smart cavalry regiment,
has been “scrapping” over chairs and tables
with Debenhafn. a young Australian student.
It is a triumph to have collected such men.
Here are some snapshots from one of
Scott’s private letters :
Words must always fail me when I talk
of Bill Wilson. I believe he really is the
finest character X. ever met. Every quality
is so solid and dependable ; cannot you imag-
ine how that counts down here? Whatever
the matter, one knows Bill will be sound,
shrewdly practical, intensely loyal and quite
unselfish. . . .a quiet vein of humor pnd really
consummate tact... .Bowers, is a positive
treasure, absolutely trustworthy and pro-
digiously energetic. He is about the hard-
est man amongst us, and that is saying a
good deal. I shall have a hundred little tales
to tell you of his indefatigable zeal, his un-
selfishness, and his unextinguishable good-
humor. .. .The Soldier (Capt. Oates) is.. . .
a delightfully humorous, cheery old pessi-
mist —striving with the ponies night and day
and bringing woeful aoounts of their small
ailments into the hut.
It becomes increasingly evident as the
diaries are read that Scott knew that the
enterprise was a risky one. Take these
extracts in turn: —
I think our plan will carry us through
without the motors (though in that case
nothing else must fail)....
I fear to be too sanguine, yet taking
Everything into consideration I feel that our
chances ought to be g00d....
U bat on earth does such weather mean at
this time of year? It is more than our share
of ill-fortune, I think, but the luck may
turn yet....
How great may be the element of luck !
No foresight—no procedure—-could have pre-
pared us for this state of affairs.... I can-
not see that any plan would be altered if it
were to do again.. ._.
It is a very serious business if the men
(of one of the supporting parties) are going
to crack up....
, Things are not so rosy as they might be,
but we keep our spirits up and say the luck
must turn,...We are struggling on, consid-
ering all things, against odds. It vs a pity
the luck doesn’t come our way, because every
detail of equipment is right....We ought to
get through....
Only 85 miles from the Pole, but it’s going
to be' a Stiff up-pull both ways, appar-
ently. . . .
The worst has happened, or nearly the
worst. Bowers detected a black speck ahpad.
Soon we knew that this could not be a nat-
ural snow feature. We marched on, found
that it was a black flag tied to a sledge-
bearer ; nearby the remains of a camp;
sledge tracks and ski tracks going and com-
ing and the clear traces of dogs’ paws —many
dogs. This told us the whole story. The
Norwegians have forestalled ns and are first
at the Pole. It is a terrible disappointment,
and X. am very sorry for my loyal companions
....All the day-dreams must go; it will be
a wearisome return.... Good God ! this is an
awful place, and terrible enough for. us to
have labored to it without the reward of
priority.
Now for the run home and a desperate
struggle. I wonder if we can do it. .. .
Well, we have turned our back now on the
goal of our ambition, and must face our SOO
miles of solid dragging—-and good-bye to most
of the day-dreams !
J*
Here it is the place to kill the suggestion
made that the disappointment of coming
second contributed to the catastrophe. Scott
and his men were made of stronger fibre.
Nowhere in his diary do we find any maudlin
despair at not winning the prize. He was
too much worried over the increasing diffi-
culties of his long slog back. And mentally,
the disappointment could not affect men
who had so cheerfully overcome disappoint-
ments almost as great. Arctic explorers are
not made in that mould —except, of course,
the Japanese who wintered in Port Jackson.
When Scott first found that Amundsen was
on the same quest, he decided not to alter
his programme one iota. He would do what
he had come to do: rightly, he would not
take the risk of making it a race.
Then the increasing difficulties and
the growing doubts on the journey back—to
death.
I don’t' like the look of it. Is the weather
breaking up? If so, God help us. ...
We shall be lucky if we get through with-
put serious injury. . . .
Yet we shall pull through all right, D.V.
We are by no means worn out. .. .
There is no getting away from the fact
that we are not going strong. Probably none
of us. We are inclined to get slack and slow
with our camping arrangements, and small
delays increase. .. .But it’s no use ineeting
troubles half-way, and our sleep is all too
short to write more.'. . .
This was when Evans had nearly broken
down in brain, the day before he collapsed
and died.
We think that. . . .his downward path was
accelerated first by the shock of his frost-
bitten fingers, and later by falls during rough
travelling on the glacier, further by his loss
of all confidence in himself. Wilson thinks
it certain he must have injured his brain by
a fall. It is a terrible thing to lose a com-
panion in this way, but calm reflection show’s
that there could not have been a better end-
ing to the terrible anxieties of the past
w r eek....
We are in very queer street since there
is no doubt we cannot do the extra marches
and feel the cold horribly. .. .Amongst our-
selves we are unendingly cheerful, but what
each man feels in his heart I can only guess
. . . .Things look very black indeed. ... I don’t
know what I should do if Wilson and Bowers
weren’t so determinedlv cheerful over things.
And so to the end of the terrible, heroic
story.
It is pathetic to note that if Scott had
been able to lay down his One Ton Depot at
lat. 80, as was his intention, the Polar party
would have reached it. But owing to the
state of the ponies on that depOt-laying
journey, Scott was compelled to make that
depot in lat. 79deg. 29min. “It is a pity wm
couldn’t get to 80deg.,” he remarks in his
earlier diary. OA hik return he and his
party struggled back well past the latitude
where the depot should have beep.
The Inlander, Vol. 1., No. 1, a quarterly
magazine dealing with national interests
from the Outbacker’s point of view, edited
by a Seotchbyterian minister, John Flynn
and published by the Home Mission Board
of the Presbyscotchian 'Church of Australia
gains this ribald page’s approval. Any
magazine that interests us in the undeve-
looed centre of the Commonwealth, as
this publication does, by means of admirable
photographs and well-wri,tten description, is
doing useful work; and any religion that
believes in Home missions instead of collect-
ing white Australian threepennies to convert
yellow piccaninnies has our commendation
Also, Flynn, who is a kind of Centralian
sundowner, has the biggest diocese of any
prelate on earth, and ministers are as scarce
there as we trust they will be in Hades.
This admirable and optimistic quarterly can
be purchased at all booksellers, God-fearing
and otherwise, for Is.

his Mates.
Yachtsmen will find entertainment and
interest in C. C. Lyuam’s illustrated account
of his yachting cruises To Norway and the
North Cape in " Blue Dragon II.” (Sidgwick
and Jackson, Gs.). The author was awarded
the challenge cup of the Royal Cruising
Club for this remarkable cruise.
For the kiddies: Maoriland Fairy Tales, by Edith
Howes (Ward, Lock; 3s. 6d.), illustrated in color
and nicely written. The Children’s Blue Bird, by
Georgette Leblalic, who happens to be Mrs. Maeter-
linck, illustrated (Methuen ; 55.), the story of the
play, specially written for children, and remarkable
for Albert Rothenstein’s colored illustrations, quite
“futurist” in conception. The Adventures of Akbar,
by Flora Annie Steel (Heinemann; 65.), illustrated
in color by Bvain Shaw. Mother Goose: The Old
Nursery Rhymes (Heinemann; 65.), one of the finest
things that “Anon.” has ever written, and illus-
trated by Arthur Rackham, with an art so dainty
and so grotesquely humorous that this book will be
bought, as most mechanical toys are bought, to amuse
melancholic fathers. Albert Rothenstein’s “futurist
work is more suitable for children, since it is just
the sort of stuff ther could do themselves.
For bridge fiends: How to Win at Auction Bridge,
by “Cut Cavendish” (Laurie). This _ work deals
with the “Royal Spades” variety of this gamble.
There seem to be some more of these
“Australian Corellis” about-—or is Harling-
ham Quinn, the author of Hands o’ the
Desert (The Specialty Press, Melb.), the
same lady as Lindsay Russell? If no t
Harlingham is a plagiarist— ‘not of phrases,
but of the Corellian essentials of popularity:
the wicked Roman Catholic priest, the good
young Roman Catholic priest, the noble-
natured girl whom he kisses, and the Direful
Doubt that assails that good young priest
jufet after he has embraced the girl, whether,
after all, he had really received a “call.”"
In Hands o’ the Desert the girl is called
Roma; she is surrounded with “pompous
footmen” and has “foaming lapes at her
breast.” She lives at a place called the
Capitol, or, more definitely, the Capitol of
Australia, the author having confused the
capital city of Australia with one of its
possible buildings, the Capitol. Also, the
wicked priest is referred to sometimes as
Father Dealabury and sometimes as Father
liealabury. The story is all so sad. Itoma
meets the good young priest in the town-
ship, whither she has been sexH for a rest:
cpre from the “social whirl” down at the
“Ca-pitol.” He kisses her once or twice, and
they know that they love each other—-and,
all the time, Roma is engaged to an Earl!
He is not only ainEarl, but he is a “scion”
—“he was a scion of one of the oldest and
proudest families in England !” Probably
the last “scion” left in literature. And
the good young priest decides to leave the
Church, and comes down to the gaiety of the
social whirl at the “Capitol” and sees her at
a dance, and....but it is all too sad. lie
goes back to the backblocks, and Roma dies,
and Japan attacks Australia, and America
comes to Australia’s aid ; but it really doesn’t
matter much, since Roma is dead. And the
saddest thing of all is the frontispiece show-
ing the good young priest standing at the
church door watching Roma go by. accom-
panied by a child: and underneath this
pathetic legend: “Regrets—What Might
Have Been.” Our compliments to Ilarling-
ham Quinn, and to Lindsay Russell.
“Soldier though he was, he quivered before
this woman, whose every whim had to be
obeyed. He knew, only too well, were he at
Land’k End and she choose to recall him, he
would immediately return to be her slave.
Such, indeed, is woman’s magic power over ,
man.” This is Gladys Lewis, in The Curse
of Passfion (Melbourne Publishing Co.,
Cromwell Fundings, Melb., Is. 3d.). The
author has learnt the trick of The Pansy
Novels, and deals only in Oantains of the
Grenadier Guards and Duchesses. In the
end, too. “At the first Court of the season,
Nita had been presented to their Most
Gracious Majesties.” Won’t some kind
friend, in the interests of Australian litera-
ture, go and drown Gladys?

VERIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ballade of Cheap Beer.
Life is a wearying round of care,
Trouble, expense and fume and fret;
Somehow or other, ’tis getting rare
To meet with a man who is not in debt.
Everyone, more or less, hates to pay,
Save for a ticket in Tattersall’s sweep,
Or for a seat at a picture-play—
Why should we worry when Beer is cheap?
Rents have gone up, and vile landlords dare
Tenants to leave; while the board “TO
LET,”
Which in old days grinned everywhere,
Lurks in a cellar as black as jet.
Clothes have just doubled in price to-day—
Footwear has taken an upward leap;
Clearly, high prices have come to stay—
Why should we worry when Beer is cheap?
Mention of food makes a father swear!
Fish is a thing you can hardly get;
Meat’s at a figure to make one stare;
Fruit we go short of, with much regret;
Living grows dearer, strive a; we may;
Old age and creditors on us creep—-
Is it a Wonder our hair turns grey?
Why should we worry when Beer is cheap?
Envoy.
Prince, if you hold it, let’s be gay !
At the Procession just one more peep !
Though the teetotallers whine and bray.
Why should we worry when Beer is cheap?
N.S.W.
O. C. CABOT.
My Lady’s Shoon.
Shall my lady be shod with rose-leaf pink
Or the green of leaves at the water’s brink?
Shall my lady be shod with hyacinth
Or blue of a windless ocean?
Shall my lady be shod with rainbow shoon,
Or grey, as the end of the afternoon?
Shall her slippers be chameleon-like
To mirror her least emotion?
Shall my lady choose for her little feet
A silver buckle all trim and neat,
A scarlet bow or a wide rosette
Or a diamond’s chilly glimmer?
Shall her choice be merely a russet brown
To mate with a modest morning gown?
Shall mv lady cover her feet with naught
But a stocking’s silken shimmer?
Let her have her will for her brief, bright
day,
Copy the blossoms that blow and sway,
Rain-white lily or sunny rose
That out of the dawn had birth.
It will come to her, as it comes to all.
The tale of the leaves that fade and fall —
And my lady’s feet at the last be shod
With a little crumbling earth.
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
Remarks to a Foggy Love.
Draw near and let me look upon thy face.
The stars are gone and morning lights the
dome
Of all the shrouded sky. Thou hast a place,
O love, on this warm breast that is thy home,
Though errant is thy step and sad thy pace,
And thou, mayhap, too full for sound or
foam.
Here let us sit beside the foggy sea
And hear the bells that clamor to the boats;
Thy drooping whisker says, and over thee
A flavor as of perished gladness floats;
The seat is somewhat wet, but such as we
May feel love stir beneath our overcoats.
Perchance last night you plucked in Gulistan
A rosebud passing fair beneath the lights.
It is the ancient privilege of man
To wander wide in thick, absorbent nights;
And ever since this tangled world began
To talk at morn was one of woman’s rights.
The rose that yesterevening bloomed so fair,
Pink-white against the bottles ranged with
art,
This morn of haggard fog disdains to wear
Beside the chafing of its wintry heart;
Somewhere there slumbers with discordant
hair
A joy that in the daylight hath no part.
Perchance in oystered palaces remote
Your manly form with crayfish you sus-
tained,
And felt Italian airs about you float,
Or old Greek smells that could not be en-
chained.
Of such events as these I take no note ;
I would not have all mysteries explained.
Nay, speak not, for the damps of morn are
loose
To hide your wreckage from the public view.
There may be plenteous reason and excuse
In various vintages for all you do ;
Nor is my morning reason so obtuse
That I should ask repentant lies from you.
Your tie is ranged obliquely ’neath your ear,
Your collar bursteth from the yielding stud,
Your broken hat proclaimeth midnight cheer
And primal comfort older than the flood.
Yea, to my loving eye it seemeth clear
The chief adornment of your coat is mud.
Let these things pass. This silver hour of
chill
Is ours for joy before the fogs arise;
Lo, at the touch of your wet lips I thrill,
And read my future in your jaded eyes.
Your hand shall sign the cheque and pay the
bill —
So much is certain, and the rest all lies.
While you have cash, why should my soul
repine?
The sun will come e’er long to cheer the day.
Love wraps us in his mystery divine,
And our delight no passing gloom can slay;
For life’s young lustre has a glint like wine
As long as I can smile and you can pay.
n.s.w.
MARY McCOMMONWEALTH.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some
care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official alivays is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable,, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” col-
umn all -contributions submitted, whether in the form
of Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short
Tales of Sketches (those deoling with Australian sub-
iects. and not exceeding tivo columns in length, or,
say, SOOO words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of
MS.

THURSDAY, DECEMBER 25, 1913.
ANOTHER FEDERAL ACT REACHES
THE ASH-BARREL.
The Privy. Council decision in the Sugar
Commission case Was cabled to Australia
last week. It dealt with the validity or other-
wise of the Royal Commissions Acts of 1902
and 1912, so far as concerned their power
to force witnesses to answer questions. Two
members of the Australian High Court—the
usual two—had held that the Acts were
valid, and likewise the questions put, under
them, to the C.S.R. Co.’s employees; the
other two members—the customary brace —
had held that the Acts wore valid, but that
the questions were not. Chief Justice Grif-
fith gave his casting vote in the inevitable
direction, and the Commonwealth appealed
to the Privy Council. The latter has now
decided that the Royal Commission Acts are
ultra vires, lock, stock and barrel. The
chief point taken, so far as can be gathered
from the weirdly-involved cable message to
the daily papers, is that neither statute nor
common law gives any Legislature the right
to so interfere with the liberty of the sub-
ject as to compel the latter to disclose facts
about his private business. It may be good
law, but, like so much else in the same line,
it is rotten justice, and pernicious in its
effect on the general welfare. As a result
of previous legal decisions, the Australian
Parliament, which was intended by its
creators to have the completest powers in re-
gard to the control and regulation of injurious
Trusts, and which, for many years, believed
that it had those powers, is almost impotent
to deal with any sort of Trust whatever.
An intra-State Trust it cannot touch at all.
And when it is remembered that the re-
cently-arrived U.S. Beef Trust is an intra-
State Trust, by reason of the fact that it is
registered in Queensland and does not, in a
legal sense, extend to more than one State,
the tragic' seriousness of this disability be-
comes apparent. And Parliament can’t in
any way interfere with even an inter-State
Trust or or Combine unless it is able
to prove what is known to the Law as “in-
tentional detriment,” which is rather more
difficult to establish to the satisfaction of a
court of justice than a simple method of
squaring the circle or producing perpetual
motion. The case showed that much
conclusively.
If a country is not in a position to exter-
minate or even curb its Trusts, injurious or
otherwise, the least it might fairly hope for
is a little information from time to time as
to how much it is being robbed of by these
organisations. Nothing is. healthier than
light. The Trust bosses will cheerfully do in
the dark things which for very shame, and
regaidless of any legal immunity they might
possess, they would shrink from perpetrat-
ing beneath the arc light of publicity. The
amount which the community at large does
not know of the way in which it is being
taken down, day in, day out, would fill a
reservoir. Commissions, which have inquired
of late into all sorts of diverse problems,
from the advisability of introducing the
Totalisator to the reasons for the rise in the
cost of living in Australia, are powerful
agents for combating this ignorance. For
example, but for the Commission which ex-
amined into the Food Supplies question in
N. S. Wales, how many would know of the
Sydney Bread Ring? This Ring is controlled
by the Master Bakers’ Association, which
from time to time fixes the price of bread in
Sydney and the suburbs. This artificially-
fixed price varies in accordance, more or less,
with tiie price of flour, which price is also
artificially fixed by the Millers’ Association.
The phrase “more or less” is used advisedly,
since, as the Commissioner noted in his re-
port, “there is no real relation between the
declared price of flour for the time being and
the price of bread.” If a rise occurs in the
declared price of flour —the declarers being,
of course, the Millers’ Ring—the Bread Ring
puts up Uvi price of bread. Often, however,
the millers inform the bakers that the. de-
clared price of flour is shortly to be raised,
but that they may have as much flour as they
want at the lower figure if they take it on
the spot. The bakers buy at the cheaper
rate ; but they lift the price of bread all the
same. Bread is costly in N. S. AA’ales, not
because the Master Bakers are thieves (as
a matter of fact, they are mostly honest), but
chiefly because the milling trade has been
driven to the use of Trust methods to save
a number of its members from extinction.
It suffers from over-capitalisation, an effect
of which is that while one mill can sell its
flour profitably at £8 a ton, another is liable
to bankruptcy if it sells at less than £lO.
The combine accordingly fixes the price at
£lO, which gives the well-run mill an ab-
surdly large profit, and the other a livelihood.
The bakers don’t object. The only party to
be prejudiced is the general public, which
pays about twice as much as it should for
its bread. It is obviously desirable that the
public should know what is going on in all
such cases—as desirable as it would be for a
private citizen to know that his home was
being burgled, or, to cite what is perhaps a
fairer- analogy, that one of his employees was
so incompetent as to be causing the'business
serious financial loss. If the milling and
baking businesses are nationalised ulti-
mately, as a matter of necessary economy, it
will be due largely to the revelations made
before the Food Supplies Commission —
revelations which never could have been
made had the witnesses acted as the Privy
Council has apparently decided that such
witnesses are at liberty to act.
* * * *
An outstanding effect of last week’s deci-
sion, is, therefore, to make Rings and Trusts,
big." and small, more unassailable than ever
•in Their operations will, so far
as can be gathered, be shrouded henceforth
in complete mystery. There will be no like-
lihood of the public being lashed to action
by a realisation of the wrongs which it
is suffering at the hands of any given
Trust, since the people are to have no fur-
ther chance of discovering anything. It
makes the necessity of anti-Trust legislation
—not the bogus, variety we now possess, but
the real, effective thing—more essential than
ever. If Australia, is not to find itself pre-
sently in the position of a person whose
wholly unlighted and unprotected home is
being rifled by a vast gang of malefactors
in, about 1500 places, its electors will need
to drop their State Rights superstition and
hand the Commonwealth Parliament enlarged
industrial powers. Australia in the bulk
must make up its mind to trust its National
Parliament to the same extent, at least, as
the individual Australian trusts the average
policeman. Let the > Constitution be' so
•amended as to give, the Commonwealth
powers in regard to the control and regula-
" tion of Trusts as effective as those which
have been held for generations by the State
Assemblies,, and last week’s Privy Council
judgment will be comparatively innocuous.
But if the Constitution is to remain un-
amended the position looks like becoming
desperate. The Combines will be doubly
armed: with the cloak of secrecy as well as
with the shield of legal immunity. Aus-
tralia will become their recognised hunting
ground, a sort of. trade Mecca; and
where Swift’s alpne is new operating we
shall have, in n short time, harpies repre-
senting the entire U.S. Trust tribe.
THE PEACE SOCIETY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Now, in the tow.n of Tooralee
They formed a Peace Society ;
And they were noted near and far
From Ilitemup to Nastijar,
The folk of this Society,
For piety—true piety.
Thev met and talked from time to time,
And held all fighting was a crime,
A sin of dark variety
In ev’ry age and clime.
They scoffed and sneered at War’s alarms,
And said that folk who carried arms
Were to be pitied and despised
As.savnge ai d uncivilised,
Devoid of all humanity
Or sanity—true sanity ;
Seduced from happy, peaceful life
Bv bloody hates of gun and knife,
And led by martial vanity
To savag’ry and strife.
A.nd they declared with ve-he-mence
Against all measures for defence,
Maintaining that a peaceful pose
Was quite embarrassing to foes,
And gained for the community
Immunity—immunity.
They said no foe would ever harm
The nation that refused to arm,
Nor seize the opportunity
To raise the dread alarm.
Said they, if nations A and B
Sail battleships upon the sea,
The day will come when some excuse
They’ll coin to let the War Dogs loose,
And shock with their brutality
Morality—morality.
While nations D and E, who keep
No fighting ships upon the deep,
Preserve a’strict neutrality,
And all the blessings reap.
’Twas such a very simple plan,
Quite plain to any thinking man ;
For A and B, you understand,
Would never seek, by sea or land,
To tackle nations D or E
(In theory—good theory).
Though A and B might rend the skies
With cannon shot and battle cries,
With nation D, you see, or E
No trouble could arise.
The Peace Society soon grew
Quite popular, as such things do.
Its logic was so clear, you see.
And Michael Slattery, J.P.,
A well respected resident,
Was President —high President.
And Mr. Obadiaii Lee
Was Treasurer and Secret’ry—
Another noted resident,
As peaceful as could be.
But in the town of Tooralee
And in full many towns there be
A certain rowdy element
Which causes strife and discontent,
And often falls to bickering
When liquoring—wet liquoring.
Tim Monagin was such a one;
When sober he was full of fun,
But when he started shickering
He fairly took the bun.
Pat Lonagin, another lad
In whom the beer brought out the bad.
Had long with Monagin a feud
Which, when in liquor, he pursued.
And folk would cry, “There’s Lonagin !
He’s on agin—he’s on agin !
For all the day an’ half the night
He’s scoured the town in search iv fight.
Shure,. if he meets wid Monagin
’Twill be a dandy sight!”
The Peace Society was pained
To see this wicked feud maintained ;
And Michael Slattery, J.P.,
Suggested unto Mr. Lee
That they might, with impunity,
In unity—sweet unity—
Approach the ever-warring pair,
And reconcile them then and there.
They longed for opportunity
Their theories to air. '
The opportunity came soon ;
For on one summer afternoon
The President and Secret’ry,
The peaceful Slattery and Lee,
Came suddenly on Monagin
And Lonagin—wild Lonagin—
Engaged in sanguinary war ;
And. as they punched and kicked and tore,
Cried Monagin, “Come on agin!”
While Lonagin he swore.
The President said just one word,
’Twas all the few spectators heard ;
Then Lonagin he turned from Tim
To Slattery, and went,for him
With fierce assault and battery.
On Slattery—mild Slattery—
Came Lonagin with all his might
And landed him with left and right.
’Twould be employing flattery
To call the thing a fight.
And as for Monagin—well, he
Was busily employed with Lee,
Who wished, and with a wish immense,
He’d learnt the art of self-defence.
Blind rage and animosity,
Ferocity—ferocity—
Besieged the soul of Mr. Lee.
He longed to slay his enemy,
Who, ’spite his ebfiosity,
Was fighting mighty free.
They say the Peace Society
Is dead in distant Tooralee.
When next they met, the President
Confined his speech to one comment.
“Takes two to make a fight?” says he,
“Quite right,” says he —-‘*quite right,” says
he.
“But Peace Societies won’t do
Unless the other chap jines too!
I bid you all good night,” says he,
“As President I’m through.”
And as for Mr. Lee, he sought
A rude, uncultured man who taught
The useful art of self-defence.
He vaguely hopes that some day hence
He’ll get a battle on again
With Monagin—mad Monagin.
And then—but it were wise to state
That they who learnt the art too late
Are apt to find they’re gone again.
It isn’t wise to wait.
The lesson is a simple one:
If you refuse to buy a gun
You’ll meet your Monagin some day
And cut no figure in the fray,
Despite your notoriety
For piety—deep piety.
A foe’s a foe, howe’er you view
The matter; and it doesn’t do
To join a Peace Society
Unless he joins it too.
victoria.
DEN.
“A FULL-FLEDGED SOCIALIST
PARTY.”
Last week a N. S. Wales Laborite referred
to the possibility of a “full-fledged Socialist
party being the next to push the Holman
party out.” The Sydney Telegraph
(17/12/T3) affected to take this to mean
that the “full-fledged Socialists,” or I.WAV.
enthusiasts, were at present supporters of the
Labor party, but would be transformed into
enemies when Holman and his allies found
it impossible any longer “to feed them on
vain hopes.” The dishonesty of this line of
argument is so obvious as to be almost
pathetic. “Full-fledged Socialists” hate
Labor far more than they do Liberalism.
In fact, these extremists, being .Anarchists
and impracticable visionaries, have a strong
natural sympathy with the ultra-Tory wing
of the Liberal party, both in Federal and
State politics. The aspirations of the two
varieties of cranks are identical in a dozen
different directions. The I.WAV. section is
hostile to Arbitration and to legislative
palliatives generally; and so is advanced
Toryism. The Anarchist would rely on the
strike weapon exclusively in all industrial
rows. The Tory would be only too pleased
to meet strikes with lockouts. The full-
fledged Socialist is opposed to “conscription”
and to an Australian navy. The whole
Liberal party was furiously hostile to these
things a few years ago; and ultra-Liberals
are of the same way of thinking still.
Nominees of the I.WAV. organisation oppose
Labor candidates wherever there is a likeli-
hood of their splitting the vote with advan-
tage to Toryism. Toryism reciprocates by
turning down anything that resembles genu-
ine Liberalism, in favor of advanced Labor,
or any other old cause —as witness the atti-
tude of the reactionary wing of the Watt
party in the Victorian Assembly the other
day.
Labor in Australia does not stand for
home-bursting or communism or revolution or
any other insanity of the sort. The
alleged Tiger is essentially a sane animal.
Individual Labor Administrations may be
weak or extravagant, but the party as a
whole is proceeding in the right direction,
and the direction that the country approves.
And Liberalism must continue to go back
steadily until it makes up its mind to ad-
vance a very considerable distance along the
Labor track. Wherever the politicaFstudent
looks he finds Australian Liberalism standing
for a policy of dull, wooden, unimaginative
obstruction. In tjie Commonwealth Parlia-
ment, with a Liberal Administration, about
the most futile and profitless session on re-
cord has just come to an end. Nothing of
value has been done ; nothing has even been
seriously attempted. With the spectacle of
the American Beef Trust establishing itself
in Queensland, the need of effective Anti-
Trust legislation has become apparent to
unbiassed observers, not in Australia merely
but in the United Kingdom. But the Lib-
eral attitude is that, though no doubt Brit-
ain’s food-supply may be adversely affected
by the manoeuvres of the Trust, while'Aus-
tralia is likely to be called on ultimately to
pay extortionate prices for its meat, yet
interference with Private Enterprise is ab-
horrent. Also there is the consideration that
if the Trade and Commerce clause is amended
so as to allow of the passing of an effective
Anti-Trust Act, the Australian Parliament
may seize the chance thus given to do all
sorts of mad, inequitable things to petty
traders. It is on grounds almost equally
baseless that Federal Liberalism opposes
practically every other vital reform of the
day. And State Liberalism is mofetly in the
same disastrous boat or hulk.
* * * *
In N.S.W. last week ex-Treasurer Cann
had a few words to say about the huge for-
tunes that are coming to certain families, in
and around Sydney, by way of unearned in-
crement ; and he hinted at the prospect of
the Holman Government dealing dras- -
tically with the scandal. Immediately, there
were cries of horrified protest from Liberal-
ism. One of the party’s chief weaknesses
lies in the fact that its official leaders always
do moan bitterly in face of all such proposi-
tions. The Cooper family started acquiring
waste lands in what are now the Eastern
Suburbs of Sydney somewhere about the
Early-Victorian period. In a little while the
community, by building schools and convents
and colleges in or near their property, and
by running trams and omnibuses to it, and
by constructing in the neighborhood of it
parks and golf links and quays and swim-
ming baths and surf-sheds and hotels and ten
thousand other things of the sort, so ad-
vanced the Cooper income that the family
became Anglicised. The head of the house
is an English baronet with an English seat
and a London mansion. He was enabled,
the other day, to pave the way to. a seat in
the House of Lords by the gift of £50,0w
to the British cadet, movement—a transac-
tion that meant no more to him fhan the
disbursement of 3s. Gd. or some, such humble
sum might to the average'resident on his
Australian estate. With every y e | r tha
passes; with every yard of Eastern Suburb
railway that is built; with -.every new. tram-
way extension; and with every municipal or
other improvement which eventuates, tn
Cooper income must grow automatically
larger. The phenomenon will be due to no
thrift or foresight or genius on their nai •
Their childishly easy job will be merely t
sit down and bank the dividends while t
harassed taxpayers of N. S. Yales b° rr o
the money that goes to improve their pr*
perty. A party is not necessarily in taui
of confiscation because it is revolted at
spectacle of this sort. A large section
Liberalism is, however, by no means
volted: in fact, such spectacles appeal
this politically astigmatic crowd* as P
fectly equitable in every way. #
The direct and inevitable effect of tlijs
attitude on the part of this section of Libci


ism is to give a powerful impulse to the cause
of anarchy. The “full-fledged Socialists,” or
“Reds” or 1.W.W., or whatever one cares
to term the industrial irreconcilables, like
nothing better than the spectacle of one man
in the enjoyment of from £BO,OOO to £lOO,OOO
per annum, which he has not earned, and
which would not be his but for the efforts of
the working members of the community.
Anarchy rejoices at the all-too-often success-
ful struggles on the part of Conservatism
to check legislative inroads on these pre-
posterous revenues; the reason being that
each absurdly rich Australian family which
spends the greater part of its perpetually
growing unearned increment in England
drives fresh recruits into the ranks of com-
munism and revolution. A “full-fledged
Socialist party” can never win an election
on its own account. But with the priceless
help of blind obstruction in Tory quarters,
such a party may some time, some where,
oust the Labor Government of the day
That day may be a dark one for all con-
cerned; but as regards the representatives
or 1 ronerty, the gloom will be absolutely
Cimmerian in its inkiness.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Certain Ruffians Called Architects.
“Aedile” asks for room to swear:—
We have here in Sydney greater chances than any other
city in the world to get a beautiful skyline. Granted
that we have to change the natural beauty of our
picturesque hills, is that any reason why the coterie
ot ruffians who call themselves architects should
render it hideous, as they have rendered the archi-
tecture of Sydney streets hideous? Coming in from
-foreign parts the other day, the first thing that
struck rue was a vast new building in Macleay-street
a monstrous affair that might easily have been
made one of the most striking architectural objects
in any city in the world. How it destroys the sky-
line we ktvow from Ambassador James Bryce's con-
temptuous reference to it, and his comparison of what
he saw here with the graceful skyline and the strict
regard to landscape-beauty in Constantinople. Imag-
ine the depths we have fallen to that the Turk —the
unspeakable Turk—should be able to show us points
in architectural site-decoration! Then Martin
DonohoE corroborated this criticism—he who loves
Sydney, but knows and admires Constantinople. Yet
this Macleay-street monstrosity is not the only, or
the worst, offence. There’s a new one now disfiguring
Cremorpe Point. It’s a huge building of no archi-
tectural pretensions, and the point about it is that
it is built back-on to the water. The visitor from
abroad, entering the harbor, whose shores he has
been told are inhabited by civilised beings, sees a
horrid view of back windows and fiat walls and the
appurtenances of a house turned the wrong way.
It 'is an outrage. Surely the architect could have
faced the building round! Then, latest of all, we
have a new gigantic “flat” topping the hill in Darling-
hurst. There it sticks up like a jagged tooth invit-
ing extraction. It could easily and without the ex-
penditure of much money have been made a graceful
feature of the skyline; but, no—it must be made
hideously ugly. All even of our fine public buildings
save one or two are built with a back and a front—-
the back uncompleted, and the front not wholly
worthy of us. Some really fine buildings are wrongly
placed. • For instance, who put the Mitchell Library
where it is, instead of fitting it in the vista from
Bent-street ? They have deliberately hidden it behind
the Australian Club. Then the new Registrar-Gen-
eral’s office —a fine bit of architecture, showing off
the most beautiful building material I have seen
throughout the world —has been stuck so that it faces
nothing. It should have faced the angle of Hyde
Park, where a tree-lined avenue could have been
made leading up to it, and showing its striking fea-
tures. But enough! I turn with gratitude to the
Art Gallery, in the Domain, a perfect public build-
ing, with a back architecturally complete as well as
a front —a building placed on the crest of a hill, seen
with fine effect from the heights of Darlinghurst,
and having a beautiful frontage to the Domain. I
have, also hopes of the new Public Instruction build-
ing, which is complete and not one-sided, and not
designed with a Queen Anne front and a Mary Ann
back, so to speak. The old Darlinghurst Courthouse,
too, is a delight, and it should be preserved when
the gaol is remWerf. (Hurry up, Labor Govern-
ment !) Then the view of the dome of the Queen
Victoria Markets, seen from anywhere near The
Bulletin office, in the curve of George-street (where
the cow of the convict period slewed round a gum-
tree when she was laying out the plan of Sydney), is
a fair city vista. But I haven’t sufficient vitupera-
tion to say what I think of the Sydney Harbor Trust’s
awful doings at Circular Quay, Voolloomooloo, Dar-
ling Harbor, Gfoat Island and elsewhere; though
when I have seen how commercial wharves can be
made ornamental —as at Antwerp —I realise that
language is, at any time, a poor, inexpressive thing
to convey the thoughts that arise within me every
day in Svdney. If a builder or an architect at
Naples, Genoa, Cannes, Mentone, Messina, Hong-
kong or«any picturesque town of hills facing the sea
designed r building which had the front facing the
hill and the back lookimr on to the sea, the local
Camorra or Wi-pu-fu or Mafia would have been pro-
voked to make a clean job, and the building would
have been pulled down with public rejoicings flower
fetes by day and fireworks in the evening.
Common Sense and Sex-Diseases.
Queensland may fairly claim to be the
first English-speaking country to adopt a
common-sense method of dealing with the
venereal disease question ; though the
Health Department of New York began a
notification system two years ago, the pull
of police and municipal graft was too strong,
and the new departure was judicially ruled
to be illegal. The Bananaland crusade is
planned on lines advocated by a meeting of
experts at the Australasian Medical Con-
gress in 1911. After a bitter fight against
the adherents of the antiquated Contagious
. Diseases Act of 1564, the necessary
statutory powers were included in the
Health Act Amending Bill of - 1911.
This Bill included' eure-food legislation,
with powers ' to coerce recalcitrant and
filthy municipal councils, and other
matters of vital importance; but it was
nearly 'blocked by the senile opposition of
the ancients in the Deg. Council, who, as
usual, could not be made -to see that the
world had moved on since 1564. However,
ith-survived, and operations were begun by
the Health Department. These include
compulsory notification by medical men of
all cases sof . primary syphilis, secondary
syphilis, and 1 gonorrhoea seen by them ; a
free dispensary, opeji at convenient hours to
either sex; drastid prohibition of the treat-
ment of venereal disease by other than medi-
cal men ; free in-patient treatment of severe
cases; systematic examination of pro-
stitutes ; and powers to compel any person
of either sex who is considered by the
Health Commissioner to be infected with
venereal disease to submit to medical exami-
nation. Infected persons may be' detained
by magisterial order for further examination, -
and may also be segregated by order of the
Governor-in-Council until they are free
from infection. Prisoners may be examined
by the medical officers, and if found to be
venereally infected they can be detained irre-
spective of the length of their sentence until
they are certified as free from infection.
This and a provision for treating as a
vagrant anyone who is found to be living on
the earnings of prostitution are aimed at
the male parasites by whom the infection of
prostitutes is largely maintained and spread.
One notable feature of the scheme is the free
distribution of supplies of salvarsan (“606”),
and similar specific but expensive remedies
for syphilis, to all hospitals in the State.
The only condition attached is that a full
history of each case in which these supplies
are used shall be furnished by the hospital
surgeon to the Commissioner of Public
Health. Queensland country hospitals for-
merly had a habit of refusing admission to
sufferers_ from venereal disease, basing their
utterly illegal action on some Wowserish
notion of “morality.” A peremptory ukase
from Home Secretary AprEL to these State-
aided institutions put a prompt stop to this
mediaeval practice. Solicitation by prosti-
tutes in public places is forbidden by law,
and residence in a house of ill-fame is pun-
ishable by fine or imprisonment. In the
case of girls or women, magistrates may
substitute a term in a reformatory, or may
release the offender conditionally upon good
behavior for a specified period. 'Any person,
of either sex, who knowingly infects any
other person with venereal disease is liable
to a term of gaol. This, in the hands of a
conscientious doctor, affords-, a potent weapon
against these a]l-too-frequent cases where
tne health of a young Australian woman
has been ruined irreparably by a man who
on the eve of marriage decides to “risk it.”
despite medical warnings as to the probable
consequences of his action. The Health De-
partment _ distributes a straightforward,
simply-written. pamphlet on venereal dis-
eases, for distribution to sufferers by doctors
and chemists, and through any other avail-
able channel. So far, the notification sections
have been applied to the Brisbane metro-
politan area only, but the evident intention
is to extend them to other principal centres
as soon as the plan has been tried out.
.~he underlying object of the scheme in all
its phases is to attack venereal diseases on
tne lines that have served to control other
communicable diseases. The old Contagious
Diseases Acts mostly attacked a few of the
vic^ims of these diseases, and did
nothing to prevent spread by infected males.
Z J-TA P"Yateer” class of clandestine
piostitute. This was about as sensible as it
would be to quarantine a few of the men in
" small-pox-infected vessel and to let the
women go.
A Farcical Act.
Fbom “10-2-4” :
J. just emerged from a study of the Act under
yKy h K N \ S \ Wales held its last elections. Without
a doubt, it is ripe for the rubbish-tip. The principle
°f_ the second ballot is stupid and pernicious in itself
a remark that was made repeatedly in The Bul-
fnTr’Tl when , , th , e Wade Government first set about
introducing that form of voting to N. S Wales four
years ago. The second ballot provides the same
opportunities for intrigue, bribery and corruption,
and political villainy generally as the Liberal sys-
, *t lect I n £ Pf r ty candidates; and the expres-
on the People s will—in the rare cases where
the People happen to have a will— is liable to be
frustrated by it under innumerable circumstances that
may be readily understood.
But it is when one goes into details that the mani-
fold imbecilities of the Parliamentary Electorates and
Elections Act (1912) —which consolidates and re-
enacts a number of other electoral Acts, amongst
them the Second Ballot Act (1910) —-become appar-
ent. For example, Section 81 says that “no polling-
place shall be created or abolished after the issue
of the writ.’ It is easy to see the point of this
proviso. The inexplicable thing is that, the Act stops
there. Nowhere is it mentioned what will happen
to the candidate or to the election or to the
returning-officer or to the Government if a dozen
new polling-booths are erected and all the old ones
abolished. The candidate is not disqualified; the
election is not voided; no one goes to gaol. The
Act just lays it down firmly that the change mustn’t
occur, and thereafter, falls into a profound silence.
It is as though the criminal law solemnly forbade a
man to commit murder, but imposed no penalty what-
ever for the crime.
Section 143 says that “at the second ballot the
polling-places ’ appointed for the first ballot shall be
used.” There is a splendid vagueness about this
item. Does it mean that only those polling-places
which were used in the particular electorate concerned
shall be used, or that the ones which were open
throughout the whole State during the first ballot
must be re-opened ? It is an interesting point for
the lawyers. To the writer it seems that all the
polling-places everywhere should have been made
available. The authorities decided, however, last
week, and the week before, that only those polling-
booths should be open which were in electorates
where second- ballots were being held. That decision
meant necessarily that thousands of people were dis-
franchised. The fact was established in every one
of the metropolitan constituencies affected on the
Saturday before last, and in an even greater degree
in the country electorates where the vote was taken
last week. In seats like Enmore and Willoughby and
Waverley, where the absentee vote ran in each case
to hundreds on the first ballot, it scarcely amounted
to tens cn the second. There might have been a
dozen St. George electors in Bathurst or Cooma on
the day W. H. Wood received his quietus ; yet none
of them would have been allowed to vote as an
absentee, the local booths being closed. On last
Saturday every country elector who chanced to be in
Sydney for the holidays was debarred from register-
ing his vote for a constituency where a second ballot
was proceeding, an absurd result of the Government’s
interpretation of the law.
Talking of the absentee vote, if any scheme was
ever devised that- is more likely than this to lay
the way open to chicanery and plain swindling, I
should like, as a matter of curiosity, to be intro-
duced to it. There are two sorts of absentees under
the Act. There, is the genuine absentee, meaning the
person who is actually outside his electorate at the
time the .poll* is taking place; and there is
what one may the local absentee. To take the
genuine absenteeTfrrstV h)s hope of recording an
effective vote , two factors—(l) his own
knowledge of the'fine "points of the Act; and (2) the
.intelligence and' honesty of .the presiding officer. The
average elector, absentee or otherwise, knows little of
the Electoral Act;.has but a moderate stock of clever-
ness; and is liable'.totbe afflicted slightly with stage-
fright when he gets inside .the booth. If the presid-
ing officer ; puts his initials, let us say, instead of
signing his name in full-on the absentee’s ballot
papers, they are thereby rendered informal. A dull
or ignorant or casual or nervous voter would be
unlilvely to notice this Igpse.. If he did notice it,
not know that a wrong had been done his
candidate. • Again, an unintelligent presiding officer
might- initial hundreds of absentee votes by reason of
shOer ignorance, and so turn an election. And,
finally, there is the possibility of a strong and un-
scrupulous party man getting control of a booth.
Such a one, assuming he was a Liberal, might be
relied on to deliberately invalidate every vote that
came palpably from the local deviation works ; while
if-he Impugned to.'be a Laborite, he would see to it
that the franchises of the loathly local squatters and
doctors went for naught.
Thp local absentee is -differently situated. It is
_ open to him. to. go into any polling place in his
electorate,' make a declaration before the presiding
officer in the terms of schedule 9, and vote. By
this arrangement William Jones can rise up bright
and early on polling morn, and, having first ascer-
tained the names of a few dozen men who are either
dead or absent from the constituency, vote till he is
weary. lie votes at Blight-street booth as Smith, at
the Methodist chapel as Brown, at the Public School-
house as O’Leary, and at the local Town Hall
as McPherson. So far as the unhappy candidate
whom he is opposing is concerned, the moment these
various votes go into the ballot-box that is an end
of the matter. They have to be counted, the bogus
equally with the genuine. Remains the chance of
the impersonator being discovered and punished.
This is an extremely remote possibility—-more especi-
ally in a crowded city constituency.
A dozen more anomalies might be cited, but the
above should do. The greater part of the Parlia-
mentary Electorates and Elections Act cumbers the
political earth ; and the Second Ballot portion of it
a calamity. It is to be hoped that one
of the Holman Government’s first Parliamentary
moves will be to reform the entire absurdity out of
existence.
Science and Sydney University.
The N. S. Wales Public School Teachers’
Union has issued another manifesto, lament-
ing the undemocratic nature of the Sydney
university, particularly in regard to evening
science lectures. Says the Union :
The petition of 100 students in 1910 and 1911
for the establishment of evening science lectures, was
refused because of “insufficient room’’! The atti-
tude of the University to evening science lectures
is too farcical even for laughter ; it borders on the
tragic. Not even one course in science will the
University add to the annual courses Of evening lec-
tures ! Industrial inefficiency is the rock on which
the chariot of the State will yet come to grief. The
Senate, by its attitude to evening science lectures
for the bright students of the workshop and labora-
tory, the soap works, and the dye works, the mill
and the factory, has shown that it is out- of touch
with the nation, oblivious of economic need, a costly,
inert, inefficient body, controlling an unwieldy,
lethargic, undemocratic University.
But the Union trips over the facts when it
states that “the average number of science
graduates _ turned out of the University
annually is six.” A fair inference from
that is that the Science Faculty is being run
for the benefit of six students in each of
the three years of the science course. As a
matter of fact, there are at present 23
science students doing first-year work, 22
at second-year work and 21 at third-year
work, a total of 67, who are qualifying for the
science degree. In addition, 10 students
are doing research work. There is, indeed,
little room for complaint as to the work of
the Science Faculty as far as day students
are concerned; nevertheless the Union is
entitled to kick against the refusal of the
Senate to provide facilities for evening stu-
dents to take the science degree. For the
Senate, it is argued (1) that evening stu-
dents would not be able to devote the time
to the necessary practical work within the
three years, and 12 months would have to
be added to the period of study; (2) that
additional laboratories, apparatus, lecturers
and demonstrators would be required. To
that the complete reply is that (1) the Uni-
versity exists to meet the convenience of
students, and (2) the more scientific equip-
ment the country calls for the better. At
the same time, the students (who do not
object to a lengthened course) say that
existing conveniences would be sufficient for
a considerable extension of the present
work.

Australian Law and Foreign Interpreta-
tion.
The Privy Council’s decision in the Sugar
Commission case was a paralyser to lawyers
all over Australia. The prevailing idea is
that there has been a reporter's error or a
stupendous cabler’s mistake, or something of
that sort. Also, possibly, there may be a
vast hiatus somewhere, the filling of which
would alter the sense and elucidate much
that is at present a mystery. Amongst a
number of novel propositions, not to say
rank absurdities, is the following:—
The question at the root of the controversy, how-
ever, remained: Were the Commissions Acts intra
vires f The burden of proof rested with those affirm-
ing that the Acts were within the Commonwealth
Parliament’s powers.
This is about the first time in history that
any Court or other authority has seriously
laid down the principle that the burden is on
the litigant to show that the measure on
which he is relying is intra vires. Hitherto
it has been taken for granted by all parties
that any given Act is valid till it has been
shown to be otherwise. Last week’s de-
cision, if it has been correctly reported,
upends this, and so gives rise to a
weird situation. In future, in any
litigation under Commonwealth law, the
case proper will be liable to be preceded by
elaborate argument as to whether the Act is
good or otherwise. The proceedings will
then necessarily be held up till this question
has been settled; and, incidentally, the
business of the Court will be delayed. As a
sort of side line, such a case, being a Con-
stitutional one, will require to be argued
before a full bench of judges; and whichever
way the decision goes, the problem may be
sent on to the Privy Council for final deter-
mination. A more admirable means of pro-
longing simple disputes it would be hard to
conceive. With skilful handling the pal-
triest of legal rows should now be capable of
being dragged out for years, to the ruin of
all hands concerned —barring, of course, the
members of the legal profession. What
must strike the ordinary citizen as utterly
ridiculous and illogical is that the ultimate
word on any Australian Constitutional issue
should be spoken in London. The members
of the Privy Council themselves recognised
the anomaly, by the look of the cable. They
“hesitated to differ from the High Court
Judges, particularly the Chief Justice, Sir
Samuel Griffith, and Mr. Justice Bar-
ton, who had special knowledge of the Aus-
tralian Constitution.” To send an Australian
Constitutional case away from a Bench whose
occupants are steeped in the local Constitu-
tion, from the fact that most of them helped
to frame it, and to lay it before a British
Bench which is wholly unused to interpret-
ing a Constitution, and which in any case
has never taken much interest in the Aus-
tralian instrument, is rather like importing
a Frenchman to referee a game of football
under Australian rules. The procedure, in
brief, is absurd.
A Conspiracy of Silence.
Within the last few days some well-mean-
ing people have plastered the hoardings of
Sydney with warnings on the subject of the
White Slave trade. The betting is all
George-street to a fragment of blue metal
that no such trade exists anywhere in Aus-
tralia. Not’ a solitary white slave has been
rescued from her dreadful lot by those whose
business it is to hunt out such unfortunates.
There is no recorded case of a White Slaver
having been captured and gaoled. The trade,
if it prevails even in the most insignificant
form imaginable, must be a perfect miracle
of successful furtiveness. But as to the
existence of what we may term the syphilis
trade there js not the smallest shadow of a
doubt. It is not even a “hidden” plague,
as apologists for a policy of silence on the
subject often allege. Its presence is made
evident on all sides by the agency of unhappy
beings with disfigured faces, loathsome skin
diseases, shattered nerves and distorted
bodies. It is commoner than cancer, or
tynhoid, or diphtheria, or even phthisis, and
more agonising in its ultimate effects than
any of them. The sufferer from (say)
small-pox has at least the consolation of
knowing that if he manages to defeat the
disease there is an end of the matter. The
syphilis patient is never sure that the lives
of those nearest and dearest to him may not
be destroyed as the outcome of his misfor-
tune. That is one of the reasons why it is
ten thousand times more important in a
national sense that the city hoardings should
be placarded with information about syphilis
than that they should supply the derisive
passer-by with futile tips on the White Slave
problem. Unfortunately the time is not ripe
for a startling move of this sort, by the
present look of things; which makes it all
the more urgent that the newspapers should
do their duty in the matter. They can hurry
on a reform, which must in any case arrive
fairly soon now, by calling tirelessly on the
Government of the day for preventive mea-
sures against what they are wont to*
call the red plague, as drastic as those that
are in vogue with respect to such compara-
tively trifling ailments as diphtheria and
typhoid ; and they may save a lot of misery
by using the same plain language on the
syphilis question as they used last month
and the month before concerning small-pox.
To bring the beginning of the anti-syphilis
crusade a month or even a week nearer
would be to do an incalculable service to>
humanity. It can only be done by ending
what bydney Evening News the other day
rightly called “the conspiracy of silence.’”
Ibis should be easy to the most conservative
ot sheets, since shamefacedness on this topic-
hasn t even the merit of being the journalistic-
fashion any longer. The London Times:
and Daily Mail have taken to dis-
cussing the syphilis horror quite openly -
the English Review and the New Review
have published recently the most plain-spoken
remarks conceivable on the same question
and the other day the Melbourne Age took
uo the running. It is not too much to say
that on the morning when the last stalwart
of newspaper conservatism prints for the ulti-
mate time the phrase “a certain disease,”
the main barrier against the assault upon
syphilis will go down.
♦
Briee though its existence was, Victoria’s
first Labor Government did one good thing
when Chief-Secretary Prendergast ordered
the police to desist from that refuge of tired
or incompetent officers, the “third degree”
examination, which consists in pumping a
suspected person until he or she is so ex-
hausted as not to know what is being said,
and then committing an alleged statement to
paper and getting the victim to sign it. In
defending their resort to the practice, the
police instanced the case of the women who
recently defrauded the Commonwealth by
forging baby-bonus claims. The detectives
admit that they had the two accused under
“a vigorous examination” from 9 a.m. on
one day to 4.30 a.m. the next, and that it
was only the information thus obtained that
ensured their conviction. In other words,
being too lazy or incompetent to sheet the
offence home to the accused, the police
adopted a species of torture until the accused
were driven to sheet the offence home to
themselves. In the open an accused is
warned that he is not bound to say anything
to commit himself; in the seclusion of the
police office he is badgered and bullied until
he does say something to commit himself.
The system is villainously unfair, and nobody
but a weary Murray would have put up with
its gradual extension during the Jast few
years.
One night last week, in Sydney, the impos-
ing Canada Building burst into flame, and
within half an hour it was a raging volcano,
belching smoke and flame. The building
was quite new, and might reasonably have
been suspected of being fire : proof. But it
wasn’t, and in three hours it was reduced
to a tottering ruin. Happily there was no
loss of life,, though the caretaker had to
drive an electric lift through the floor that
was on fire, and escape with his family by
the skin of the teeth. Had the fire been on
the ground floor he would have dropped into
an oven. Fortunately, it was not on that floor.
And fortunately, also, the blaze took place by
night: had it been by day, when every floor
would have been more or less thronged with
people, the chances are there would have
been scores of charred human remains
among the ruins. For the Sydney citizen
and the country visitor the point of import-
ance is that this modern building, .though
perhaps as good as most, was not as fire-
proof as human ingenuity could make it.
What the fireproof building should be is
for the experts to say; and the sooner they
are called in to decide, and the sooner the
authorities compel adherence to the stan-
dard laid down by them, the less risk we
will run of being grilled citizens. It is
understood to be most unpleasant to be a
grilled citizen. ‘ • >

The N. S. Wales Government has won a
great victory over an aged public servant,
who for 25 years drove a pump. This officer
was appointed by the Governor-in-Council
under the Sydney Abattoirs Act of 1877. In
1895 the Public Service Board came into
power ; in 1896 it graded all the public ser-
vants, wrote their names in a book, and
published it as a record. The steam pumper,
after noting that his name was in the list,
went on pumping for eight years, when the
age limit was reached. Thereabouts he re-
ceived a letter from the Board of Health dis-
pensing with his services. He argued that
he was “retired” under the P.S. Act, and
therefore entitled to a gratuity. The Govern-
ment retorted that he was dispensed with by
the Board of Health, and that the Board of
Health was not liable to pay gratuities to
old servants. After a weary course of liti-
gation the High Court has decided against
the venerable on the ground that he was
not and never had been a servant of the
Public Service Board, the fact that the
Board had said he was being an unfortunate
mistake. In entering judgment the High
Court refused, to make an order for costs.
Thus, while the aged pump expert does not
get his gratuity, the State pays the money
just the same, but to the lawyer. In other
words, Ijaw is nourished even though Justice
starves.
Willy Watt has two ex-Premiers in his
new Victorian Ministry—Jack Murray and
Alec Peacock. Murray takes his old Chief
Secretary’s job, in which he could do good
work if he were only half as energetic as he
used to be; Peacock takes the Education
office, which he first managed 21 years ago.
Shiels put him there in the first place be-
cause he had started working life as a public-
school teacher. Billson having been dropped,
Drysdale Brown is made Mines Minister;
and it will be a relief, anyway, to get him
out of the Law office. Donald Mackinnon
goes there. “Big Bill” Baillieu, who is too
busy money-making to take a portfolio,
nevertheless “leads” for the new crowd in the
Council,
The personnel of N. S. Wales Assembly is
rapidly changing. Of the 90 members re-
turned in October, 1910, only 64 are in the
newly-elected House. Of the 90 who lined
up in 1904 (less than 10 years ago) 58 have
faded from the sound of the division bells.
At this rate Ma will have a brand-new As-
sembly in about six years’ time. It’s slow,
but apparently reliable.
Burns Philp’s guide, “Picturesque Travel”
for 1914, contains the usual fund of general
information for the globe-trotter, as well as
photos and letterpress for the traveller who
confines his to the morning and even-
ing ferry trips. And not the least satisfac-
tory thing about it is that the book is a
wholly Australian production.
The famous portrait of Mona Lisa, stolen
from the Paris Louvre, has been recovered ;
and the Italian workman who did the steal-
ing seems to have got mixed in his explana-
tions. Firstly, he said that he took the
painting in revenge for Napoleon’s looting of
Italian masterpieces. Next day he pro-
fessed to have done it with the idea of get-
ting enough money to provide a modest
competency for his aged parents. In cither
case he was a fool for his pains. For two
years he kept the stolen treasure in conceal-
ment, and as soon as he offered it for sale he
was of course arrested. No work of art has
any considerable value outside of art-loving
circles, and any sort of connoisseur was
bound to know that Leonardo da Vinci’s
famous picture had been stolen.
The new size and style of The Lone Hand
Magazine has proved very popular. Though
several thousand extra copies of the first
number were printed, the issue was sold
right out.
“Bills”: N.S.W. Full Court has upset
with Costs a decision of Judge Murray that
income earned in N. S. Wales as dividends on
shares in a co. registered in London is liable
to income tax in the Ma State. The co.’s retail
operations are confined to Sydney. The
head office is nominally in London, but four
of the directors, including the managing
director, reside in Sydney. The dividend is
ascertained in Sydney and paid in Sydney
to 200 N.S.W. shareholders; nominally, it
is declared in London, where it is paid to
600 English shareholders. The Full Court
has decided that as the co. is registered in
London, income paid in Sydney out of
profits made in Sydney from goods sold in
Sydney, is not taxable in the State. It is
good law perhaps, but hardly satisfactory
from N.S.W.’s point of view. The co. doesn’t
look to the London policemen to protect their
goods or to the London fire brigades to save
their premises.

Australia saw its first Revue at Her
Majesty’s, Sydney, on Saturday night. As a
spectacle the new show is exquisite; it is
the biggest, the most picturesque, and pro-
bably the most costly thing on pantomime
lines that has been done in Australia. The
two chief sensations —a race between a
motor-car and a train, and the apparent
drowning of a herd of show girls at Manly—
are highlv ingenious, though they are not in
the least convincing. The furious motor-
car emerges in a right cautious fashion
from the heart of the stage darkness ; and the
train does not suggest by its conduct the
wild onrush of so much as a suburban steam
tram. But enough of carping. The people
who are allured by this, the penny-dreadful-
ism of the stage, get their pleasure along
the same lines as Dr. Johnson’s friends
who watched the performing dogs or fleas
or whatever the creatures were: they marvel
not at the) skill with which the deeds are
done, but at the fact that they should be
done at all. The charm of “Come Over
Here” does not lie in its sensations, any-
how, but in its scenic effects ; in its ballets
with their gorgeous color-schemes and their
perfectly-trained regiments of skilled
dancers ; in its queer, disturbing atmosphere
of abandonment and unexpectedness, due
possibly to the lawless way in which the
performers keep arising from the midst of
the audience ; and in its charmingly dressed
—or undressed, as the case may be —show
girls. By the way, the Revue habit of bring-
ing the show girls away from the footlights’
deceptive glare, and landing them practic-
ally on top of their male worshippers in the
stalls, is likely to have a powerful influence
for moral good. Some of the really beautiful
ones—Nance Anderson, Tina Breon, and so
on —contrive to continue to be beautiful de-
suite their nearness to the audience and the
“COME OVER HERE," AT HER
MAJESTY'S, SYDNEY.
Not Futurist statuary—merely Jack Cannot and
Johnny Osborne as “ The Dying Gladiators."
abysmal depth of the make-up which is
visible on their faces. But a deplorably large
number of others, who used to look absolute
gems while supporting a property pillar in
the mellow middle distance of a ballroom
or Royal Palace scene, turn out on close
scrutiny to be as plain as habitual chapel-
goers. It is sheeted home mercilessly to the
stunned bald-head that the average chorus-
girl 'has weight, et prceterca nihil. And so
is ’-et another of life’s fair illusions blasted.
Where “Come Over Here” fails mis-
erably is in its fugitive and halting
efforts to review current happenings in a
lightsome spirit. An Attic audience of 2000
years ago, or more, would unhesitatingly
have stoned those responsible for such
banalities, while an educated Parisian crowd
would be liable to register its disapproval
of the whole fatuous business by tearing the
theatre to pieces and burning the remains.
The gags are not even up to normal panto-
mime form. Parliamentary satire is repre-
sented by a pointless shouting match be-
tween C. G. Wade —a depressed character
who has never been known to do anything
more politically uproarious than moan about
his wrongs and hint darkly at the moral in-
famy of the enemy—and mild James
McGowen, who never shrieked at anyone,
even in the long dead past, when he was
Premier, and who i& gentler than ever
now that he is a mere subordinate.
Social comment takes The form chiefly of a
grotesque presentment of George Reid. If
Reiu destroys his pantomime double dur-
ing the week he should be able to plead
the unwritten law with some success. Are
these the sort of imbecilities that the Aus-
tralian public really wants? Or is it the
managerial theory that the national infcllect
has become atrophied as a result of 20
years of musical comedy?

artist though he is, makes heavy weather of
it in a part that should suit him down to
the ground—or he was doing so when this
paper saw him. Probably, by now-, however,
he is. cheering things up w-ith all sorts of
amusing and demented business. He has a
song, idiotic as to its words, but w-ith a

with her face than, she does with her voice.
Maiden shyness of the early-Victorian brand,

ment, are her strong suits. She has some
