hack-parlor morsels that are suited to her
temperament. Charlie Stone and Leslie
Holland are as The Bulletin has known
them for years past, a wary, non-committal
comment that may also be applied to Ed:
mond Sherras, Louis Victor, Claude Bantock
and others. Ivy Schilling and Fred Leslie
supply a superb dance in the second act —
perhaps the most beautiful feature of a pro-
duction that is full of scenic beauties. And
finally, Miss Collison and Miss Winnie Volt
are charming. “Come Over Here” has no
plot; and at the end of it the ordinary
human being will be delighted, never to hear
a ragtime melody again.
Julius Knight has come back to the Syd-
ney that loves him; and on Saturday, in
Shaw’s “Man and Superman,” he offended
all the people who read playa by wearing his
own face instead of the whiskers that are
prescribed by the author. From a strictly
Julius Knight point of view, he was right.
Julius might easily be ruined by a set of
Dundrearys, while “a full set” (to quote
Frank Richardson) might obliterate him for
ever. Furthermore, Shaw’s third act was
dropped, leaving a blank space and a dis-
location M the later remarks ; but the only
really regrettable effect was the consequent
absence from the play of the low-comedy
brigand who inhabits the Sierra Nevada
scenery thereabouts. Julius Knight, as
Tanner, gets inside the character of the ver-
bose Socialist, who, when he has almost
finished talking, subsides to the blandish-
ments of the pursuing Ann Whitefield. Ann,
by the way, is now accepted quite placidly
by a world which a few years ago would
have rejected her angrily as a monstrosity
and a libel on the sex. Up to the time of
Ann (and Shaw), Woman was supposed to
be the timid quarry fleeina. from the hunter
Man. Irene Brown, as Ann, cat-sprints after
the talking Tanner in a natural manner, and
curls up gracefully in the last scene when
she has got him squeaking under her velvet
paws. It is a graceful performance through-
out. Greenaway, as the Gdhkney chauffeur,
gets in some strong character work' T«,r

A travelling critic not long ago said of the
Australian that he had no appreciation nf
humor, but that critic had never been in
theatre when a U.S. comedian was kickin!
another humorist from the greatest country
in the Yewmverse on his pneumatic stomach
to the accompaniment of a bass drum “A
Day at the Races” is of the same type as
‘‘The Grafters,” which was strong on the
pneumatic stomach. But it has many like
abilities. Bert Le Blanc, one of the most
energetic and popular comedians that the
Palace has sheltered, received a welcome on
Saturday night that would justify him in
hinting to Bilanderson something about the
increased cost- of living. Encores of the
songs, dances, choruses ami ballets were the
last and loudest proof of the appreciation
of the packed house, and in face of that
there is nothing to be said. It appears likely
that the town will take to “A Day at the
Races” as it did to “The Grafters, and if
that is so even offers of free seats at the
Linnean Society’s lectures won’t stop the
stampede.
Clara Butt and her Kennerley Rumford
came back to Sydney Town Hall on Satur-
day night for the first of three positively
final farewell concerts. Except that Mr.
Rumford’s bracketed trio of Brahms “Seri-
ous Songs” were a trifle too heavy for him,
the programme was a most admirable one’
and the artist in every case was well up to
concert pitch. Murdoch and Mowat Carter
supplied the instrumental numbers, and
Mowat Carter earned a special posy for him-
self.
Willoughby’s dramatic co., at the Adelphi
Theatre, closed its season there on Satur-
day, and the place was shut until Wednes-
day night, so that the panto. “Aladdin”
could be given the finishing touches. Tiie
Bulletin is unable to make a few observa-
tions on the show this week, owing to ina-
bility to alter (1) the calendar, (2) the
opening date of “Aladdin,” (3) the day of
publication.
“Officer 666,” who is alleged to be “a
scream” will arrive from the United States,
handcuffed to Fred Niblo and Miss Josephine
Cohan, on Boxing Day. His first public
appearance will be at a Criterion matinee.
For the indigestible year-end the Tivoli
(Sydney) has decorated its bill with Joseph-
ine Davis, a young American entertainer
whose press notices from the big cities of
the Old World are hereby endorsed. Before
her arrival it was stated as an inducement
for general interest that her work was
“something like Nella Webb’s” ; but there is
a good deal of difference. Josephine Davis
has an artistic method of delivering her
songs and illustrating them with touches of
characterisation that Nella Webb knew not.
In case anybody reckons Josephine isn’t
enough for the money, it may be hurriedly
explained that Hawley and co. (with a fine
sketch, “The Bandit”), Du Calion (the
nerve thrilling ladder balancer), the Pas-
quils, Barton and Ashley and half a dozen
other notables in their business are filling in
the blanks.
Like other Sydney shows the National
Amphitheatre is running an unusually big
programme for Christmas and New Year.
Apart from mere human amusement-makers,
the services of a party of gentlemanly
roosters have been accepted, as well as those
of a herd of bears and dogs. Manager
Fuller knows more about performing pigs,
cats (quadrupeds and others), monkeys,
donkeys, fowls and miscellaneous animals than
any show-man in the city, and he says the
roosters at present throwing handsprings at
the Amphi. are the best to date. No extra
charge will be made at the doors to those
desirous of verifying the statement.
Special programmes are marking the fowl
season at Sydney picture houses. At the
Crystal Palace the big feature is a Kalem
film entitled “The Man Who Vanished,” a
dramatic story of the troubles that beset an
heir to a fortune before he could finger the
gilt. The Lyric stars a drama of love ana
marriage, and the desire for forbidden
apples; the Colonial, a story of revenge
from South America, where the savage peons
hang out. At Spencer’s, on Boxing Night,
an adaptation of Dickens’s “Christmas
Carol” will be shown, with Seymour Hicks
as Ebenezer Scrooge. The Glaciarium is
housing the picture that set London talking,
“The Miracle”; the Olympia has “The Mas-

The \\ hite City is still at the old address
in Rushcutter’s Bay (Sydney), and every-
body is welcome. The management desires
it to be known that any employee refusing
admittance to sober, respectable citizens will
be promptly discharged. The mere sordid
money to be coughed up at the gate and
side shows is mentioned incidentally in the
advertisement.
Melbourne s panto, this year is somewhat
less bulky and more patchy than usual, whilst
the general effect also is somewhat different.
Although the disembowelled version of “The
Forty Thieves” ends as vaguely as it begins,
there is at least one brief period when the
performance appeals to the imagination. In
the cave of those fair, slim and 40 ladylike
criminals, the dramatic action, poetic dan-
cing, and brilliant final tableau are true to
the spirit of the old story. The forcible
actirnr of Atholwood in this scene explains
how he came to get into such strange com-
pany. On the other hand, some of the other
members fail to account for their engage-
ment. Maude Terry, as Morgiana, is con-
spicuously out of place, and a prettily-
trimmed ballad falls flat from her lips. Nor
is Jessie Lonnen a sufficiently strong Ganem
to carry so weak a Morgiana through their
love duets. Began and Ryan are a painful
pair of alleged comedians, but the conven-
tional American fooling of the Three Rubes
is good of its kind. The Rubes are an inter-
polated item. Miss Dolly Manner's loud
drollery is emphasised by the comparative
gentleness of Impino and Brett, as Mr. and
Mrs. Ali Baba. Edwin Brett, stouter than
when he was here last, is a genial, fussy
female who errs on the side of restraint.
Lupino is an accomplished pantomime artist,
fairly humorous and wonderfully agile. His
principal comedy scene with Brett is suc-
culent, and his leg work is a feature of the
show.. He can.spin and leap as gracefully as
Volinin, or tumble as adroitly as a vaude-
ville knockabout. The star song of the
panto, is warbled by Marie Eaton as Abdal-
lah, assisted by a boy soprano and a chime
of electric bells in the auditorium. This
happens near closing time, when the Captain
of the Forty Thieves comes to sing a ballad
concerning the joys of Loveland, with rings
in its refrain and bells on its symphony. The
panto, may be said to peter out then. The
tinkle of the bells is followed by a terrible
beating of drums in the closing scene, which
bristles with flags and noble national senti-
ment. Earlier in the evening the Australian
Navy is a matter for vociferous song and
chorus. But, then, a panto, is nothing if
not patriotic.
** * *
“The Land of Nod,” produced on dis-
tinctly American lines at the King’s last
Saturday, promises to go better than any of
Anderson's previous experiments in panto, or
musical extravaganza. The Yankee novelty
is in a class by itself. As far as it attempts
to be a “phantasy” it is crudely suggestive
of “The Blue Bird,” and as far as it re-
sembles a panto, it is a glorified perversion
of “The Grafters.” The principal girl is
Bonnie, who hastily falls asleep in the open-
ing front scene and dreams all the ragtime
incidents that eventuate in the Land of Nod,
where the King of Hearts lives in a house
of cards, and subsequently in the Nightmare
Palace, where Sandman, the vociferous vil-
lain of the vaudeville, is treated with con-
tempt by all who wander into his clutches.
The principal boy is Jack of Hearts; the
Man in the Moon is the henpecked husband
of Rory Bory Alice; Welsh Rarebit is cast
into the Peppermint River by the basso
Weather Man and rescued by April Fool —
or hollow jests to that effect. Phantasy
runs in double harness with Inanity, and
Irrelevancv holds the reins. In the course
of this crazy entertainment there are 25
turns for- the principals, who are rarely un-
assisted by the chorus when the orchestra
(20 in number) }s at work.
* * r * *
Fourteen characters are billed, and 10 or
a dozen fresh faces from America appear in
the cast. Tom Cannam, Priscilla Verne
(looking only a few years younger), and
Maud Chetwynd are the well-known mem-
bers of the cast. ■ Generally speaking, the co.
is more than strong enough to do justice to
the poor little libretto —which is weakest
in its silly local allusions —and the various
ragtime features. There are pleasing
voices enough and to spare. Ruth Nevins,
as Jack of Hearts, lets her high, in-
cisive soprano go most effectively in sundry
solo numbers and ensembles. The Bonnie of
*\nna McNab is a melodious girl with ginger
in her comedy; also some of the men are
respectable vocalists and fairly imposing
persons. Apart from the performance on the
stage, there are outbreaks of song in the
stage boxes, in one of which an impersonator
of George Reid rubs shoulders with George
V. Chorus girls pop up unexpectedly in the
auditorium, and such cheap and easy Ameri-
can trick effects seem calculated to advertise
“The Land of Nod.” It is a liberally-staged
show. The scenery is bright, some of the
electric lighting devices are very ingenious,
and many pretty girls are decoratively
clothed.
A revival of “Monsieur Beaucaire” is to
round off the Lewis Waller season at Mel-
bourne Royal. On Saturday (27th) Waller
will appear in the ornate garments of the
gaudy character which he “created”—in an
actorial way of speaking—long years ago.
It was. as a matter of fact, the author of the
yarn who created the character, and Waller
was the original interpreter thereof; but the
point is not worth worrying about. Melbourne
will be glad to meet a new Beaucaire and a
fresh young Lady Mary, and the devotees
of romantic comedy are already sniffing in
advance “the fragrance of a bunch of old
roses” (vide advt.). Meanwhile Waller con-
tinues to be taken prisoner by Miss Lily
ritheradge, whose name is Miss Elizabeth
for the nonce. The present double bill holds
good until Boxing Night.

. Ihe Christmas outbreak at the Princess’s
is another amended version of poor old
“Uncle Tom’s Cabin.” The bathetic bellow-
drama shed its last vestige of live human in-
terest long before The Bulletin was born,
and even its American color is fading away.
Little Eva has evoluted into the Gibson
Gut. Great grandsons of the niggahs whom
Simon Legree walloped for wallop’s sake
are coomng at music-halls, or stoushing
white hopes” for large sums of gate-money.
Uncle Tom is dead and massa Jack Johnson
guffaws in his stead. In the present up-to-
date production of Uncle, which makes one
almost weep over the abolition of slavery,
the Willoughby management’s specialities
are the Era Comedy Four, the New York
Jubilee Singers and 12 Piccaninny Rag-
timers. Thick slices of vaudeville flavored
with the comforts of religion are sandwiched
between thin layers of familiar narrative;
and persons of all ages and every class are
cordiallv invited to the picnic.
The much-needed new attractions at the
Opera House are the Schmettans and
the Clemendos, who are described respectively
as-famous athletes and renowned acrobats,
and act accordingly. Also the five Petlevs,
in the same line of business, have just
arrived from Sydney. All these hard-working
people _ clashed at their outset with Gene
Greene’s deplorable ragtime contests, but the
amateur competitions were to close on Tues-
day night, and The Bulletin trusts the
advt. kept its promise. Greene himself will
go on singing as before.
The Christmas dish at the Auditorium will
be the musical Kennedys, who start their
little season on Boxing Night. At the Town
Hall the time honored revival of “The Mes-
siah” by the Philharmonic Society comes to
pass on Christmas Night. This year the
Phil, celebrated its diamond jubilee by put-
ting in a previous “Messiah” performance
last Saturday.
Zola’s “Germinal” has concluded its brief
but prosperous stav at ! West’s Palace, Mel-
bourne. On Boxing Night West will unwind
“In the Bishop’s Carriage,” the pictorial ver-
sion of the drama wherein Nell Stewart
figured of old.
Bill Plain, Minister for Lands in Elmslie’s
\ictorian ex-Cabinet, is one of the very manv
real farmers who are not frightened of the
Labor party’s policy. With an amazing
amount of information about the land and
the way to use it, Plain would have been
Minister of Lands and Agriculture long ago
if there were elective Ministries in Victoria.
But the absurd party system decreed that
he must stand aside directly Elmslie was
beaten on some matter with which Plain
Bill’s department had nothing whatever to
do. Before William came to Australia he
was a “hand” on ex-Governor Thomas Gib-
son-Carmichael’s estate in Scotland.
Collins’s Diaries for 1914 are now avail-
able. Every year the series has grown
larger, until now it seems as if there isn’t
any class of diarist whose special require-
ments are not catered for.
The recent attempt to assassinate Lionel
Phillips, the S’African millionaire, revives a
story of the Jameson Raid period, when
Phillips, being arrested as a boss raider,
seemed likely to be hanged by the Kruger
Government. The devoted little wife of
Phillips (she is now dying of sleeping sick-
ness, it is said) was in London at the time
of the Raid fiasco, and she made frantic
efforts to interview Prime Minister Balfour
on hubby’s behalf. Cable messages in the
London papers announced the date fixed for
the execution of Jameson, Phillips and Co.,
but still the weary magnates at Downing-
street seemed indifferent as to Kruger’s in-
tentions. Finally Mrs. Phillips got hold of
Miss Balfour, and tried to get her interested
in the of the “doomed” filibusters. Miss
Balfour protested that she saw no need for
this urgent pleading. Why worry? The
agitated wife replied that the execution
would come due on the following Monday.
The date had been published in the papers,
she said. Miss Balfour met her tearful gaze
with an icy, high-bred stare. “We nevah
read the papahs,” she said.

of chilly snakes sprawling around her shoulders.

“Constant Fire-Bug”: A big city fire is the best
draw in the world, bar none. 1 was at a Mel-
bourne theatre one night when the whisper floated
through the crowded auditorium that there was a
gorgeous blaze just up the street. It was a gay
show, and everyone had been absorbed in it up to
that moment; but, at the first hint of the free blaze
outside, the audience stirred like a field of standing
wheat and started to melt out. I was in the first
wave ; but even then I could see the poor stage-show
going flat and dying on its various and beautiful
legs. It had encountered the one irresistible rival.
The other night, in Sydney, hundreds of people let
their last trains go, so they could gloat over the
seven-storey pillar of fire that used to be the Canada
Building. A few weeks ago, when Ambryn Island, in
the New Hebrides, started to boil over, and buck,
and shake the seas, the sightseers from Vila hurried
along to watch furious Nature remoulding things. I
know of only one person who ever voluntarily left a
big fire before it had lost its full golden strength.
He was a small boy with an abnormal sense of duty,
and he turned his small back on one of Melbourne’s
record conflagrations to go and buy a pair of cheap,
insignificant boots in collaboration with his maiden
aunt. I was the other gloating small boy, and I very
nearly throttled him for it. Why, when Rome
burned, Nero merely fiddled —he didn’t degrade him-
self by buying boots.
“J.F.H.” (from London) : Lillah McCarthy ought
to be a good judge of a play by this time; but she
isn’t, or she wouldn’t have put on at London St.
James’s such a hopeless and repellent anachronism
as “The Witch”—a prolonged attempt to make the
flesh creep. The play is a weird story of sixteenth-
century Norway; and not a gleam of light intrudes
upon its murk. Lillah made a thrillsome creature
of the witch-wife, and Australia’s old acquaintance,
Janet Achurch. was a grandmother who did some
terrible cursing. But though they did well enough,
it was all time sadly misspent.
“Harrison O.”: One advantage a repertory theatre
such as McMahon’s in Melbourne has over the com-
mercial institution lies in the much larger number
of performers it has to choose from when it comes to
casting a play. In practically every show given by
McMahon’s amateurs there lias been at least one
strikingly convincing character; not because of any
special histrionic ability on the part of its exponent,
but because the said exponent has borne a very strong
resemblance, physically and in other: ways, to the
person he has been supposed to portray. The pro-
fessional stage introduces us to the better actor, but
the repertory theatre gives us the more life-like figure.
Premier Jack Seaddan (W.A.) recently
shocked his followers and gave Liberals
cause for a joyful grin by threatening to
thrbw up his State job and have a run for
the Federal seat vacant through C. E.
Fraser’s death. Probably, if he felt sure of
success at the Labor selection ballot, he
would have carried out his threat. Seaddan
is just worried to his wits’ ends through the
utter hopelessness of making ends meet even
with the utmost economy ; and instead of an
improvement, about all he can see ahead of
him is a bigger bill for wages and supplies.
It is only about a year since the Common-
wealth was definitely committed to the 4ft.
S|in. railway gauge, with a view to
accelerating the establishment of a stan-
dard throughout the continent. The
diversity of gauge is one of the great
military dangers of Australia, and it is only
in keeping with the general attitude of the
Cook Government that Glynn has decided
that the Pine Creek to Katherine River line
shall be built to 3ft. 6in. gauge. Glynn’s
argument is that the Port Darwin to Pine
Creek section is 3ft. 6in. But that is no
argument in favor of building the extension
to the same size, though it is an overwhelming
plea for the immediate reconstruction of the
existing line. Also, it drives home the
criminal folly of constructing the Northern
Transcontinental line from the North at
all. Glyn’s idea seems to be that it is
better and cheaper to alter a whole line in
the future than rebuild now a small piece of
it at each end.
The Bedford Estate, the remnant of which
has just been sold in London, is an awful
instance of the appropriation of unearned
increment. The founder bought 19 acres
in what was soon to become the heart of
London, at a time when its annual rental
value was £129. lie stuck to his property,
and under the law of entail it passed from
heir to heir, whilst circumstances over which
they had no control raised the market value
to the Lord knows how much. The new
purchasers are supposed to have paid about
half as many million pounds as the original
Bedford paid hundreds for it. One could
go the rounds of Covent Garden market,
stroll into three great theatres in neighbor-
ing streets, feed at the Waldorf Hotel, see a
fight at the National Sporting Club and be
committed for trial at Bow-street Police
Court without going beyond the confines of
the estate. Big, open spaces were closed by
order of the Duke, who was the most auto-
cratic landlord in London —doubtless be-
cause his ancestors had been built that way.
The public created the value, and the land-
lord grabbed it for his own ; and the appal-
ling injustice of it pointed 10,000 arguments
in favor of the public ownership of all land.
The N.S.W. Public Service is perturbed
by the verdict of the High Court in the case
Stephen v. Miller. Plaintiff, one of the
Stephens, was in the Audit Department, which
he had served faithfully for years. Reaching
60 he was asked if he wanted to stay on, and
he said he did. The Auditor-General, how-
ever, recommended to the Public Service
Board that he be retired. The Board then
recommended to the Governor-in-Council
that Stephen be retired, and retired he was.
He then applied for the gratuity allowed to
public servants whose services are dispensed
with by the Board otherwise than for an
offence. The Government took the point
that Stephen’s services hadn’t been dispensed
with —he had merely been called upon to
retire. The Full Court pondered on this in-
genuous remark for a long time, and unani-
mously decided that his services had been
“dispensed with.” The High Court, with
equal unanimity, now decides that he was
“called upon to retire.” This play upon
words means a loss of £179 to Stephen—
which is liable to be serious thing to a
man over 60. It is equally serious to the
whole crowd of ex-contributors who put up
some of the money to fight Stephen’s case.
The mania of the “Crown” for fighting the
aged public servant over obscure law points,
which its own Judges aren’t sure about, is a
strong argument for a new Superannuation
Act, which will wipe away the legal intrica-
cies and pitfalls of the present Public Ser-
vice Act and place all public servants in a
position of some finality. Just now they
don’t know where they are.
THE PAY’S THE THING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“He draws a princely salary; by far the biggest
sum we have ever paid to any artist under engage-
ment to us in Australia.”
—Too frequent theatrical gag.
The crowd within the playhouse walls
Was simply rocked with pure delight;
Its frenzied clappings and recalls
Disturbed the drowsy Summer night.
“Bravo!” it cried, “Hooray! Angkore!”
The smiling singer gave it more.
I turned upon my neighbor, who
Had stamped the soles from off his boots.
“What is this wicked thing you do?”
I asked of him. “Where are the hoots,
And where the hisses, fierce and free,
This wretch deserves of you and me?
“He cannot jest, he cannot sing;
He couldn’t dance for nuts. It’s clear
The beggar can’t do anything,
And half the vagrant Pointers here
For something more than half a jim
Would act the bloated head off him.”
Said Smith: “You’re right; he isn’t great—
Such voices kick round anywhere.
And—yes-—his actin’s second rate.
But p’r aps you ain’t considered, sir”—
And here he threw in more applause—
“ The splendid salary he draws?”

F. W. Lundie, a city councillor of Ade-
laide and secretary f the A.W.U., has sue-
cessfully defended an action (damages
claimed, £300) for calling a prominent Port
Adelaide Labor man a “scab.” The defence
was quite simple: (1) Plaintiff had shorn
at a shed in 1906 for less than the ruling
rate: (2) Lundie had mentioned the fact,
but had never used the word “scab” at all.
So the expensive bunch of lawyers engaged
had to disperse without settling the really
interesting questions—(la) What is it worth
to call a man a “scab” when he is or has
been one? (2a) When he isn’t and hasn’t
been one?
“Snell” : There are a few little things the
matter w r itii the outbreak of “Clav Face”
(B. 18/12/T3) concerning the Australian
memorial portraits, and Andy Fisher, and
Hugh Patterson and some others. “Clay
Face” is all right in barracking for the Aus-
tralian artist, but he is all wrong in his
other emotions. Meldrum has not had three
commissions. He is now engaged on his
second. Phillips Fox’s portrait of Fisher
was sent back for a few unimportant modifi-
cations, with the making of which the artist
was in perfect agreement. Ambrose Patter-
son has not been “turned down”; he has
painted an excellent portrait of Charley
Kingston to order, and has been paid for it,
and the portrait is now suspended in Queen’s
Hall in full view of “Clay Face” and other
curious investigators. Finally, to leave “Clay
Face” nothing on earth to complain of, let
me point out that Meldrum is an Australian
artist, too, despite a “Glaskee” accent. He
is an old student from Melbourne Art Gal-
lery, and when he first came to this country
was so small a Scotch that he scarcely
amounted to a nip.
“Circle”: The row over the “third degree”
methods of the Victorian police reminds me
of the story told me by the sergeant’s son.
His dad, who was in charge of a Melbourne
suburb, had been worried by a series of
clever fowl thefts. He and his men were
certain that a young vegetable John was
responsible; but all the traps they set failed
to catch him. However, one frosty morn-
ing, John was discovered dozing on a park
bench with a suspicious-looking sack by his
side. The hounds of the Law were stealing to-
wards him, when he jumped up like a rabbit,
threw away his bag, and raced through the
trees. . But the policemen were just a bit
too fast for him, and presently he was being
lugged back to where the bag lay. They
forced him to take it up, though he pro-
tested wildly that it didn’t belong to him.
When he was led to the station door, the old
sergeant tried to get from him an admission
that the bag belonged to him. But the alien
shook his head doggedly 25 times, till the
sergeant lost his temper and snapped:
“Here, Joe! run th’ divil across the road a
few times. I’ll tach him to loi to me!” Joe
instantly grabbed the slack of the Confucian
pants,'knotted his fingers around the greasy
collar- and shoving the head well ahead of
the rest, rushed and dragged the helpless
fowl-stealer across the dark road and back
again. “You, stole thim fowls!” growled the
sergeant threateningly. “No savee fowl!”
yelped the prisoner. “Give him another
thrip, Joe !” And so it went on, until Joe
was streaming with perspiration. But the
prisoner hadn’t confessed. Then the .ser-
geant had him hustled into the cell, where
he took on the job in person. Grabbing the
floor mop and presenting it like a gun he
chased the defiant wretch into a corner
where he crouched and blinked like a rat’
“Now, you lyin’ divil,” roared the sergeant
“I’m goin’ to get th’ thruth out of yez ! You
stole thim fowls!” The jewel of Asia stared
at the threatening mop quivering within a
few inches of his face. He was nearly de-
mented with terror and rage, and his face
muscles were running and twitching. But
he looked the purple sergeant in the eye
bared his yellow fangs and sobbed: “You
—you Hanky liar!” The proceedings ter-
minated just there, and that’s the only third
degree story I can vouch for.
DRAFTS OF LOVE.
THE GRASS WIDOW : “Well, my husband says he loves me, but he never sends me any money ”
THE UNATTACHED BRUTE: “Sort of unremitting affection, eh? Bai Jove!”
A sound principle of Australian law has
just been re-established in Victoria. When
John Ahern died in 1888, he left his 74-acre
paddock at Wariandyte to his widow, on
whose death it was to go to William Lyons,
and, after him, to his son, if he had one,
and to his brothers Michael and Patrick, if
he hadn't; Michael and Michael’s sons to
get it before Patrick and Patrick’s sons.
They were to have no power to borrow,
mortgage or otherwise deal with the allot-
ment, which was “to go to the heirship of
the Lyons male family, and never to be sold
by them or heirs.” That was the dead
man’s dictum. A while back the widow
Ahern followed her good man, and the other
day William Lyons died, without issue;
whereupon the living man, in the person
of Justice Hodges, briefly remarked that a
testator might leave his property to a first
and a second beneficiary, but there wasn’t
any entail in this country, consequently
William Lyons’s heirs would have the pro-
perty, and they could do as they thought fit
with it.
From a Melbourne matron :
In these days of missing girls and white-slave
alarms, does this interest you ?
I was waiting for a train at a seaside suburb, when
a young woman approached me casting furtive
glances over her shoulder. I fancy I was selected
because I was standing somewhat apart, and was by
myself. She caught hold of my arm, asking if she
could stay with me. She was so frightened—there
was a Horrid Man, and he had taken her purse, and
she didn’t know what to do. The poor young thing
quite broke down.
It seemed she had taken some work —something in
the sewing line—from her aunt (with whom she
lived) to a lady, and had received 7s. 6d. in pay-
ment. She was sitting on the beach for a while
before returning home, when a man appeared, and
after a iew minutes’ conversation made the usual
infamous proposal. This she resented, and in the
commotion she dropned her purse, which he grabbed
and refused to give back, unless she fell in with his
views; so she fled to the station for protection. At
this stage a man appeared on the platform, and the
girl, becoming dingier than ever, gasped out, “That’s
him.’ He took a casual glance around; then leftr
I looked at him, but I am afraid I am not suffici-
ently acquainted with the Mark of the Brute to be
able to say whether he had it or not. He seemed
to me to be ordinary, common or garden man.
In the face of this loss the young woman could
not go back to her aunt, who possessed the usual
number of paralysed husbands and small children.
In addition to this, she lived at Footscray. It
seemed almost too much for one poor woman to bear.
I offered to buy my young friend a ticket to her
suburb, telling her she could travel with me as far as
town. This offer was coldly received, as by this
time I knew it would be. It was the 7s. 6d. she was
after.
Her distress was very well done. So were the
tears; but they did not impress me in the least. I
am a woman, and I know how easily tears are worked
up. As the train showed signs of coming, I cut her
short, telling her I did not believe a word of her
story. The instant change in her was simply ludicrous •
and her language was unfit for publication. She then
left. I was sufficiently interested to let the train
go. A minute or two later I strolled outside the
station, and was not a bit surprised to see the
young woman going back towards the beach with the
same man.
The game is played successfully sometimes, for I
heard of an almost similar case where the result was
far more satisfactory—-to the girl.
“Thirsty Bobite”: The N. S. Wales
Liquor Act, which provides for the closing
of hotels during the hours of polling for
Parliamentary candidates, shut up all the
bars on Sydney Cricket Ground during the
Queensland match, the S.C.C. being geo-
graphically in the Waverley electorate
where the second ballot scrimmage was rag-
ing. When I say it shut all the bars °I
mean it shut those which supply the bloke
on the hill and the fellow in the grand-
stand. But the bar in the membahs’ stand
passed the bottle and the crushed ice as
usual. _ That’s what they call democratic
legislation. . Anyhow, it’s what Gregory
Wade furnished as a sample of democratic
legislation in the Liquor Act of eight or nine
years ago.
CHRISTMAS EVE.
The serpent is a long-suffering and patient
beast. He has been reviled and cursed tor
centuries, and he has taken it all lyi“S
down. At intervals some extra-foolish indi-
vidual isn’t content with heaving verbiage
at him, but insults him by pulling his leg.
Then, occasionally, like the other worm, he
turns. The other day he reversed twice. At
La Perouse (Sydney) he bit one tormentor,
who was giving an exhibition of ‘charm-
ing,” and the infallible self-invented anti-
dote” didn’t do the coroner out of a jot).
The same day a Melbourne “expert got it
in the neck from a tiger snake, and it wa
the hospital and the surgeon’s knife ana
eventually the coroner for him, too.
THE SOUL OF ENERGY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The farmer and settler are the most industrious
units in any community.
Hundreds of farmers have been sworn in as special
constables along the water-front to keep the strikers
in order.
Alfred Biddle, who enticed Mrs. Liiley from her
home at Mayfield, and murdered her with a black-
smith’s hammer, was hanged at Lvttelton on Satur-
day. Death was instantaneous. The hangman was
a farmer, who volunteered his services.
—Maoriland news items.
He tills the land, and sows and reaps ;
His hay abounds in giant heaps;
His axe resounds amongst the hills;
He plods unwearied by the drills.
At dawn he rises from his bed,
And, in a voice to wake the dead,
Hunts some to harness up the ploughs
And some to herd and milk the cows.
All day he sweats at heavy toil,
This tireless yeoman of the soil,
And, as the night falls down the sky,
When city men to pleasure fly,
He seeks his sleep that he may be
Recharged with goodly energy.
Life is to him an endless task,
And nothing better would he ask.
The loafer’s lot, the hours of ease,
He has not thought nor use for these.
Day is for work, and night for rest ;
The frills of life are not its test.
He knows that Satan finds some ill
For idle hands and feckless will;
Wherefore, he plods across the farm
In search of jobs that fend such harm.
Odd times he helps to break a strike
■ (For laziness gives him the spike),
By hurrying away to town
To knock his loafing brother down—-
There’s nothing like a loaded shaft
To inculcate the love of graft.
Then, if he’s got an hour to spare,
And no one else will venture there
To drape the scaffold with the rope,
And grease the hangman’s knot with soap,
And fit the cap and press the knob,
You’ll find the farmer on the job.
N.S.W.

SYDNEY.
The Melbourne committee of the King
Edward VII. memorial fund is up against a
legal proposition. An enthusiastic citizen
who, in his first burst of loyalty to the late
monarch, promised a £5OO subscription, has
failed to draw a cheque for that or any lesser
amount. He is willing to meet the obliga-
tion in a testamentary manner, after his
decease; but the fund wants the cash, so the
committee proposes to test the question of his
liability in the law courts. Whichever way
the judge’s decision goes (the question is
considered too delicate to be submitted to a
jury), the case will be a warning to emo-
tional capitalists of the ultra-loyal order, as
well as a satirical reflection on the foolish-
ness of “loyalty” in placing a too-hasty val-
uation on its reverence for a dead king.
Kings, like their meanest subjects, are a
long time dead; and a sorrowing citizen,
whilst wearing inky pants and a purple
necktie on account of a national bereave-
ment, needs to look ahead and wonder
whether he will still feel like paying a £5OO
tribute to the deceased Majesty’s memory
when the time comes for unveiling the statue.
THE BENEFACTRESS.
MERCY : “She’s a wonderful good woman. I
never miss one of her lectures. I feel I
owe more to her than to any other living
creature, don’t you?”
MAY : “ Yes—she was co-respondent in my
divorce case.”
With the conviction for manslaughter of
the parents of the boy John Arthur Cooke,
of Fitzroy (Vic.), the end is reached of a
horrible and significant case of social
cruelty, the result of social folly. A case
may still be stated on the point whether, if
want of due care had only accelerated the
death of an already doomed person, there
was sufficient ground for a charge of man-
slaughter ; and it is this point that con-
cerns society generally. The boy Cooke
was 13 years of age at the time of his
death, and weighed 181 b. 2oz. He had lain
on a none-too-clean bed for two years,
slowly dying, and doomed to death from the
day of his birth. He was an almost com-
plete idiot, being unable to speak more than
the single word “Mum” or even to feed him-
self, save that, if a biscuit were placed on
his chest, he would feel for it and slowly
convey it to his mouth. The medical evi-
dence showed that the cause of death was
chronic inflammation of the brain, due to
congenital syphilis. This condition might
have been checked by treatment in the earlier
stages of the disease, but it must necessarily
have proved fatal before many years. The boy’s
parents and brother and sister are appar-
ently sane and healthy, yet the lad himself,
their firstborn, was a hopeless idiot from
birth, and was doomed beyond chance of
recovery to an early death. Away back,
two, three or even four generations ago,
syphilis had inoculated the blood of some
long dead and forgotten man or woman, and
this innocent boy was the victim, or one of
the victims. And, though in every State in
the Commonwealth, Government after Gov-
ernment goes on building reformatories and
enlarging lunatic asylums, hardly one will
lift a finger to attack the cause of all this
pitiful suffering and death.
* * * *
At last the aeroplane has figured in a real
battle. A Spanish force, onerating against the
Moors at Muley Abselam (Morocco), found
itself bogged in a sort of network of Moorish
trenches, so a flotilla of aeroplanes was let
loose and showered such a storm of
bombs that the Fuzzies swarmed out
of their pits in disorder, and, as they
did so, were charged by the Spaniards
with the cold steel. In this case the
tribesmen were armed only with rifles,
and the aeroplanes ascended to a
height beyond the range of such weapons.
A European army, of course, would be
equipped with special machine-guns for this
sort of work ; wherefore not too much weight
can be attached to the Spanish success.

which buzzed round St.
Peter’s Dome, and wept
because it was his mis-
sion to destroy the edi-
fice. A tomtit’s beak
swallowed him. No, you
must go a decade behind
Hickson and Forsyth,
back to Don, Berry,
Ebenezer Syme, Owens
and Wilson Gray—men
mostly who sat in “the
corner” in the Legislative
Assembly. That tiny
thundercloud overspread
the whole sky. Their
serious business started
with the famous Land
Convention, in Alderman
K e e 1 e y’s Conference
Hotel, where Brunton’s
Hotel is now. Journalist
O’Plea, afterwards nick-
named the Chief Baron,
was a useful member.
He wrote for the Symes,
and the Tory caricatur-
ists had a fearful down,
on him. Don worked as
a stonemason on the new
Melbourne Post Office by
day, and was M.L.A. at
night. However, there
was no payment of mem-
bers, so the Tories got
him into a Fitzroy pub,
and it was all over with
him when their carica-
turist depicted him pull-
ing the beer handle.
Letitia Anderson, of “the Rocks,” may
have been wrong, but her despairing cry,
uttered in Sydney Water Summons Court
lately, seems worthy of attention. Also, a
little investigation might be worth while.
Letitia was being ordered to quit premises
by January 1, when she wailed as follows:
Qt J get a housej and I owe 18s. rent.
I wish I was a Chinaman and kept a gam-
bling house. Then I’d be all right.” If Sydney
had not been afflicted with the congestion
trouble, through trying to digest the whole
trade of the State, Letitia might have been
comfortably housed amidst European neigh-
bors at a reasonable rent, and the unpleasant
court proceedings might have been cut out.
You get some curious results by crowding
people together and boosting rents.
THE JOYS OF CHRISTMAS.
CUSTOMER : “I think I will change my mind."
ABSENT-MINDED SHOPMAN : “Sorry, madam — can't allow customers to change anything
at Christmas.”
The Victorian police hold £135, which they
don’t quite know what to do with. They
don’t exactly want an owner, because that
habitually needy person, the State Treasurer,
will presently claim it; but if anybody
< chooses to establish a claim, he can have the
money—and the consequences. The money
was seized by the law when it raided certain
Melbourne premises allegedly occupied by
“Barney” Allen as a betting shop. Allen
wasn’t there at the time, being in Sydney,
and occupancy by him was denied on his
behalf. Three alleged “managers” or assist-
ants were in the place, but they all dis-
claimed ownership of the immoral money,
and unanimously averred that one “Bertie”
Allen, who hasn’t set foot in the Common-
wealth for six months, is the tenant. When
the Bench had' grasped the facts as sworn,
it smiled grimly, fined the three “managers”
£25 apiece, and hinted that it would be so
glad to meet the real boss when he called to
claim his £135. So far, he hasn’t called.
At Geelong recently certain Laborites
asked permission to hold “Pleasant Sunday
Afternoon” gatherings on the beach slopes ;
but a Wowserish council decided that
nothing of the sort could be allowed, not-
withstanding that golf is played on the links
near by every Sabbath, and that the Labor-
ites did not propose to compel anybody to
come to their immoral meetings. Then it
was rumored that the Council would be de-
fied and that a meeting would take place.
The Council hurriedly got its authority to
bear, and 11 large-footed policemen were
introduced to the crowd, with instructions to
arrest anybody attempting to give an ad-
dress. The Laborites, not yet at the end
of their resources, hired a small boat,
moored it a dozen yards from land, and —the
rest was easy. The Council’s authority
stops at the shore, so the John Hops had
to grin and bear it like Christians. But
the poor old Sabbath was desecrated hor-
ribly, and has hardly been able to hold its
head up since.
At this happy season of the year, when
the smirking Ah Chow rewards the white
Australian for her custom by presenting- her
with a pot of ginger, The Bulletin has no
displeasure whatever in introducing a statis-
tical enthusiast, who lately went into figures
regarding these gifts. The jars went the
specified weight ail right, but the ginger in
one, labelled to contain lib., panned out at
50z.; “syrup” made up the other lloz. The
21b. jars averaged 9foz. each of ginger, and
the 31b. humbugs panned out 14oz. Mr.
C. E. Waters, a Sydney man, who lately
had a look at the ginger factories in Southern
China, here takes up the narrative :■ —-
The factories are merely sheds with earthen floors,
without sanitary appliances of any sort. Among the
workers are cripples and others suffering- from loath-
some diseases, which prevent them from actually
moving about. Such workers are employed trim-
ming and cutting the ginger root. The water used
for the syrup in which the ginger is boiled is ob-
tained from little canals about the factories. This
water, which flows through the paddy (rice) fields, in
which the Chinese wash themselves, and which they
defile, is so polluted that no European or intelligent
Chinaman would dare drink it. The old ginger that
is left over from last year or the year before, and
has been condemned and returned from other
countries, is cooked up fresh, packed and sent down
to Australia. These factories are only among those
which cater for the cheap trade. No other country in
the world receives such cheap, common quality of
ginger as is packed and shipped to Australia. None
of the European houses handle any of the cheap
stuff, which is shipped entirely by Chinamen, the
Europeans, of course, not being able to compete.
After which The Bulletin trusts that the
white Australian woman will enjoy the
Christmas delicacy as usual.
“Marloo”: When N.S.W. statesmen have
finished discussing Miss Alice Bilking, would
they mind prodding somebody until that sec-
tion of the Bure Foods Act is enforced whicly
requires that drinking vessels used in hotel
bars, restaurants, refreshment rooms, etc.,
shall be cleansed in clear running water,
when such water is or can be made avail-
able? I’m tired of eating my beer from a
glass which, after being extracted from a
sweaty, beer-stained, microbe-infested beard,
is plunged into a tub of grimy fluid, and
licked with a filthy rag to ensure a fair
and honest distribution of all the germs on
the premises. In the country the disregard
for the law is even more scandalous than it
is in the city: in a North Coast court the
other day the licensing inspector said he
knew only one hotel in his whole district
where the law wasn’t the deadest of corpses.
THE FESTIVE SEASON.
SHE : “Oh, George darling, isn't Christmas
just too jolty. Everyone looks so happy
and gay. ”
“Lometo”: Apropos the curses that have
been heaved at Australian small-pox vac-
cination lymph, The Bulletin may be in-
terested to know that the Government medi-
cal officer here, in the Marshall Islands, re-
cently vaccinated several hundred Kanakas
with Australian lymph, without a single fail-
ure. All “took” beautifully, and there were no
cases of “bad arm.” Whether the goods
were up to standard in the first wild
rush for lymph I don’t know ; but it is pretty
clear on this experience that the stuff is all
right now.
A SONG OF PEACE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The remarks of Dr. Manmx, coadjutor-archbishop,
on Sunday evoked a reply from Rev. 11. Gainford
lederal grand president of the Protestant Defence
Association, last evening. —Daily papev.
Peace to the servants of God !
Locked in a death-grip once more,
See how they batter and prod,
Down on the paper-strewn floor !
Ink on their vestments and hair,
Kicking with boots iron-shod !
Would you see zeal? It is here!
Peace to the servants of God !
Peace to the servants of God,
Sitting in Heaven ! Down here,
Where it is dusty and dim,
Someone is biting an ear!
Kicks and collections and yells,
Fighting incessant and grim,
Under the scullery bells !
Peace to the servants of Him !
N.S.W.

PHENOMENAL GROWTH OF A BUSINESS.
The approximate sales of O.T. certified by the
Company’s Auditor for the 12 months ending June,
1906, were 43,000 bottles, equivalent to 1,000,000
drinks. For the 12 months ending June, 1913, sales
were over 3,000,000 bottles—equivalent to over
73,000,000 drinks. Judging by its great popularity,
there must be singular merit in O.T.*

PERSONAL
ITEMS
GRAHAM, who was
Watt's Minister for
Agriculture and Water
Supply (Vic.).
Jesuit Father Sydes,
lately a practising
barrister in Brisbane,
is now delivering Sun-
day evening lectures
at the church of his
order in Richmond
(Yic.). He is forcible
and dramatic, and the
“jury man” sticks out
from under the stole
and surplice. Father
Lentaigne, one of the
two founders of his
order in Australia in
the 60’s of last cen-
tury, had been an Irish lawyer. The Oblate
Fathers have, of late years, settled down in
Westralia. Their famous preacher, Father
Cooke, had been a medical man before he
put on the biretta.
General Kirkpatrick left for London on
December 9, and his salary runs till April
30. John Fuller, ex-Gov. of Vic., draws
nearly six months’ screw, or over £2500, for
doing nothing. And the case of George Reid
is nearly as bad. Meantime policemen get
7s. 6d. a day, and we say we can’t afford to
pay them more. Well, perhaps we can’t.
Governor Bosanquet, who will be leaving
Soutfi Australia in February, has been ban-
queted by the resident consuls. As a
British admiral, he has visited nearly all
the countries some time or other, and they
were all represented, from the U.S.A. down
to Liberia. Germany took the chair, and
the toasts (besides “Our Guest”) were
simply “King George” and “Other Nations.”
Charles H. Dight, one of the best-known
residents of Albury, died last week. He was
one of the pioneer settlers on the Upper
Murray. The original Dights were Hawkes-
bury people, and Dight’s Falls, on the Yarra,
dating from the early days of Fawkner and
Batman, keep John Dight in memory. He
built the first mill at the Falls; it became
a picturesque ruin after the roaring ’so’s,
when Dight and Rucker gave up the busi-
ness.
Till Henry Scott died last week, at 77,
few people in Adelaide knew he was still
living. He had been one of its foremost
citizens for years : squatter, wool-buyer and
insurance man, Mayor of Adelaide, and Leg.
Councillor for a long term, director of the
Adelaide and National banks, and promi-
nent in a variety of charities. For years
past he had, however, been completely
broken down in health.
Penry, the youngest of Dr. Bevan’s four
fine sons, died in England last week. He
was to have come to Australia next year as
secretary to one of the Science Congress
sections, and a number of grave matrons
and scholarly spinsters were longing to see
him again. All the Bevan boys did brilliant
things at Melbourne University before going
off to fill “positions.” Two of them are
lights of learning in poor old China. One
is the first Judge of the Supreme Court in
the Northern Territory. Penry, whose
death is reported, was a science professor
at an English college. He had some ailment
for which “an operation was necessary,” and
the operation proved fatal. He was still
on the sunny side of 40.
Henry Parker, Chief Justice of Westralia,
has resigned, and Justice McMillan has suc-
ceeded him. The Attorney-General, who
made the announcement in the House, is
“Tommy” Walker, well known at one time
in N. S. Wales as representative of a min-
ing constituency, and as a careless man with
firearms. The ex-Chief Justice is a native
of York (W.A.), and is 67 years of age; he
has been on the Bench 22 years. The new
Chief is a Londoner, born in 1858, son of a
barrister, and 11 years on the Bench. No
mention is made of his successor, but it is
highly improbable that Attorney-General
“Tommy” will claim the office.
One of the inscrutable mysteries of
woman’s character came out in the case of
Mrs. Newman, who, beyond doubt, murdered
two, if not more, babies, and buried their
bodies on her husband’s selection near Mor-
dialloc (Yic.). One evening last winter she
and her husband called on a neighbor, whom
thev knew only by sight, but who had four
children down with diphtheria. While the
husband chopped up a bit of wood, she made
herself friendly, and then stayed on, nursing
the sick little ones for days and nights. On
the doctor’s authority she was the main fac-
tor in pulling them through. And she did it
all without hope of fee or reward.
Engineer R. Duppa Lloyd, who died lately
in London, aged 92, was Ferdinand de Les-
sep’s first consulting engineer on the big
Suez job. He had one brief spell in Aus-
tralia, coming to Sydney in 1868, to report
on the railways for the N.S.W. Government,
and remaining till ’73. During those years
he picked up a financial interest in the Bur-
burgate station on Liverpool Plains. By
way of recreation he raked together from
everywhere a vast collection of pictures,
china and bronzes. C. W. Lloyd, the
“father” of the Australian Club (Sydney),
is a brother.
Major F. A. Dove, who joined the Big
Legion lately, was primarily a public-school
teacher, but became a lieutenant in a
N.S.W. volunteer regiment in 1898, and a
captain in 1899. He played a good hand in
the Boer card-party, with scouting as his
strong suit; was “mentioned in despatches,”
and made a D.S.O. Later, he did fine work
as adjutant of the N.S.W. public school
cadets. He was appointed to the Adminis-
trative and Instructional Staff in 1906, and
in 1911 became Director of Equipment at
Headquarters (Melb.), with the rank of
major. About a year ago paralysis of the
spine got hold of him, and ultimately beat
him at the age of 46.
Mrs. Tracy left Melbourne last week, with
a Federal Government commission to urge
immigration to Australia on British plat-
forms. The handsome, energetic woman is
just cut out for such lecture work. When
she made her first visit to England, two or
three years ago, she was in the political line
of business, and she drew cheques from the
Imperial Mission, the National Service
League (Lord Charles Beresford’s affair)
and the Tariff Reform League, and got her-
self accepted as a dashing Australian with
plenty to say and an effective manner of say-
ing it. Her present undertaking was mooted
at the time when the late Batchelor was in
the Labor Ministry. Batchelor suggested it
to George Reid, whose presence in Australia
just now enabled Mrs. Tracy to get an
arrangement fixed up.
EDWARD GOLL, PIANIST.
The Rev. Alexander Barnard Davis, a
former Chief Minister of the Jewish Syna-
gogue in Sydney, died recently. This vet-
eran rabbi was born in London in 1828, and
20 years later was headmaster of the West-
minster Jews’ School in Soho. Later, he
was in charge of the Jewish congregation at
Kingston, Jamaica, and came to Sydney in
1862, when the Synagogue was a very small
circumstance in York-street. He had a large
share in building the big Elizabeth-street
tabernacle, opened in 1878; was one of the
pioneers of the Anglo-Jewish Association,
and was generally in the front when any-
thing was doing for the betterment of Israel.
He retired in 1903. In addition to many
sermons, he published (in 1868) “Jewish
Rites and Ceremonies” —an interesting guide
which has found its way into many Gentile
libraries.
Died in Adelaide, W. L. Neale. This time
the tragedy was of failure rather than of suc-
cess. Twenty-five years ago Neale was the
brilliant man of the S. Australian school
service: still a youngster, he was headmaster
of Sturt-street —the big “model” school of
the State. Then he took a false step: Tas-
mania called him in to report on its educa-
tion system, and afterwards invited him to
do the necessary reorganising. He thought
little of the material he had to work on,
brought over numerous S. Australians, and
met criticism in his usual perky way. In
the row that followed all the force "of old
Tasmania threw itself against the stranger,
and he w-ent dow-n and out. His old service
was closed to him ; so, after many buffetings,
the brilliant educationist took a job as clerk
in the Federal Land Tax Office, and he was
there when paralysis ended it.
From “Chasuble’s” funeral oration:—
Exit Cardinal Rampolla, the most bitterly disap-
pointed man on earth. As Secretary of State he was
Prime Minister to Leo XIII., and thus virtually Pope
tor several years. Generally he was tipped for the
Papacy in 1903, and appeared to have a right to the
billet. But a section in the conclave of cardinals
nominated Cardinal Gotti, an amiable, pious gentle-
man, Rampolla being more famed for statesmanlike
qualities. Nobody thought of humble Cardinal Sarto,
Patriarch of Venice. After several ballots, Rampolla
came out with such a strong lead that his election
seemed certain. At this stage, however, the Cardinal
Archbishop of Cracow put in a veto by the Austrian
Emperor against Rampolla. It does not appear
whether the Archbishop was armed with this all
along, or whether he had managed to smuggle out
of the conclave a wire, to the Emperor and get one in
return. Anyhow, the conclave bowed to the mandate.
The angry Rampolla partisans then refused to vote
for Gotti; they pitched upon Cardinal Sarto, who
was accepted by the Gotti followers as of the same
class. So, as usual, nothing happened but the unfore-
seen. Pius X. at once decreed that the user of a
veto at any future conclave would be visited with
excommunication. But that didn’t mollify Rampolla,
who declined to continue as Prime Minister; so the
office was conferred on young Merry Del Val, along
with a cardinal’s hat.
An Adelaide constable is under suspension
for the most remarkable excess of zeal this
paper can remember at the moment. One
hot night he simply went out, dragged in any
citizen he happened to meet, and charged him
with drunkenness. The first was duly
locked up, but, after that, the swift proces-
sion of protesting and bewildered captives
added to the staggering fact that the captor
had discarded his uniform, caused the official
mind to suspect that all was not well.
The old-time murder of martinet Price
by a group of Williamstown (Melb.) con-
victs was recalled last week by the death
of a nonagenarian baker, named Knight
IVhen the news of Price’s death was noised
around Vyilliamstown, it was stated that all
the convicts had “risen” for the purpose of
promiscuous slaughter, though, as a matter
of fact, the very men who did the gory deed
had contentedly calmed down as soon as
they were quits with the tyrant, and the
other convicts were full of peace and good-
will. The rumor of the imaginary “rising”
brought a band of excited townsmen upon
the scene, and Knight, the local baker was
one of them. Each carried a musket’or a
blunderbuss, and professed to be eager for the
fray; but as none of the weapons were
loaded, and nobody had brought any cart-
ridges, it was lucky that the convicts were
not on the job.
Seamen’s missionary Shearston, who the
other day was made Naval Commander
under Admiralty warrant, is a man of one
idea the worthy one of saving man-o’-wars-
men from the harpies of Sydney port. Those
who to-day see the Naval House in Gros
vonor-street, with its fine appointments are
not to suppose that the thing grew up of its
own accord. Its success was laid in years
of penury, distrust and even opprobrium
Shearston and his wife first started a lodging-
house on the Rocks. They picked up a booz-
ing Jack Tar sometimes from the gutter
sometimes out of the hands of the police-’
they saved his money and his reputation, and
they placed him on shipboard with colors
flying. They were no mere praying machines •
they knew exactly what they were up against
and brought solid sense to bear on each situa-
tion. From that early effort grew the Naval
House, which is known through almost all
sections. of the British Fleet. Shearston’s
new designation as naval commander became
necessary in consequence of a hitch in the
quartering of the relief crew of the Torch:
Naval House was temporarily gazetted a
ship, and Shearston was placed on deck as
naval commander, with the Admiralty pen-
nant flying aloft.

Watt's Minister
of Mines,
looks towards

The irony of Death ! A group of Ballarat
sporting men were talking about ages, and
one proudly remarked that he had scored 58
and never felt better in his life. Then, the
cheery boast made, he flung up his arms,
gasped, and died of heart disease.
THE SPOILS TO THE HUSTLER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
:>lr. Wade was at the opera when he heard of the
Labor victories.. . .Mr. Holman was campaigning
at Bega, having rushed into the country to whip up
the electors to the second ballot in the contests yet
to be decided. —Press item.
The “leader” of the Liberals was drugged with
Wagner’s brass,
While subterranean Pitt-street cursed the thing that
came to pass,
And Wood who fled from Bega fell before the “work-
ing class.”
All in an opera stall, and nigh unto the trombone’s
shade,
While St. George destroyed the Dragon and the
Pitt-street cellar prayed,
Ihe statesman of the opera was in the balance weighed.
Now, half a hundred Hurstville men had each given
seven days’ pay,
And worked a week for nothing that their man might
win the day. ...
But Wade dozed at the opera where all the brasses
bray.
And Holman went to Bega, and he travelled and he
spoke,
And the Energetic Party put its strength unto the
yoke—
And the Cellar said that party was a common Labor
bloke.
Nor has it learned the lesson yet, because its head
is fat,
That electors like the man who hustled while the
others sat —
Like the men who aim at something, while Wade
sleeps beneath his hat.
And subterranean Pitt-street brayed like one collec-
tive ass,
And Wade, the statesman, doped himself with Mister
Wagner’s brass —
And the votes went to the hustlers of the vulgar
working class.
N.S.W.

This finely-organised concern has nowadays a capi-
tal of £1,000,000 in 200,000 £5 ordinary shares and
£5OO 000 in 50,000 £lO 5 p.c. prefs. In addition
it has £2,263,140 in irredeemable debentures
(mostly at 4 p.c.), and another £376,473 in ter-
minable debentures (all but £2OOO of it also at
4 p.c.). Thus, unlike most concerns in the pastoral-
finance business, it works to a very small extent on
withdrawable money: liabilities of all kinds other
than to debenture-holders amounted at the end of
June to £2,998,688, including customers’ balances
(for produce sold, etc.), sums owing for mer-
chandise and balances in transit between branches.
On the other side —but this table puts the matter
in a few lines:—
Liabilities.
1900. 1912. 1913.
Capital .. .. £1,000,000 £1,500,000 £1,500,000
Perpetual 4£ p.c. 620,000 511,037 509,749
„ 4 p.c. 1,643,210 1,755,098 1,753,391

In the foregoing Sundry Creditors are down at
£3,021,069, as against the £2,998,688 mentioned
previously; the difference is made up of interest
owing to debenture-holders and on pref. shares.
There have been times, perhaps, when the 4 p.c.
fixed debt has seemed a burden; but the policy of
securing financial supplies by means of interminable
debentures, instead of through withdrawable deposits,
is unquestionably the right one in a business of this
kind. Though it has its city agencies, and would
just as soon sell a motor to a suburban doctor as
to a backblocks squatter, most of Dalgety’s business
is with people who grow wool and wheat and butter ;
and no doubt the woolly connection is easily the
most important of the three. Now, from the very
nature of the business, wool is grown chiefly where
conditions are too uncertain for closer settlement
by wheat or cow cookies; and occasional failures
and heavy losses in the sheep-growing business are
therefore inevitable. On the other hand, there is no
business in the world where so quick a recovery can
be made. Hence the man or institution to pull
through is the one that doesn’t have to realise as
soon as drought has done its worst; wherefore the
enormous importance of having funds which are not
liable to be called for when things look gloomy.
Dalgety’s picked some of the fruits of its wise policy
when it passed through the ’9o’s without having to
make a composition with anyone. And it has shown
the same prudence in the recent fat years by sitting
hard on dividends and either writing off surplus
profits or putting them by to formal reserves^ —a
policy against which shareholders are all the less
likely to rebel because they know that, if things go
wrong, they will be liable for calls amounting to
£l5 per share. Mention of the fat years recalls
the , fact that properties (“advances, properties,
etc.,” in the foregoing comparison) no longer exist;
the chairman told shareholders at the annual meeting
that, “taking advantage of favorable seasons, we
have sold all the properties we formerly held.”

The National Mutual has thrown a lot of energy into
its work during the last few years ; and, not being so
tied up with redtape as some offices, it takes special
business which others turn down because they haven’t
got ready-made tables to meet the case. In other
respects it wanders off the regular track. Some-
times, in this paper’s opinion, the regular track is
safer; yet, admittedly, there are good pickings to be
had by wandering, and whether any bad results follow
is largely a question of management and close
supervision. Also, perhaps, a matter sometimes of
luck. For instance, there is the question of medical
examination. The advantages of medical examina-
tion are to some extent a matter of opinion. Evi-
dently, in the National Mutual’s opinion, you can
dispense with it very largely by a system of lay
reports. The immediate result is that, whereas the
A.M.P., for example, spends between 12 and 13 p.c.
of new-premium income in medical fees and doctors’
travelling allowances, the National Mutual last year
spent barely 5 p.c. The total outlay in this direc-
tion was £6737, while the new premiums amounted
to £122,897, exclusive of single premiums and con-
sideration for annuities. Whether the mortality ex-
perience will suffer remains to tie seen, though as the
system has been pursued more or less for a good
many years the society ought by this time to know
if it is on dangerous ground or not. Apparently it
doesn’t think so, seeing that medical inspection is
being cut out more than ever:—
Proportion of Medical Exes, to New Premiums.
New Medical
Premiums. Exes
1903 .. £68,456 £7503 10.9 p.c.
1911 . . 116,168 7844 6.7 „
1912 .. 120,518 7265 6.0 „
1913 .. 122,897 6737 5.4 „
For the present, the most obvious result of the cut-
ting-out of medical examinations is to produce a
shrinkage in the expense rate —due not perhaps so
much to the mere fact that the doctor’s fee is saved
as to the other unquestionable fact that thousands of
people don’t like the idea of medical examination.
And while the man with a weak spot may be
attracted to an office which has no medical examina-
tion, to be bowled out easily enough unless he gives
fraudulent answers to the society’s questions, it is
pretty certain that most of the people who have a
stupid objection to be examined by a doctor are
those who are so beastly healthy that they have never
made a doctor’s professional acquaintance. This field
is a big one, and the working of it is no doubt largely
responsible for the reduction shown in the following
figures:—
Expenses Compared with Total Income.
1904 ..£lB 1 5 p.c. | 1909 ..£l5 1 1 p.c.
1905 . . 18 2 9 „ | 1910 .. 15 0 3 „
1906 . . 17 13 9 „ | 1911 . . 14 14 4 „
1907 .. 17 10 8 „ j 1912 .. 13 11 11 „
1908 . . 16 14 6 „ | 1913 .. 12 10 9 „
The reduction is accompanied by a certain amount of
risk. The size of the. risk depends on the closeness
of the supervision already alluded to. Even now the
expense rate is not by any means low. Still, follow-
ing The Bulletin’s old plan of charging renewal
premiums 7 p.c. and single-premium business 3
p.c., the cost of the new business has been got down
to 92.3 p.c., which is a very marked improvement on
the figures of a few years ago. It brings the society
at any rate within speaking distance of the best,
from this point of view. But whether the reduction
is made at the expense of the mortality experience is
a point which revenue accounts won’t determine. The
actuary’s report and the bonuses must speak as to
that. The National Mutual is one of the very few
offices which haven’t adopted the system of annual
valuations. Its bonuses are distributed only every
third year. The last investigation was made as at
the end of 1910. It is to be hoped that in the
forthcoming report special attention will be devoted
to the effect (if any) which the no-medical examina-
tion system has had upon the mortality rate.
In one other important respect the National Mutual
wanders off the regular track. This is in regard to
investments. Most insurance people, believing that
absolute security is a consideration of overshadowing
importance, are nowadays inclined to put more and
more of the policyholders’ money into public securi-
ties, which are guaranteed by the whole taxable value
of the property in the State which issues them. The
National Mutual, however, looks on these securities
coldly, and prefers to take its chance with loans on
mortgage and similar investments. The following
table shows the disposition of the assets:—
1900. 1910. 1912. 1913.
Public securities 3.4 p.c. 5.1 p.c. 6.2 p.c. 5.6 p.c.
Mortgages .. 50.5 „ 55.1 „ 60 6 „ 59.5 „
Foreclosures . . 4.4 „ 1.3 „ 0.9 „ 0.8 „
Freeholds.. .. 12.7 „ 12.8 „ 10.8 „ 10.3 „
Loans on policies 14.3 „ 9.8 „ 9.6 „ 9.1 „
Other assets . . 14.7 „ 15.9 ~ 11.9 „ 14.7 „
“Other assets” include “deferred instalments of
annual premiums,” £139,681 —a highly questionable
line which no other insurance office of standing in
Australia maintains in its balance-sheet. The society
really takes credit for unpaid premiums, some of
which may never be paid. Not that there is any
dishonesty whatever about it, for where the item
doesn’t appear, the actuary, in making his calcula-
tions, takes the “deferred instalments” into account.
The effect of the National Mutual’s plan is to inflate
the balance-sheet, and make the proportion of mort-
gages and freeholds look rather smaller than it reallv
is. The natural consequence of holding so large a
proportion of the funds in mortgages is the earning
of high interest: —
Interest Earned on Averaoe Funds.

On the other hand, mortgages take a lot of watching,
and watching costs money in management. Also,
there are almost inevitable losses, in spite of the
best of management, and this society's investment
fluctuation fund is only £69,517, which is much
less than it ought to be.
J. Fielding and Co., Ltd., boxmakers,
printers and carton makers (Sydney), who began in
1910 with a goodwill or paper asset of £5OOO,
shown hereunder as a deficit: —

When Thomas Davis and Co. made a valuation just
before the co. was floated, the assets were certified
to exceed the liabilities by £7867. As the vendors took
£867 in cash and 12,000 shares, the price paid for
goodwill was £5OOO. Besides those 12,000 shares
issued to the vendors 6068 have been issued
to the public; and though that didn’t bring
in as much capital as was required, the
business has done so well that, notwithstanding an
outlay of £9065 on land and building, the overdraft
(secured on mortgage) is now only £3976. Other
liabilities total £2276 ; but against that there are
book debts of £4045, in addition to stock and
plant. Still the co. would be safer and more com-
fortable if its liabilities were less ; and as the man-
agement recognises this the dividend has been reduced.
However, 8 p.c. isn’t to be despised, especially when
there is enough profit left over to pay very nearly
another 7 p.c. Certainly, profit in a case of. this
kind depends upon the stocktaking; a pessimist with
a liver not working quite right might value the stock
at a few hundreds less, which for that year, at any
rate, would lop that much off the profits. If the
stock has been properly valued, and other assets are
worth their book value, then the little co. has done
very well indeed to get rid of more than four-fifths
of the goodwill loading in three years. At the outset
the tangible assets were equal to 14s. 6d. per share.
Now they represent 19s. It is very good progress
indeed.

This co., whose motto is understood to be “Go on
the lend, young man,” now balances once a year
instead of every six months ; and that fact has to be
remembered when the eye is running down the profit
column. For the past year results have been excel-
lent ; they have been rather better than the figures
in the last line suggest, inasmuch as £4OO has been
written off Furniture and Fittings account. True,
almost everything depends on the valuation. If the
zhendleman who puts a value on the pledged over-
coat is an optimist he is liable to think very well of
the garment, in which case the reserves shown in the
table are optimistic also. The co. has “pledges,
stock and sundry goodwill items” valued at £32,551,
so there is plenty of room for optimism. On the
other hand, there is just as much room for pessimism.
The fact for the investor to note is that the stock is
of a nature which must make a valuation difficult, and
as to both profits and reserves he has to depend
wholly on the judgment of the management, which
can do no more than make an estimate of things.
Capital and reserves combined are only £18,286 ;
capital alone (now £12.911) used to be £25,176,
but the balance was written off after several of the
co.’s servants had taken serious liberties with the
cash. So the co. has to work on outside money to
the extent of over £16,000, chiefly overdrafts and
fixed deposits. As to the prospects, this and every
other reputable three-balls shop seem to be on a good
wicket. The idea that their harvest comes only in
bad times is exploded. People borrow, certainly, in
bad times because they are hard up and want neces-
saries; but they borrow just as strenuously when
times are good and they want luxuries.

The reserves are made up this way:—
Sinking Fund . . . . . . £28,441
Reserve Fund . . .. . . 8,359
Total . . . . . . £30,800
Less Revenue account debit . . 555
Total Reserves .. . . £36,245
The sinking fund is, of course, designed to accumu-
late profit and eventually pay off the borrowed
money, so that the municipality may actually own
the works which at present it merely thinks it owns,
the real owner being the moneylender who put up the
capital. The idea is to make the undertaking the
city’s very own in something under 50 years; and
though 50 years may be a long time coming, half a
century is far shorter than eternity. And without
a sinking fund the city wouldn’t really own the
business until then. Besides the sinking fund the
profits are feeding a replacement reserve, whose pur-
pose is to give the city a plant, etc., as good as new
when the time comes to pay off the last loan.
Whether enough is being paid into this fund to
counter-balance current depreciation, The Bulletin
is not prepared to say. As one of the foregoing
tables shows, the total amount available for reserves
last year was £8785; and this was cut up as
follows: To sinking fund, £2837; to replacement
reserve, £6231; total, £9068. But as there was
only £8785 of profit available, it follows that, in
order to make those appropriations, the council had
to increase the Profit and Loss account debit by
£283. In other words, the undertaking didn’t give
enough profit to pay to the reserve funds the amount
which it was thought necessary to credit them with.
This means either that the charges are a shade less
than they ought to be or the exes, are a shade more
than they ought to be.
Vic. State Coal Mine (Wontliaggi) :
The profit for the year to June 30 was £41,463,
which, together with previous profit, has been allo-
cated as follows:
Written off To Sink- Total
June, for Depreciation. ing Fund. Profit.
1911 .. £17,052 £7,050 £24,102
1912 . . 1,892 7,942 9,834
1913 . . 10,629 30,834 41,463
£29,573 £45,826 £75,399
The position is not, however, anything like so good
as it looks from those figures. Thus, though so far
£29,573 has been provided for depreciation, the
general manager estimates that the total deprecia-
tion has been £42,000. For the past year, it is
said that the depreciation was £25,546. That being
so, the net profit for the year, after paying interest,
was £15,917 ; and £9OOO of that goes towards the
repayment of capital. The fixed capital, raised by
the State, was £150,000, of which £7050 has been
paid off ; and £53,866 was advanced by the Treasury.
The total amount owing to the State on June 30 was
therefore £196.816; and other liabilities totalled
£31,591. Against that there are works (including
money spent on development) which cost £203,956,
and stores, cash and investments amounting to
£77,456. The accounts are very badly presented;
most public accounts are. As far as can be made
out the actual position is that, after paying interest,
the mine in three vears has earned £75,399 in
excess of outgoings; and the depreciation has been
£42,000. If the assets are worth £42,000 less than
they were brand-new, then the net profit has been
£33,399, of which, as mentioned above, £15,917
was made in the last 12 months. If, however, the
depreciation doesn’t include amortisation—in other
words, if it doesn’t provide for the return of capital
sunk in the mine, and used up as the mine has been
carted away and sold—then the aggregate net
.profit is much less than £33,399. Probably it is
less than half of it. And the profit for the past year
was less than half of £15,917. It is impossible to
say just how much, because the amount which
ought to be charged for return of capital depends on
the quantity of coal in the mine.
To put things on a proper basis, a fixed amount
ought to be charged on every ton of coal taken from
the mine, and the total sum ought to be used on the
one side of the books to write down (that is to say,
pay off) capital, and on the other to write down the
book value of the undertaking. This sum, made up
by charging (say) 2d. per ton of coal, shouldn’t be
reckoned as profit at all; it is capital consumed in
earning the revenue. The depreciation fund, on the
other hand, should be a fund, provided out of surplus
earnings, for the replacement of plant. In their
present form the accounts may mean anything. The
one thing one can be pretty sure they don’t mean is
that a profit of £41,463 was made last year. And
yet that is what they seem to be most anxious that
one should think they mean.
The general manager puts the matter as follows:
