modities it should move up and stay up, if there is
anything at all in the theory that the enormously
increased production of gold has cheapened that
metal and thereby caused higher prices, gold being
our one standard of value. On the other hand, the
largely-increased attention given to dredging may be
expected to give the tin market a downward ten-
dency. As a matter of fact, the tin market defies
all efforts to fathom it. It got down in 1896 to
well under £6O without any justification whatever
from statistics; and though there is no reason now
to expect anything of that sort, it is just as well to
remember all the time the extraordinary vagaries of
the animal.
Before Denham left for the London pawn-
shop with Queensland’s credit in his pocket and the
right to tax Queensland in his bag, the president of
the Merchants’ Association dropped in with a letter
of advice. Briefly : “Lay hold of as much loan money
as possible. No matter what it costs, the State can
stand it.” Queensland is the old, hard-drinker
whose last previous drunk isn’t all paid for yet.
Twenty-five _ years ago the ass of the period was
saying precisely what the Merchant Ass.’s president
is saying now. And the result was that Queens-
land’s three banks went so disastrously bung that
the biggest of them hasn’t paid a dividend to this
day, but is still busy paying off deposits which it
couldn’t pay back when they were due; the general
manager of another committed suicide when he saw
the dreadful mess into which the borrowing in-
sanity had landed his shareholders; and the third
has still so much leeway to make up that even now,
after nearly 25 years, its shares are still a long
way under par. Surely it’s about a fair thing to
square up for the old drunk before bursting out into
another.
* * * *
At the recent half-yearly meeting of the
Bank of N. S. Wales the chairman said the directors
had decided upon demolishing the old premises upon
the corner of Barrack and George streets, Sydney,
and erecting up-to-date head offices. This will be a
sort of celebration of the centenary of the Bank.
In its 96 years of life it has had but three head
offices. The first was a building in Macquarie-place,
adjoining Reibey-lane, which was rented from Mrs.
Reibey. In the early ’2o’s the head office was in
Chisholm’s Thistle Inn, a quaint old building which
stood on the site of Bradley’s auction rooms that
were and the Burlington Cafe that is. In 1853, at
the sale of the old Military Barracks site, the direc-
tors purchased the spot, on which they placed their
modest headquarters, the Commercial Bank securing
the Barrack-street corner. Between the banks were
two shops, in one of which Hoffnung’s made their
start. In the early ’6o’s each bank secured the
adjoining shop, which gave the two institutions the
whole frontage between Barrack and Wynyard streets.
* * ■* *
The Privy Council delivered its judgment
last week in the Sydney Meat Preserving Co.’s ease,
and Miles, the plaintiff, is knocked out in the last
round. It is law, of course, because the Privy Coun-
cil says so, and the Privy Council speaks last; but
whether it is justice is quite another question. The
principle laid down is that, though a company may
be formed to (presumably) pay dividends to its
shareholders, it need not do anything of the sort,
but may buy its supplies from certain shareholders
at such a high price that no profit may be avail-
able for distribution. This aims a very nasty blow
at joint-stock enterprises. Supposing a co. is formed
to carry on, say, a printing and stationery business.
Under this decision the directors need not ever pay
a dividend (the Sydney Meat Preserving Co. never
has paid one), but may abolish profits by the simple
device of letting the shareholders have their printing
at 25 p.c. under cost. It would be outrageously
rough on shareholders who didn’t want any printing
done, but no rougher than it is in the case of Miles,
who, though the largest individual shareholder in
the Meat Co., has no stock to sell, and therefore
gets no benefit whatever from his holding in the co.
The Colonial Mutual Life directors send
their policy-holders a bulky Christmas card, in the
shape of a summary of the report of the Vie. Govern-
ment inspectors who lately overhauled the society’s
affairs. Of the many conclusions at which the offi-
cials arrived these three would, standing by them-
selves, be sufficient to make many a policy-holder
spend a happier Christmas this year than he did
last, when wild rumors about the office were still in
circulation: —
“(1) We are satisfied that, at the date of our
investigation, the Society was solvent and able to
meet all its liabilities, both immediate and prospec-
tive.”
“(2) We are strongly of opinion that the charges
of fraud, dishonesty, mismanagement and incapacity
made against the directors and managers in the
petition presented to Parliament in September last
are wholly unjustified.”
“(10) We are of opinion that the business is now
being conducted on sound principles, that the policy
which is being pursued should have the effect of
further reducing the business costs, and that, if the
confidence of the public is retained, the results in the
future should be materially better than they have
been in past years.”
British Consols, at £7l 55., are lower than
any living man has ever known them to be before.
At that price they give the investor a penny or two
over 3i p.c. ; so the Treasurer who expects cheap
money on any “Colonial” security is in for a disap-
pointment. In ordinary circumstances, such a price
would suggest that Britain was on the verge of a
gruelling war. But present circumstances are not
ordinary. The drain of capital has recently been
tremendous; and, in addition, the increase in the
world’s output of gold has been so huge that the
metal has become cheaper, which means that you
have to pay more of it to buy a given quantity of
goods. Hence the increase in the cost of living,
which has been followed, naturally enough, by an in-
crease in the wages, not only of labor, but also of
capital. We can no more expect to get capital at
the old rate than we can expect to get carpenters—
or members of Parliament.
“Y. & A.”: At last a State Government
has recognised its obvious duty to encourage the
search for petroleum in Australia, the only un-
proved oil-country on earth. The South Aus. Govern-
ment has offered a bonus of £lOOO to the first per-
son or co. to obtain from a bore or well in the
State 100,000 gallons of crude petroleum containing
not less than 90 p.c. of products obtainable by dis-
tillation under certain conditions to be observed
by claimants. What is meant by the cryptic 90 p.c.
proviso will probably be understandable when the
conditions of the subsidy are gazetted. With its
vast belts of tertiary rocks in its western division
and its unparalleled deposits of kerosene shale in
the east, N.S.W. should go and do likewise by offer-
ing a bonus for petroleum produced from a well.
The kerosene shales of N.S.W. will ultimately be
drilled through, and crude petroleum will very likely
be found in the porous oil sands far below the oily
shales.
Spelter, though still nearly £5 cheaper
than it was 12 months ago, recovered 10s. last week
—the result, no doubt, of the renewal of what is
known as the Zinc Convention for two years. The
“convention” is a combine of all the principal zinc
smelters of Europe to regulate the output and con-
sequently the price; and the methods by which this
is done were indicated lately when some tens of
thousands of tons of rich zinc concentrates were left
for months in stacks along the Broken Hill line.
Earlier in the year it looked as though
there might be industrial trouble at Broken Hill
while 1914 was an infant; but the men—especially
those on contract—are making such rattling good
wages (anything up to 18s. or 20s. per shift) that no
disturbance is now apprehended.
Apart from the gamble in the Carpathia
squibs and for that the public has to thank the
Sydney daily papers, which allowed themselves to be
used so disgracefully—Great Cobar was the disaster
of the year in the mining market. Opening at a
little under par (£5), the finish was at 15s. 6d. —
with every prospect of a still further shrinkage, in
spite of the arrival of 8., M. and Co. on the scene
as general managers. Other copper stocks generally
are weaker, having followed the metal market down-
wards ; but a steady lead market has kept most
Broken Hill stocks steady. In the case of the North
there has, in fact, been a well-deserved rise.
The heavy metal for the B. H. Prop.’s
new steel works at Newcastle is now coming to
hand.
Occidental (N.S.W.) has full ore bins;
and 40 stampers are kept busy. There are 60 head,
and it is intended to put the lot in commission when
the sand pump is repaired. Assays from 4585 tons
hauled in eight weeks have been excellent, averaging
from 12dwt. to 21dwt. The last fortnightly lot was
1102 tons, worth 15dwt. 21gr. Included in that
were 408 tons of 30dwt. stuff. The long interval of
three years since the last dividend was paid, and the
uncertainty about profits from the deeper levels,
keep the share market depressed, in spite of good
yields. The Occidental is, the bears say, a patchy
show. But if the mine is big enough, that isn’t
very serious. For instance, the Waihi (M.L.) is
horribly patchy.
11. Woodward, of Parkes (N.S.W.), is
erecting a plant to treat 50,000 tons of slimes pur-
chased from the Myalls and Peak Hill Co. Wood-
ward, who has much experience in cyaniding in
N. S. Wales, is the enterprising person who worked
the low-grade stuff at the London mine, near Parkes,
for some years, merely crushing the ore with rolls to
about the size of peas, and then cyaniding it direct.
Ultimately values dropped, the workings reached the
sulphide zone, and the venture blew out; but the
operations proved the possibility of profitably rough-
crushing and cyaniding ore under favorable condi-
tions. It was, moreover, probably the first experi-
ment in direct cyaniding lode matter in Australia —
on a working scale, anyway.
Production of radium bromide is a pain-
fully slow and tedious process ; but the Radium Hill
Co. has got far enough on to now promise a continu-
ous output of standardised product. The first lot
available was 40 milligrammes, which found local
buyers ; but the price isn’t mentioned. Bowie Wilson
lately overhauled things at the co.’s mine in S. Aus.,
and wasn’t enthusiastic; the concentrating plant is
badly designed, he says, and several of the leases are
not worth hanging on to.
The Great Pitzroy Co.’s Mount Chalmers
(Q.) still struggles for existence. During November
2123 tons of ore were treated showing 3.37 p.c.
copper, and 2.6odwt. gold, and the lot yielded
£15,687 (with copper at £67), while the working
expenditure ran to £15,979. This cannot go on for
ever ; but there is too much capital at stake to give
in without a stiff fight. Shortage of water is the
latest excuse for the poor showing.
* * * *
Mammoth (Q.) reports unexpectedly
striking a copper lode in the Chieftain mine, at the
230 ft. level. The main lode has been followed in
the winze from the 160 ft. level. At 33ft. this
winze carries 2ft. of ore assaying 15 p.c., with low-
grade ore extending into the hanging wall. The
south level is now in 461 ft. from the crosscut at
the 160 ft. level. They are through the richest por-
tion of the shoot. Another mine, the Clansman,
reports assays of 20 p.c. copper, lOoz. silver, 15dwt.
gold from below the 80ft. level. The 15dwt. gold
per ton is very choice.
Mount Morgan has had a fire, and some of
the month’s copper and gold is still in matte that
the plant has been unable to treat. Mount Lyell,
warned by a similar disaster, is installing allegedly
fire-proof power houses, and it is up to Mount Mor-
gan to do likewise. Morgan has produced 3897 tons
of copper in 24 weeks. The revenue, taking the
base metal at £6O, was £457,643 ( £233,820 for
copper and £223,823 for gold)—a handsome £57
Bs. 7d. worth of gold per ton of copper. But the
market for Morgans closes rather weak, and would
be still weaker if the Baillieu-Robinson syndicate
wasn’t standing behind the stock.
Gympie (Q.) is still hopeful of striking a
third bed of slate beneath the conglomerate. Monk-
land and Inglewood will sink to 1650 ft. in the
expectation of cutting the Great Eastern reef. It
may be easy enough to reckon at what depth the reef
should be met with, but the position and thickness of a
gold-bearing bed of slate are a gamble. The South
Glanmire and Monkland is working a conglomerate
formation at the 990 ft. level, with floating slate
floors carrying leaders; 20 head of stampers are
employed during the day shift. The East Oriental
and Glanmire has been assisted by a Government
subsidy. They are down 428 ft. below the 2154 ft.
level, and are hopeful of meeting with a reef. But,
in general, the field is exceedingly depressed.
Brilliant Deeps, Charters Towers, comes
along with a crushing worth £5 7s. 9d. per ton
( £2184), following £3 9s. 6d. per ton in Novem-
ber. The two lots together averaged about £4 ss.
from 998 tons. A close clean-up at the mill
probablv accounts for the sudden improvement in
the yield. The cutting of a water-catchment in the
underlay shaft below No. 6 level exposed 2ft. of good
stone. Ten head of stampers have been kept going,
but profits have been small and quite insufficient to
feed 150,000 shares. Under improved conditions with
regard to ventilation, the Deeps should give a better
account of itself.
Victory. Charters Towers, hands out a
threepenny div. The aggregate return for 10 months
was £13.584 from 1560 tons, and one or two other
payable little lots have come in since then.
No other Government than the Tasmanian
could have agreed to such a one-sided agreement as
the Balfour railway contract discloses. The co. is
to try to build the railway, but if it fails to com-
plete the job the Government must take over the
work and pay to the co. all the money spent on it.
At latest the Government had given the co. a further
two years to complete, and as the co. has already
spent £40,000, which it must be recouped in case
of failure, the Balfour shareholders will have some-
thing substantial to draw, anyhow. The co. holds
the thick end and the middle of the stick; and the
Government is such a boob that it isn’t likely to
hold even the thin end for long.
The quantity of ore sent along to the
furnaces indicates a bumper Christmas return for
Mount Lyell, which has got so far back to normal
that general-manager Sticht is going to the other
side of the world for a long holiday.
Ivulamadau (Woodlark Island) faces a
2d. call. Reports have mentioned small profits from
time to time ; the last £62 came from a run of six
weeks. Such small profits don’t pay administra-
tion, bank interest and sundry expenses. This
Papuan show has undoubtedly a big ore body, but
working costs—23s. per ton—about make a tie of it
with the gold recovered.
Maoriland’s famous old Waihi paid its
quarterly shilling this month. There are now
73,053 shares on the colonial register, as compared
with 64,918 in September. This increase of 8135
shares indicates returning confidence at this end, and
the share market shows a big rise over slump prices.
Tongkah Compound, the remarkable patch
of tin ground at the head of the Siamese Harbor
which the Tongkah Co. is dredging, gave a profit
of £35,600 during the half-year; and, accumula-
tions having been drawn upon, £40,000 was paid
in dividends on the 50,000 £1 shares. Including the
amount payable on Dec. 19, the shareholders will
have received 395. per share, or in all £97,500, in
18 months. And the co. will still have £B6OO in
hand, plus the value of the December output. It is
magnificent, and it would be still more magnificent
if it would last. But, apart from the slump in the
tin market —the quotation has fallen £5O per ton in
a year—the ground held originally by the co. is
only enough to keep the co. going for about five years,
and additional purchases have given it a life of but
12 months’ more. The new ground has been well
bored and proved to carry 1.691 b. tin oxide —very fine,
but still a good way short of the 2.541 b. averaged dur-
ing the past six months. Lately, since the half-year
closed, the dredge has been on even better stuff,
averaging just under 31b. The percentage of profit
is dazzling: after paying the Government royalty, the
product netted 32.57 d. per cubic yard, while costs,
though higher than in previous terms, were 6.08 d.
Future profits depend on the price of tin; and, though
they will no doubt be excellent while they last, the
slump in the market will lick off a lot of the cream.
Deebook is Captain Miles’s “baby,” now
that he has dropped the Tongkah, of which he was
also the father. Mostly Deebook is a Miles family
affair, which, like the Tongkah, hopes to scratch a
fortune out of the Malay Peninsula. The dredge
with which it proposes to make a first attack on its
350 acres was shipped from Victoria last week, so
there has bfeen no dawdling so far about the business.

while in N. S. Wales so much new ground has been
put und«i the plough—largely through the opera-
tion of tne Commonwealth Labor party’s land tax—
that under average conditions the Ma State’s harvest
is now twice as great as that of its western neigh-
bor. An estimate of the N.S.W crop now being cut
makes it worth approximately £6,800,000; 'Vic-
toria’s, £5,800,000; S. Australia’s, £3,150,000;
Westralia’s, £2,250,000. This is net, after paying
or allowing for railway freight.
“Tawonga”: Pirate Enterprise, nosing
round for concessions, is casting glances at the water-
sheds of the Kiewa, Ovens and Mitta rivers (Vic.). A
flying survey has been made of the Upper Kiewa, which
has such a fall that generating stations could be
erected, one above the other, for miles. At Tawonga,
at a low estimate, the water supply would be 60ft.
wide and Ift. deep, with a flow of from four to five
miles per hour ; the fall is about 100 ft. to the mile,
and higher up it is more. Melbourne is only about
200 miles away.
“Youth and Asia”: Beachport (S.A.) is
another neglected port which has become decadent
through the insane S. Aus. policy of dragging everything
to Adelaide by rail instead of allowing the primary
products to find their way to their natural ports.
Shortly after the Mount Gambier to Beachport rail-
way was opened the Mount Gambier Shipping Co.
was established to take the wool, potatoes, wheat and
barley grown in the district to Adelaide or Mel-
bourne or London. A little too much went to Mel-
bourne to please the S.A. Railway Department, so
the railway was extended from Mount Gambier to
Narracoorte, and connected with the trunk line.
After that Beachport languished, and the shipping
co. was forced to disband. The harbor has 50ft. of
water at its entrance, and a natural channel to a
fine jetty only needs a little dredging to make it
available to deep-sea steamers. But the accursed
policy of pulling all produce to the capital cities
lies like a dead hand upon Australia, and prevents
Beachport becoming a live, populous seaport. Its
destiny to be the natural outlet of the rich S.E.
district of S.A. will some day be fulfilled, yet there
must be an entire change of policy, which at present
seems as unlikely as that George Reid will be able
to crawl through the eye of a needle into the King-
dom of Heaven.
“Lud” : Deeley, the buyer of the bloated Bed-
ford property in London, probably represents a Yankee
syndicate. The estate includes Covent Garden, which
was really its nucleus. “Covent” is a corruption of
“convent” ; Convent Garden was an open space north
of the Strand, belonging to St. Peter’s Abbey, West-
minster. After Henry VIII. had confiscated the con-
vent, the garden site remained vacant for a time,
and became a sort of Paddy’s Market, for vegetable
stalls, which stood up against the garden wall of
Bedford House, in the Strand. In that day there
were a few mansions on the north side, and many on
the south, with gardens to the Thames. The general
population was clustered in East London and West-
minster, the Strand being a road between them. In
1631 the Earl of Bedford put a quadrangle of build-
ings round his Paddy’s Market, but had craft enough
to preserve and encourage the stalls, which grew
into the present vast building's in 1831. The Bed-
fords came into the property through a bit of bad
luck which befel the Somersets. When Henry VIII.
confiscated Convent Garden he tossed it over to the
Duke of Somerset; and when the latter lost his head
for treason, Edward gave the property as a titbit
to the Earl of Bedford.
“Red Tape” : The life of the public servant
in N.S.W. has taken on a new terror. It is a tradition
handed down from the earliest days that you mustn’t
bustle the accounts branches of the public depart-
ments. In an endeavor to hasten the gradual account-
ants, the Stores Supply Committee on July 1 entered
into contracts containing a clause that the contractor
would allow a 24 per cent, discount if his bill was
paid within a month. When the departmental account-
ants got wind of the scheme they all voted it a good
idea, and gave the Stores Supplies Committee credit
for an awakening intelligence. Unfortunately the
scheme was forgotten in mid-career, with the result
that many of the departments are paying their July
and August accounts now'. Wherefore, to the horror
of all concerned, these delayed vouchers are coming
back from the Auditor-General with a curt intima-
tion that they have been paid too late to claim the
24 per cent, discount, and the remark is accom-
panied by a polite request that the officer responsible
should make the loss good within seven days.
Watt has taken the “Corner” bull by the
horns and thrown it out of his new Victorian
Ministry. The bull, which made most of the
trouble the other day, demanded two salaried
portfolios and two honorary jobs, and as
Watt wouldn’t stand that.much, the bull
would take nothing. The animal’s next
move will probably be to go back to its stall
and be disagreeable. Watt’s idea, of course,
is to whip all members into complete sub-
servience to one party or the other —in other
words, to make members pretend to endorse
things they honestly don’t endorse. Under
the party system Parliament is becoming a
place where it is useless to look for a man
with opinions of his own and the pluck to
stand by them. If the machine doesn’t man-
age to squeeze such a man out of the pre-
liminary ballot, and he gets into the House
by accident, he is the last person in the
world the party leader wants to see in the
Cabinet.

A MARTYR FOR YEARS.
“For years I was a martyr to liver complaint,”
writes Mrs. Corlett, 112 Clyde Street, Ballarat, Vic.
“Would get dreadful sick headaches and be unable to
eat. I consulted a doctor, but he was unable to do
me any good. Eventually I tried Chamberlain’s Tab-
lets, and derived such benefit from them that my
health is now perfect.”*

Sydney, December 2% i9i3.
My Dear Arini, —
Parliament opens early this week in a
subdued whisper. There won’t be a cocked
hat or lace frill present. Even the lady-
voter is too busy with the Christmas ham
to worry about anything that may happen
in Macquarie-street.
The average A.D.C. must be a lineal
descendant of the Wandering Jew, for he
rarely stays very long in one situation.
Governor Bosanquet, who retires from S’Aus-
tralia, doesn’t leave his young gentlemen to
walk behind his successor. Both Help
Walker and Help Fletcher are likely to
bustle off to their regiments, the Grenadier
and Scots’ Guards, unless someone falls on
their necks and implores them not
to desert Australia. The son of a Howard of
Glossop is in the running for Gover-
nor Strickland’s staff, though he is not
a certainty. The Glossop barony is just
old enough to be well aired, but the Fitzalan-
Howard who is coming our way, being di-
rectly descended from England’s premier
ducal family and the grandson of Queen
Victoria’s favorite Vice-Chamberlain, is a
rather terrifying Social circumstance.
New A.D.C. Robbins has hurried off to the
vice-regal cot, at Sutton Forest, to pay his
respects to the Stricklands, who now culti-
vate chickens and early rising on that salu-
brious hill top.
The Christmas gift—an annual nuisance
Which hits the perpetual giver very hard —
is stalking in our midst, and people are buy-
ing things they have no use for, in order to
pass them on to friends who are equally
short of house room for superfluous lumber.
The frantic Christmas shopper, after, a mad
pursuit of the articles she really wants, in-
variably gets home a week before the Feast
with a tram fare and both arms full of brown
paper parcels containing snares and delu-
sions she never meant to buy. And even
then she hasn’t bought everything, where-
fore she goes shopping again on Christmas
Eve, which is one of the dates whereon Adam
can consider himself lucky to be dead.
News drifts in to the effect that Melba is
still keeping her little home together with
the results of her American tour. In dollars
the sum is too vast to be handled without the
aid of a chartered accountant. In mere
sterling it works out at £50,000 —the lowest
figure. A mere £40,000 was actually guar-
anteed as the Australian skylark’s share of
a trip in which Kubelik’s fiddle carried off
another big whack of dollars. In N’York
the Hippodrome held £2020 at a concert on
Sunday evening, November 2. Mrs. Melba
sent a Sydney friend a photo of the house
from the booking-office point of view. Seats
and their prices were arranged with the pre-
cision of cabbages, and the fat proceeds were
gathered together in figures for the camera.
To the guaranteed £40,000 an extra £lO.OOO
was added as percentage profit. A N’York
music-machine man, writing here, says that
Melba is singing even better than she did
five or six years ago, and her ’phone records
lick creation.” After this, “decadent” Aus-
tralia can lean back and wonder whether its
climate hasn’t produced the only imperish-
able voice of the age.
Still another yoice is trembling on the
horizon. This time it’s a lyric soprano,
aged 18 years, owned by Renfie Maxwell.
I m not sure about the way Renee likes to
spell her front name, but the other part is
plain sailing. Quinlan heard this voice
floating round the gallery at a pre-opera
performance in that lofty perch during the
now defunct season. He stamped upstairs
(it was about 7.45 p.m.), heard some more,
and went off and dropped the girl a word of
advice. The enterprising Thomas suggested
that the Voice should get into training for a
year or two, and then take a billet in his co.
He says it’s one of the lyric sopranos the
world hungers for. A benefit concert to
help the girl with her exes, to Europe is
likely to occur in March.
The Leo Cohen-Gladys Henriques wed-
ding the other evening started in the Syna-
gogue and finished up with a sit-down
supper in the home at Bellevue Hill. Who
says there are no housekeepers left in this
city of restaurants? Every course at the
supper-banquet was cooked on the premises
in the family kitchen; and an epicure de-
clares that he hasn’t over-eaten himself for
years with so much pleasure as he did at
the Enidor wedding feast. The bride’s
beautiful dress just missed the prevalent fish-
train by an inch or two. It was draped in
some foamy white material, over the fash-
ionable blush of faint pink which is the last
word in 1913 bridal plumage. Her bouquet,
shaped like a fan, was a lovely trifle, with
a frivolous lace background. The bride
flung this symbol of matrimony at a bevy of
girl pals. I fancy Miss Beattie emerged
triumphant out of the, scrimmage. The
troom, who. is a son of Df. A. A. Cohen, of
>arling Point, built a ptetty dove-Cot for his
bride on Bellevue, as I told you before, and
thoughtfully settled it On his best gifl as a
wedding present;
Miss Kitty tidy, on 6 of the sheeff-reffring
family whose flocks feed on the sffnburnt
pastures out Warren way, has said “Yes” to
a Vitginian who lives in Ugahdd. This is
as near to the facts of the cdse ds a vaghe
acquaintance now gadding in Europe could
get. Miss Kitty, who has been wandering
in other countries, met her fate on her
travels.
Fiddler Cyril Monk and Miss Petersen
(Isabel Varney) were quietly wed in St.
Philip’s Church this week. No young lady
under a mosquito net veil, no top hats, no
snorting procession of petrol cars were in-
cluded in this knot-tying. So the Voice
that breathed o’er Eden was not stifled with
confetti.
_ Miss Kater, a daughter of the late Edward
Kater, of Mumblebone station, Warren, is
going to marry Frank Crawford, and settle
down to domestic duties in England. The
groom-to-be has something to do with the
laying down of cables to the uttermost ends
of the earth. The good-looking Minnie and
her mother have been staying on the other
side for a while.
Mrs. John McCormack and her sister,
the gentle Miss Foley, are still petting the
two babes of the family at Rose Bay. They
depart in the Niagara on January 12, with a
ton or two of baggage, and a retinue of
maids, and gather up John and his com-
pany in Auckland.
The majestic Butt and her Kennerley
Rumford are back in Sydney, collecting any
loose cash that John McCormack forgot to
bank.
The veteran singer Nordica and her
party, the Panama Commissioners, and a
well-known Java tea planter named Neu-
mann went off in the Tasman. “Who’s
Who” (the brute!) puts Nordica’s birth-
day in the year 1850, but you needn’t believe
it. I don’t!
■Bertie Adams, a N.S.W. girl, is touring
John Bull’s provinces with Huntley Wright’s
co. in musical comedy. She is a daughter of
the Dr. Adams whose stethoscope used to
take soundings up Chatswood way. The
Adams family moved on to the United States,
but soprano Bertie, a former pupil of Mrs.
Slapoffski, is doing steady business in the
land of the British drizzle. (Do you know
that London’s actual rainfall is just half of
ours, and only a pointer two more than
droughty Adelaide’s?)
J. and N. Tait have engaged the Kennedy
family—the N.S.W. musical team which is
just back from a world tour —for Christmas
week in Melbourne. The soprano will be
Dorothy Grfce.
Oswald Anderson, a promising song writer
from this town, is earning buttered crusts as
pianist to Peter Dawson on the other side.
Peter is the young Adelaide singer who is
perambulating Britain with considerable
success. He and Ada Crossley warble the
ditties written by Anderson, and find loud
demands for more awaiting them.
“Goody” Goodman, whose presence was
missed at the front door of Her Majesty’s
during the opera season, is back in his old
stand. He took half a holiday at the Royal
while “The Ring” and other long-winded ar-
guments were clamoring for a hearing at the
Pitt-street theatre.
The English Revue girls are fine, strapping
young women, with no unduly humble ideas
about their place in creation’s hymn. Writ-
ing before seeing their highnesses in war-
paint, I may hint that no dazzling beauties
threaten to rob .our girls of their prizes, if
they’ll hold their chins well up in the haughty
London Revue style.
Conductor Wynne Jones has written most
of the chorus music for “Aladdin,” who fLds
his lamp at the Adelphi Theatre this Christ-
mas. It is said to be a melodious back-
ground for the book —the latter the work of
A. E. Martin, a well-known Australian
pressman and theatrical promoter. Mabel
Batchelor’s trills will decorate the ad-
ventures of the Principal Girl; Grace
Palotta’s cherry-colored tights will envelop
second-boy Koko; and Carrie Moore will
skip back to her old place as Principal Boy.
Josephine Davis, the brown-eyed Tivoli
charmer, who is just out from ’Murka, is a
character artist with a voice and some frocks
you’d like to take away while the owner
wasn’t looking. The Wild Life Preservation
Society may lie in wait for the new arrival
and forcibly remove a few yards of osprey,
and some bird-o’-paradise trophies, otherwise
they’ll waggle in the air while the Voice
gets through its business. Lucille, the Duff-
Gordon dame, who very nearly went to the
bottom of the sea when the Titanic found-
ered, still turns pale, Miss Davis says, if the
conversation swings off hooks and eyes to ice-
bergs. She is responsible for some of
Josephine’s frocking. Redfern, of Paris,
looks after some more. All these gowns
show the slim, silhouette effect, the film of
lace over the shoulders and the cunning
drapery of the French skirts. A black gown,
like a slice of midnight starred with bril-
liantSj has the Turkish trouser skirt, cut
to show a pair of feet that fill a child’s size
in shoes; A cloth-of-silver robe, with a pale
pink flush-o’-dawn ground, is held together by
one hook and a half and a foam of lace, de-
signed in cobweb pattern. This is rather
finer than the web the confiding spider hangs
on your picture frames every Sunday morn-
ing. Emerald green clouds of drapery, and
garlands of tiny blhsh-pink roses set in
leaves of gold gauze, are details no daughter
of Eve should fail to copy; A blue gown
hung with these baby blooms is a French
model that would turn the head of St. An-
thony.
Woman, according to a French writer,
spends her time (he is not sure about her
eternity) making up her mind whether she
will simulate a folded umbrella or a fire-bell.
Just now the umbrella has the upper hand
Still, even the umbrella vogue has its ad-
vantages over the Greek costume, which has
also made a re-appearance. The umbrella
lady is at least tidy on a windy day,
whereas the lady of Athens is liable to wear-
some of her drapery over her head when a
sudden gusty breeze whisks round her san-
dals.
If the recent Teachers’ Conference had
sharpened any small hatchets, it buried them
all in a harbor launch-party at the end of
the confab. Some of the State-school marms
murmur at the tendency to cram the young
child with more “vittles” than it can digest,
while plain English grows cold as a small
side-dish. But there is a worse grievance
even than that —a grievance which declares
itself in a hand held out for equal pay for
equal work. Some day the hurt feelings of
Miss Pedagogue, who draws less cash than
her male colleague in exactly the same grade
of service, may be soothed by the balm she
clamors for. At present she is in receipt
only of that less marketable commodity, “De-
partmental sympathy.”
Miss Dumolo, the youthful-looking princi-
pal of the Kindergarten Training College,
waded waist-deep in statistics concerning the
efforts of the past year. The portentous
wedge of information having been handed
over to the company that met in St. James’s
Hall the other evening, Under-Secretary
Peter Board srave the successful students
their certificates. Any uncertificated per-
son or non-prizewinner at the break-up of
1913 is almost as rare as a bunyip.
The Sydney Grammar School break-up
was nicely talked at by Governor Strick-
land, and permeated by the ’Varsity Mac-
Callums and many other proud parents, who
hurried along to see their offspring walk off
with the usual December clutch of prizes.
Young MacCallum, a tall youth given to
study, was heavily laden with rewards of
industry. A thrifty dame was so much
struck with the fact that the boy has quite
outgrown his male parent, the learned little
Professor, that she whispered that the son’s
nether garments would cut down beautifully
for father. This habit of looking at
trousers with a pair of scissors is peculiar
to the Good Mother species which flocks to
these affairs. Headmaster Sloman —a vet-
eran of 29 —made a delicate allusion to his
own leanings towards science and history;
he is not nearly so rabid about classics as
the older school of pedagogues. Hence he is
likely to have his youth flung in his face
by the Greek and Latin antiquities.
Little Jack Lindsay, who won the Bowen
House scholarship in Brisbane the other
day, is the eldest of artist Norman Lindsay’s
three bright youngsters. Brains seem to be
a habit in this family. The precocious
scholarship-winner can now help himself to
three years’ tuition at Brisbane Grammar
School, and demand, free of cost, any litera-
ture he may require on the passage.
The Austral Orchestral College has added
the wail of its fiddles to the breaking-up
choirs which always howl on the verge of
December 25. Alfred Hill and Downs John-
stone were in charge of the teachers and
students’ rally in the Repertory Theatre on
December 19.
The petticoat portion of the G. H. Reid
party has been sniffing the sea breezes in
the roomy hotel at Clifton Gardens.
The Justice Sly family is booked for
flight some time in March. They will move
their Saratogas over the most attractive
scenery in Europe.
Mrs. M. Forrest, the Queensland writer,
who belongs to the ancient Mills family of
Parramatta, has wiped her pen for a brief
holiday. She will eat her Christmas pud-
ding hereabouts.
The Christmas turkey scrimmage is driving
numbers of usually-settled Sydney folks off
to mountains or seaside. Mrs. Steer Bowker,
whose health hasn’t been up to its old bril-
liant form of late, meditates a trip to her
married daughter’s up-country homestead.
Mrs. Jim Gordon, as you know, used to be
Gladys Bowker. Pretty Marjorie and blue-
eyed Barbara are invoiced for the same
journey if their mother’s health gets no
worse.
Mrs. Martin Donohoe, who lingers here
till March, is still camping at The Gunyah
with her sister, Mrs. Clunies-Ross, the slen-
der Ashfield dame who looks like a minia-
ture by Bess Norris. Journalist-husband
Martin, possibly in search of more of those
war _ alarms in which he specialises,
hurried off in the Orama.
The fashion of one parent more or less
surprising the other with a portrait of the
finest children on earth is taking root here
The G.-Gr. gave it a kind of kick-off in Mel-
bourne with the “great secret” the Power
portrait of the Denman youngsters, which
was waiting to welcome her Ex. when she
tripped back from England. llow much in
the dark. the parent who is on the spot
actually is, when one or two juveniles are
getting their hair curled to sit before
an easel about twice a week, the parent
never mentions. For the last month or so
the Macneill Simpson household held its
breath and tiptoed over its prodigious
secret. The small, blue-eyed maiden of the
house was sitting to Florence Rodway for
her portrait—and there was much hair-
tying and blue ribbon in consequence. Last
week, when Dr. Mac had a birthday, the
great secret, his little daughter’s picture, a
present from her fond mamma, was handed
over to her more or less astounded papa.
Mrs. Harry Rickards, who came back
from Britain some time ago, is going
to be in her old haunt in the Town Hall
basement on Christmas Day. The annual
Rickards feast to the poor—or 1000 of
them—will occur at noon.
Miss Marcia Brace, the English horticul-
tural lady who, two years ago, bought a
property at Yarraford, now let on a lease,
has gone off to Maoriland on a holiday jaunt!
She departed in the Makura with the Quin-
lan Opera Co. and the McCormack party,
after many attempts to sail the seas during
the now-dead strike. Even the Canadian
mail-boat didn’t get away to time, and pas-
sengers hung over the sides for the best part
of a day, wondering if they could diagnose a
crew on the horizon.
A returned wanderer who knows the lead-
ing cities of Europe fairly well suggests
that it would pay Sydney to send
some of its aldermen to inspect Berlin. There
the civic management has reached a crisis of
cleanliness that would almost stun the casual
person who scatters dirty papers and breaks
every municipal bylaw all over our parks and
streets. The German frau has not a voice of
any consequence in the civic or political life
of her country; but even without her help
Berlin is so clean you could eat off its pave-
ments. “Australian women who have now
the weapon of the vote,” says Mrs. Sydney-
sider, ‘‘ought to use it better, and get into
the active management of civic affairs. No-
thing is more disappointing to a returned
Australian, after many years’ absence from
her native land, than to note the apathy of
women in regard to the municipal affairs of
their lovely but untidy city.”
Baker’s Stadium is going to house a huge
athletic event on January 7. The proceeds
go to the Royal Hospital for Women (the
largest in the State to deal only with the
woes of one sex') and the Renwick Hospital
for Infants. Both institutions are within
the fold of the centenarian Benevolent So-
ciety, which will reward R. L. Baker with a
shrill pipe of thanks.
Henceforth the Nightingales at St. Vin-
cent’s Hospital will have large and airy
premises in which to spend their short hours
of ease. The new Nurses’ Home is built in
the Spanish fashion, round a garden square
or patio. The rooms are bright and cheery
havens for weary limbs, and fresh air pre-
vails. St. Vincent’s has always dealt more
tenderly than most big hospitals with its
staff, but considerations of space used to
block the way against the little luxuries
now supplied by the new Home. The old
hole-and-corner methods which some of our
big Australian hospital committees once
considered good enough for nurses, whose
hours usually range from 12 to 14 out of
every 24. are dying hard ; still, the end is in
sight. The spacious Nurses’ Home in St.
Vincent’s grounds was officially opened on
Sunday afternoon.
There is nothing new under the moon.
Even the tango—which is going to obsess
the next dancing season here, if the English
tangomania be a safe forecast —is not new:
it is primitive barbarity, slightly diluted
with ragtime. It is the apotheosis of all that
is grotesque—the modern caricature of the
rhythmic dance movement. As already seen
here, it is a cross between Turkey trot and
Bunny Hug. The dancers give way sud-
denly at the knees about every three sec-
onds, while the coat tails of the male part-
ners fly out like sails, and the lady’s bolster
skirt strains at its moorings. After threaten-
ing to sit down suddenly every other minute,
the pair rush madly round locked in a fierce
embrace which makes the Apache method
look like a mild drawing-room entertainment.
The Sun’s London correspondent speaks
of “our old friend Wallingford” in attend-
ance on Queen Mary. So ephemeral is fame
that even the suave smile of Better-not Wal-
lington is now confused with the airy persi-
flage of Get-Rich-Quick Wallingford.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, December 22, 1913.
My Dear Yarrie, —
The city is very Christmassy, and hot citi-
zens are swearing weakly outside-shop win-
dows in which imaginative assistants have
stuck fat images of Father Chimney grin-
ning like an unseasonable lunatic in red
flannel and cotton-wool. The streets are
cumbered with bare-legged youngsters, the
sorrowful wheeze of the tin trumpet is heard
through the traffic noises, and house-
Avives are busily preparing to overfeed all
hands with hot, spiced food suitable for a
bleak July.
The Denman dame —a persistent hunter
of antiques—is going to have a show of art
treasures at Government House. The Very
Best People are lending their treasured
“pieces” to spread alongside the Denman
hoard on a date which is not fixed yet.
Lady Denman handled the Repertory
Theatre dance meeting on the 15th like an
expert in such matters She put the high-
brows through their evolutions, and nailed
down necessary points with precision at the
right moment. During the costume discus-
sion, Cornish, of the Melbourne Club, held
up the traffic while he made a few unneces-
sary jokes. He was at once pulled up by
the businesslike chairwoman, who adroitly
turned the laugh against him. She dispensed
tact throughout the meeting, and earned the
grateful applause of those who wanted to
get things done. One man suggested tab-
leaux such as he had seen at a big charity
ball in Paris, run by actors and actresses.
All the costumes were from stage tableaux,
and, as the pictures broke up, those taking
part joined the onlookers in the hall. It
looks as if it would be an improvement on
the usual procession.
The Repertory Co. has had no fixed address
thus far. Its literary drama has happened
at various places—the Athenaeum Hall, as a
rule—and the culchawed few have followed it
around town. Given a permanent abode it
might tempt also the Goths to drop in some-
times to see what was doing.
The Denman dame and the Anglican arch-
bishop, both dressed in perfectly lovely
clothes, recently dispensed prizes, smiles and
good advice to the Merton Hall students.
Merton Hall is the Church of England
Grammar School for Young Ladies, and the
Tone of it is tremendous. The Town Hall,
where the solemnities occurred, was filled
with party frocks and pigtails : and the in-
fants they were attached to gushed prettily
just as their mothers did before them, and as
their daughters will do after them. The
wretched flat hat and the expectant smile of
the Church were also to be seen on every
hand. The children were hustled out and
homeward in chattering flocks at 9.30.
The Denman dame then mounted to the
upper regions, where Mayoress Hennessy, as
president of the Alliance Frangaise, was
giving a party in French. (No, my dear,
not in Choctaw—in French!) Whereas Mer-
ton Hall “broke up” in the Town Hall, the
Alliance people reckoned they were in
“L’Hotel de Ville.” The place blushed
vividly in scarlet carnations, and the Council
Chamber was pink with rose-decked supper
tables, at which the Best Fed took their
nourishment, while the crowd hustled for
refreshments at the buffet in the portico bal-
cony. Her Ex.’s French was good; but,
then, she probably paid a lot for it. She
wore a tiara above an apricot-colored gown
and a collar of black velvet and pearls. John
Madden, seated impressively at the special
sup Der table, conversed in Franco-Irish, or
something of the kind, till he was purple
with exertion. His wife inhabited a neu-
tral-tinted gown and was quiet; but Mrs.
Hennessy, in pink, talked at phonograph
speed. A Hennessy nephew, Irish right
through, wandered about disconsolately. Mrs.
Liet was a conspicuous figure in black ; and
local Help Crespin took cover behind a
mound of strawberries and resolutely re-
fused to converse in anything but plain Aus-
tralian. Guy Madden evidently deserted in
the face of the enemy, for he was not sighted
at all. After supper, music saved the situa-
tion for those who didn’t know. There was
less conversation than usual while the music
was being put through. Kirby, of the fero-
cious lip-whiskers, and Lovie Cadorin sang;
Beryl McAdem, the amateur fiddler, sawed
off an item or two; and Rene Vanderkellen
talked from the platform.
Clara Butt is a poor sailor, but she reck-
oned it well worth while to brave the horrors
of Bass Strait for a clear profit of £6OO for
two nights of song in Launceston and Hobart.
Government House was put at the Butts’
disposal in Hobart. From Hobart and its
stacked apple-cases (artist Hal Gye has a
nightmarish impression of them in the holi-
day book he did with a brother-in-ink) Clara
and her Kennerley motored back to Laun-
ceston. shipped across to Melbourne, and
trained on to Sydney, lingering for one night
en route at the Macedon home of the Arthur
Baillieu pair.
The Butts will leave some of their con-
cert people behind them when they clear
from Sydney. Advance-man Roland Foster
finds he cannot tear himself away from Aus-
tralia, and intends to settle down and teach
in Sydney. Frank Carter, violinist, after
much wandering in strange places, will also
sit down in his native city and wield a
skilled bow within calling distance of artist
Norman.
Last Wednesday night the Opera House
stalls and circle were dotted o’er with smart
“sports,” male and female, and the air was
heavy with expectation. One of the boys
had bet Miss O’Hara a tenner to a goose-
berry that she wasn’t game to go on the
stage and perpetrate a ragtime ditty in Gene
Greene’s prize competition. The sporty man
(Doolette) had to part up. The surgeon’s
dauntless daughter rose to the occasion and
warbled her ragtime lay in a small, tuneful
voice. Afterwards she filled one of the chairs
on the stage along with a dozen nervous
young men who were taking the job seri-
ously. In her white serge outfit and yellow
thatch the lady faced the house until the
time came for the contestants to line up and
receive their respective meeds of applause
from the audience. Then somebody whis-
pered her and she retired into the “wings”
shrugging her shoulders. Afterwards she
sat with her pals in the stalls to see
the second part of the show. Stage fright
doesn’t run in Miss O’Hara’s family. Her
dashing surgical dad, just now in England,
used to let his manly baritone go at Melb.
Town Hall concerts occasionally. Tradition
credits him with having been an ideal Cap-
tain Corcoran in amateur “Pinafore” per-
formances. And brother Clive is the hand-
somest conjurer in the business. He started
a - a magician under Rickards management,
years ago, when his hands were slicker than
his tongue. Nowadays he figures adorably
at the halls in London and thereabouts as
Henry Clive, and drawing-room patter is his
speciality. So the O’Hara colleen was only
following in father’s footsteps and walking
in her brother’s way.
St. Kilda’is doing its official darnedest to
be wicked or “Continental” with a munici-
pal dance-liall; and a number of people are
doing their best to save it from sliding into
the Pit. Amid the racket, the promoter can
be heard writing to the papers that he claims
to have as much decent feeling as the people
who are against him and his “Palais de
Danse.” The name did it. Even before the
foundations were finished a decorous depu-
tation, accompanied by umbrellas and Vida
Goldstein, had clamored on the mat of the
seaside council; and since then the noise has
merely increased. Pending the start of the
actual infamy, the pier flappers and their
boys are getting quite a lot of entertainment
out of the written wails of “Father of Ten,”
“A Mother” and other apprehensive citizens
who seem to think the pen is mightier than
the Lord.
Mrs. Guyon Purclias, who has a first-
cousinly claim on Melba, enlivened the dog
days somewhat by giving a party to the
most important Arts and Crafts workers.
You can imagine just how technical the
meeting became when the invited had shaken
down. The grand old subject of crochet
was debated from all angles, while poker-
work was argued in three volumes. The
Crochet Guild president—one of the hand-
some Miller dames—had given the job up in
despair, and a desperate party of plotters
lurked in a corner and made efforts, while
the tea was poured, to reconstruct. The
younger guests played croquet on the lawn
to the music of a string band which twanged
prettily under an old-fashioned rose pergola.
(Oh, I forgot to mention it! The party was
held at Fairlie Flats.) Mrs. Purchas, look-
ing as much like a fashion-plate as possible,
drew her guests into a cool, shady lounge,
where Miss Ingram drafted them carefully
into a pleasant foodery behind closed shut-
ters. The men help the Arts and Crafts a
lot, but only three heroes ventured to the
tea party; Montgomery, president of
the Artists’ Society, was one of them.
Mary Cholmondeley was in the vortex, of
course. Miss McGeorge, who is running the
A. & C. Depot, which is to bring more money
into the concern, rotated close by. At pre-
sent, the A. & C. has only subscriptions to
feed on ; and subscriptions are an uncertain
diet, though the impetus given by the Carmi-
chael and Northcote dames has helped it to
keep going for five years. Mrs. Ernest White,
a Geelong woman who has promoted herself
to Toorak, divested herself of a few ideas
which lnok valuable. The social butterfly
was conspicuously absent, though the func-
tion was well strained in a social sense. Evi-
dently there are many Earnest Women
amongst _ the first flight. Some of them
looked with stiff-kneed horror at the rugs and
cushions spread invitingly on the lawns, and
didn’t relax until maids hurried along with
chairs. I don’t know whether their sense of
propriety kept them upright, or whether it
was just their corsets.
Mrs. Charles Tait gave a party last Fri-
da v at Gnaroo —the quaint house without a
chimney that baffles Santa Claus and angers
him so that he kicks the stuffing out of his
reindeer costume. The guests absorbed tea
and cake in a regulation way, as if they
were fathomless. The object of the gather-
ing was to say “How d’ ye do?” to Mrs.
Jack Scot, a favorite sister of the numerous
Tait brethren, who has just returned from
foreign places. Various other returned voy-
agers hurried along to the festivity and
made appropriate noises. Of course
everybody noticed Mrs. Scot’s Paris
frock of silk, pink striped like a stick of
lolly, with a duck of a vest. Nowadays, a
vest is as important to a fashionable woman
as is the vast white chest-covering to a
successful alderman. Mrs. Charley Tait, the
hostess, struck an effective note with her
quaint panniers—black tulle fins sticking out
from her clinging blue crepe-silk dress.
Ex-widower Thompson, of the Riverina,
and his new bride are rounding off a Tas-
manian honeymoon with a week at the
theatres and the Oriental. Mrs. Thompson
is making it a sort of continuous dress
parade. She lived up to ljer reputation
as a striking froeker even on the turbulent
sea trip across the Straits. The cut of her
flannel deck-coat turned women green with
envy—or those who were not already green
with other emotions.
The Creswell dame, who has drifted back
into social affairs after a long spell of mourn-
ing, finds the whirl appalling after the quiet
of ordinary existence. The Melbourne
Society squaw is always in a fever; when
so-called gaieties slacken, there is some
charity stunt to work or tango for, or
a ticket-selling job, or a job of plain bri-
gandage. The Creswell lady, looking on at
the whirlpool with a calculating eye, has
decided to hold aloof from everything except
naval shows. Henceforward she is a special-
ist. She is off to her native Adelaide hills
for a cool breeze during the dog days.
Guy Innes, who for some years has been
wedded to his job of news editor of the Mel-
bourne Herald, found time to commit
bigamy, so to speak, the other day, when he
painlessly extracted the usual “I will” from
Miss Grey, one of Melbourne’s lady dentists,
who did not lose her femininity when she
grasped the forceps. Keith Murdoch, who
represents Sydney Sun in these parts, was
with difficulty induced to don the frock coat
of a shameless life for this occasion only,
and, as best man, was not the least imposing
part of the entertainment. Guy and his
bride are now honeymooning among the
orchards and historic tombstones of Tas-
mania.
Dr. Seymour Armstrong and his wife are
staying with Mr. Seymour Davis, a Mel-
bourne bank manager. The visitor is Brit-
ish Representative in snowy Labrador, and
can talk like a Pole-hunter about Esqui-
maux, frostbite and furs.
Lilian, the late “Dolly” Devlin’s daughter,
who some years since took a voice and in-
come to flourish in the Smoke, is about to
add a husband to her possessions. Albert
Rawlinson, son of a Cold Country vicar, is
the selected man.
Miss Tudehope, a Melbourne violinist of
some note, is shortly to be married to Dr.
Gibbs, one of the Collins-street specialists.
Harold Hunt, of the hundred waistcoats
and the three-volume motor, is off to America
to an accompaniment of matrimonial whis-
pers. Hunt bounded through America once
before with a tennis racquet, and did deadly
execution amongst the girls.
Goll, the dreamy, Chopinesque pianist, is
taking on an Australian job under a German
name at Melbourne Conservatorium. He
steps into the shoes of Scharf, who moves up
higher to help fill the Marshall Hall niche.
Marshall is not returning to this country.
A new feeding place has been thrown or
dashed open in the new premises of a city
store, and all the people who are curious
about such things are running to find out if
the tea is good and the girls pretty. Every-
thing is painfully new at present, and there
isn’t one cup with edges like a sawblade. The
spoons are so bright that you don’t need a
purse-mirror, and an odor of romance clings
to at least two of the tea-maids. One of the
pair is alleged to belong to one of the best
English families. She is beautiful and pen-
sive and has a moated-grange look. I once
knew a girl-cashier in a pork shop who had
just the same look, but this one may be
the genuine article. And now I come to
think of it, the other may have been genuine,
too.
Steaming records have been going over-
board along the coast. The speedy Howard
Smith boat Canberra raced round to Mel-
bourne from Sydney in 32 hours 57 minutes.
The Mcllwraith boat Katoomba smoked
close behind and swept past Gellibrand
light 38 minutes later; and as the
Canberra had been given a start, the differ-
ence in actual steaming times was only three
minutes. The Canberra went into dock here
and had a crop of marine rubbish scraped
off, so the captain reckons that he can now
cut an hour off his ship’s time. But life is
full of disappointments, and when he first
tried to do the clipping he ran into a heavy
current at Green Cape, and couldn’t manage
anything better than 35 hours and some odd
minutes.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
Henley-on-Torrens and the inter-collegiate cricket
were the chief items in the social dissipations of the
past week. The former occurred on Saturday on our
solitary river-lake; and in spite of a glaringly hot
day there was a kaleidoscopic crowd of sightseers
both afternoon and evening. House-boats and mar-
quees decorated the south bank, and there the City
Fathers, members of the Adelaide Club, the South
Australian Rowing Association and other notables
entertained their friends. White shoes and stockings
(especially the stockings) were the most conspicuous
features of feminine attire below the knee ; north of
that point, washing frocks, shady hats and parasols.
The Kookaburra Rowing Club (women only) captured
first prize, in both afternoon and evening competi-
tions, for a decorated boat. Mrs. Jack Lea, Mrs. G.
H. Dean, E. T. Smith, Miss Phillipps, Dr. and Mrs.
Symons, Mrs. Arthur Rymill, Miss Campbell, Miss
McMillan and Mr. Leslie Dean were amongst those
who shared the tea and shade available.
Professor Mitchell and his two small children have
gone off to Colombo, where they will put in the long
vacation. Mr. E. H. Bakewell and his sons and the
W. G. Hawkeses, of Koonoona, were 1
the same boat, but they were booked for London b>
The Inter-collegiate Cricket Match resulted in
easy win for the bluc-and-white team and drew "h
moderately large and enthusiastic crowd to the Oval
the Governor and one of his daughters were presin t
His Ex always turns up at College sports and speech
days. Incidentally, headmaster Girdlestone aired
views at the St. Peter’s breaking-up, and said
some emphasis that the modern school-infant had too
soft a time. 100
The Chief Justice, who has been on the sick lm
for months, was back at the Supreme Court W
week looking as if he were still fit for a little work
He has filled part of his time in writing up iud£'
ments, and otherwise clearing up judicial lumber
The University women held their annual dinner at
Jackmans on Wednesday. It was an impressive
affair, as only two of the guests couldn’t claim to
be graduates.
The E. W. Frazers and their daughter, who’ve been
here seeking the pleasure remedy, or rest cure wil
return to Sydney by the Otway.
Brian Swift, son of the doctor, who has been im
bibing learning at Cambridge, has incidentally estab-
lished for himself some reputation as a golfer He
scored two wins against the Royal Wimbledon Club
last month.
Lionel Fletcher, our ex-newspaper man parson
has arisen with the hungry light of advertisement
in his eye to announce that Port Adelaide is a
sink of iniquity and most of its inhabitants deep-
sinkers. They drink, gamble and otherwise cut loose
while the Law remains asleep or wilfully blind. And
fancy, my dear, I’ve been missing all the fun through
sheer ignorance. How do the saints get the address
of these things?
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

“Kuscobin”: It’s not every man knows when he’s
on a really good thing—quality calls to quality just
as deep calls to deep. But there are some good
things no man can miss—and one of them is Helidon
Spa. Just you watch the connoisseur call for his
drink next time you’re in a high-class bar. No doubt
about him—he says right off “Whisky and Helidon.”
Sometimes he says this or that brand of whisky—
but he’ll say “Helidon” every time. The wine drinker,
too—hear him call for “Claret and Helidon” these
hot days—he knows that just asking for Spa may
mean anything down to plain Sodawater. That’s
the tip—and it’s well worth remembering. Always
split it with “Helidon” and you’ll give a sparkle, a
snap to your favorite liquor that brings out the best
that is in it. So healthy too —Helidon Spa is the
only true Australian Mineral water that has genuine
Osmotic qualities—therefore now and at all times
“Cornstalk”: It does an Australian good to travel,
it only to find out how good Australia is. I v ®
lately been about Germany and the Continent o
Europe a deal, arid used always be meeting to
going to “take the waters.” There’s no doubt these
Mineral Springs in Europe do a lot of good, but
was surprised to come across a statement in the
Lancet some time ago definitely stating that our
own Helidon Spa was the finest mineral water i n * ®
world. 1 tell you this was a startler to me, bu
since I’ve been back I’ve tried it and quite agree
with the Lancet. The best whisky and the bes
wine both taste better still if you split them
with Helidon. A doctor chap explained to me
that the reason of that was that Helidon had a hig
percentage of Lithium —it mellows the whisky an
prevents that next-morning head by eliminating any
noxious ingredients from the system. So it does oo
—you take my tip and always
Split it with

A NATURAL CONCLUSION.
1ST BUSHMAN: “ There was a terrible fire while
I was dozen in Sydney. It burnt out 12
families .”
2ND BUSHMAN (astonished): "Twelve families!
Cripes, it must have covered a few thou-
sand acres!”
“Pick”: “Marloo” (B. 4/12/T3) ques-
tions the veracity of the Darwin Chow who informed
“J.J.C.” that his stock-in-trade consisted of
gohannas’ gall-bags. All the same, almost any old
gall is marketable with the yellow peril. Before
the bear was wiped off the Australian landscape,
Chinese often paid as much as five bob for the galls
thereof. They also buy crows’ galls in any quantity.
I have frequently heard of their fancy for the like
innards of gohanna and carpct-snake, and when my
mate and I once set out to earn our fortunes by
shooting cormorants for the fourpence per scalp
paid by the N.S.W. Fisheries Department, we largely
augmented our earnings by selling the galls to a
gang of neighboring heathens at threepence each-
“Machete”: “Pick” (P». 27/11 /T 3) refers
to the porpoise as a wholesale fish-destroyer. A
much smaller but quite as destructive sea wolf is our
friend the “tailer,” or, as the Yankees call him,
the bluefish. It is an education in slaughter to see
a school of tailers clean up a shoal of mullet. Like
the porpoise, the tailer seems to often kill for the
mere joy of it, leaving a trail of fragments behind
him as he rushes with his mates through the huddled
mob of mullet. By the way, can any Abo-liar tell
me if the fish is called “tailer ’ because, as Queens-
landers assert, he tails up the great niullet shoals;
or should it be “tailor ” because of the marine
beast’s shear-fitted jaws ?
* * # *
“H.S.”: The treatment of his womenfolk
by our cannibal brother of Malekula (New Hebrides)
is brutal. At the age of six or seven, as soon as the
permanent teeth appear, two of the upper lot are
knocked out, and the girl, after adopting a yard of
calico, is ready for sale. When a purchaser appears,
she is taken up to his village and “left” there, as
they say. This is not a mere betrothal, as with
some tribes, but an actual marriage, and its conse-
quence can be imagined. A woman is unwelcomed
at birth, unhonored in life and unwept at death.
Boys live with their mothers until they are six or
seven years of age, when they are circumcised;
shortly after, if father can afford it, they are
initiated into the first rank of hara, after which
they must cook their own food. From their earliest
daj r s they are trained in the use of the bow and
arrow, and the great ambition of their lives is to
possess a musket. For a musket they will even go to
work. The principal religious idea is a kind of
ancestor worship. Images in the public square are
intended to represent the tribes’ dead chiefs and to
them offerings of fruit, food and pigs are made at
intervals to secure their good will and prevent them
doing injury. The natives also believe in a sacred
man who can make rain and wind, and cause disease
and death. There is also a kind of fetichism. Cer-
tain stones are credited with supernatural powers.
One in my possession, just an ordinary pebble of
coral rock, was supposed to protect the owner from
being killed by arrow or musket ball; others, when
placed among pigs’ food, make the porkers grow
fat. Still another when placed upon the tongue is
supposed to be conducive to eloquence. When a man
dies his spirit is believed to wander in the bush
until his relations perform his funeral ceremonies.
Then his spirit, driving before him the spirits of
pigs sacrificed at his grave, goes to the entrance of
the spirit world. At the gate stands a spirit called
Lesiv-siv, who examines the spirit of the new comer
to find if he has certain tattoo marks of a snake or a
lizard on his body. If so he is admit.-ed, but if no
marks are visible he has his spiritual head split
open and is sent back to wander in the bush.
* * * *
“Potbrook” : Kelly hated the Murphy boys
-as much as they hated Kelly, and that was saying
enough ; but they rubbed it all off the slate the day
Kelly’s dog called on them. They smoodged and
cajoled him until they got a grip on him, and the
rest is only surmise. Kelly was riding down the
main road about a mile from Murphy’s.
The old prad was extra-slow that day,
and when he pricked up his ears and
evinced signs of breaking into a canter
Kelly could not understand him. Before
he had time to look behind him the quad,
gave a wild snort and passed beyond con-
trol. Kelly threw a hurried glance to
the rear. About a hundred yards behind
was Kelly’s dog, a treacle tin on his tail,
and all the “books” in the world couldn’t
have stiffened him or the horse, and Kelly
knew it. The next furlong Kelly put in
cursing the Murphys. Then an extra
sprint on the dog’s part shortened his
odds, and Dan Murphy, ’watching from
the stable roof, laid his brother 6 to 4
that the dog would catch him before they
reached the break down the road. That
same break was just then giving Kelly
some concern. If the infernal dog could
only get the lead before they reached it,
he would, no doubt, get a pull on the
prad ; but if the dog didn’t—and again
he broke into terrible language, all of it
directed against the Murphys. Then the
break loomed up in front of them, with
Kelly a length in the lead. Whether the
prad was tiring, or he slowed down to
measure his stride, will never be known ;
but in the last chain the dog caught him,
and the field rose together. Kelly doesn’t
know how he did it, but he hung on, and
the dog dashed ahead in the next hundred yards. Kelly
then pulled the steeplechaser up and dismounted, and
led him home. He is still in that part, but he
doesn’t keep a dog, and the Murphys remain un-
popular.

‘Tat”: The ideal sporting place in
Queensland is on Inkcrman Estate, near the mouth
of the Burdckin River. One cannot travel more
than a couple of miles without striking a lagoon
swarming with ducks, wild geese and less tempting
waterfowl. I have seen on tha bank of one of these
lagoons a battalion of whistling ducks, eight or
nine deep, enjoying a siesta. In the river itself
one can get barranmndi, bream, fiathead and mullet.
Flathead are so numerous on the sandy beaches
along the Burdekin delta that on; gets good sport
even with a sharpened picket. The only fly in the
ointment is the presence of crocodiles and sharks of
unusual daring. Have seen one of the latter hurl
himself after a fish with such speed that on missing
his grab he ran right up on to the sand. But to
the man with the correct rifle and the right nerve
they furnish sport, too. When that palls you can
get more thrills amongst the wild boars. W’allabies
are also plentiful if you want to punch something
that can’t hit back. But that’s poor game.

“S.T.S.”: How old “mad as a hatter”
may be I don’t know, but “The Vagabond” is quite
wrong (B. 20/11/’l3) in dating it from “Alice in
Wonderland” of merely half a century back. Lewis
Carroll’s game was to play with types already
familiar to everyone, and the very fact that his
amusing lunatics' were a March Hare and a Hatter
shows that “mad as c hatter” and “mad as a March
hare” ( i.e in the mating season) were household
words already. Would one accuse Carroll of invent-
ing the Cheshire cat’s grin, the lion and unicorn’s
fight, the Knave of Heart’s tart-stealing, or the fall
of Humpty Dumpty?

‘Tick”: In his par. deprecating the idea
that bushmen have literary leanings, “Albion” (B.
27/11 /’l3) knocks out his own argument by liis
reference to country Schools of Arts. Will he make
his affidavit that even 10 per cent, of these institu-
tions have billiard rooms attached? “Albion’s”
knowledge of the subject is confined, evidently, to
goodly-sized inland towns, wherein billiards do
occasionally augment the funds of the School of
Arts, but I can name him a few dozen literary insti-
tutions where no towns exists at all, but which have
been centrally established by the scattered residents
of sparsely-populated districts. Further,. almost
every country schoolhouse has its library, from which
parents as well as children obtain books; also, cir-
culating libraries fire frequently run in connection
with country stores or post offices. In the tiny vil-
lage where for the time being I hustle for my daily
crust and nightly beer, there is a Literary Institute
wherein are stocked a few thousand volumes repre-
senting every author of note, and including scientific
works and treatises on almost every nameable sub-
ject ; also, we get leading periodicals and a regular
supply of books from the Sydney Free Public
Library. For 20 miles around, the district is liber-
ally catered for with every good class of literature.
And this is one instance only of several with which
I have been in touch in Various parts of the Ma
State.
“Marloo”: Something about Outback liter-
ary appetite? Try this. Arrived in a cow locality
a fortnight prior to the annual meeting of sub-
scribers to the School of Arts. The roll-up filled the
hall (30 x 20). When we got down to biz only 17
persons (including a committee of 12) were eligible
to vote. Others were unfinancial. By paying 3s.
that night you could have secured a quarter’s sub-
scription and admission to a concert and dance.
Furthermore, you were guaranteed two dances during
the quarter (light refreshments provided). The re-
sult was that 67 financial members departed, each
armed with a book from a library of 800 odd vol-
umes, and I came forth hon. see. of the institution.
At the two succeeding monthly “hops” most of the
67 members turned up. In the meantime only five
of the books had been returned or exchanged. With
the opening of the new quarter we encountered a
lock-out. The gents mostly refused to pay the 35.,
and sought to reduce the entrance fee to the dance
for non-members from Is. 6d. to Is. We made a
circumspect retreat, with 23 members. Understand
we got subsidy on subscriptions only. At the time
of the next annual meeting the membership num-
bered under a score, and the monthly dances had
lapsed. The job of collecting 62 volumes taken
out devolved on the secretary, of course, and after
a month’s strenuous canvassing I handed in 25
derelicts and my resignation.

Boulia, seems to have a fairly big deposit of a
very heavy, friable black earth. It is rendered all
the more remarkable by the fact, that everywhere
around, as far as the eyes see, there is nothing but
sandy country. It is used externally as poultices,
and internally. The blacks bake themselves in it,

and also carry it in pouches round their necks. A
Binglii who showed it to me told me of great cures
effected. The soil is so heavy that it is certainly a
mineral substance, and I think it is probably radio-
active, from which it obtains its medicinal value. I
can vouch for one cure. An old gin crawled to the
pit suffering from a terrible skin disease on her feet
and legs. Having washed them, a mixture was made
of the earth, gumleaves and saliva, and applied to
the affected parts every day for three weeks. I saw
the same gin a month after, and she was walking
with scarcely a limp.

THE FIGHT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Now this fantastic Christmas tale
Is just as true as Jonah’s whale;
For none has ever proved to me
That such a thing could never be.
’Twas Bill the Drifter homeward went
When star-set was the firmament;
And Bill had spent a pleasant eve
With friends whom he was loth to leave.
And as he dwelt on past delight
His eyes beheld a wondrous sight.
Within the narrow creek there lay
A man-o’-war in armor grey;
And cheek-by-jowl, and neclc-by-neck.
Five hundred sailors on the deck.
And every eye on Bill was set,
Before each eye a bayonet.
Then one stood forth and sternly said.
“Unless you stand, we’ll shoot you dead."
But Bill refused to stand his ground,
And swayed, and swayed, and swayed around.
Then came the merciless command:
“One volley, men, before you land.”
And out there poured a fusillade
Which very little impress made;
For Bill arose, and in his eye
The light that shines when heroes die.
He firmly gripped his trusty blade,
And met the foejnen unafraid ;
And slashed about on every side,
And reyelled in the" crimson tide.
Three of those bold marines were dead
When courage from their leader fled.
A sudden pause. “Back, back, my crew;
We’ve done as much as men can do.”
And Bill stood victor—sternly frowned,
Then wildly laughed, and sought the ground.
When Bill arose, the creek before
His humpy gurgled as of yore;
,But not upon its bosom lay
That warship in its armor grey.
Yet that ’twas not a dream I swear—
Three dead marines were lying there.
QUEENSLAND,
MEONA.


YOUR ADVANTAGES.
There are a number of distinct ADVANTAGES TO YOURSELF in buying a Beale Piano in preference to any other. Here are
some of them :
1. You make a Big Saving in Price, Quality for Quality.
You buy your Beale Piano direct from the factory, without paying profits to any middlemen or agents, and
without paying the 35 per cent. Customs duty.
2. You get a Better=toned Piano.
Beale Pianos have a most harmonious, mellow, delightful tone. They contain the only successful steel
tuning-system ever invented, and by means of this device the tuning-pins are held firmly for all time,
confining all sound to the sounding-board , which is the only proper construction. This invention makes the
piano more durable, because the iron frame alone bears all the tremendous strain (nearly 30 tons) of the two
hundred and sixteen tiglitly-stretclied steel strings. This construction cannot be had in any other pianos.
3. A Better=toned Piano because, also, of the Violin=like Sounding Board.
Only Beale Pianos contain sounding-boards constructed on the same principle as a violin-belly.. This
construction, and our secret sounding-board varnish, are responsible for that splendid tone which wins such
favor with singers especially. The Beale tone is sympathetic, sweet, mellow, sonorous and lasting. The
tone of Beale Pianos, beautiful at first, improves with use. This is the sure test of an artistically-
constructed piano. '
4. You get a Longer=lasting Piano.
Beale Pianos are made in the Australian climate, of timber seasoned in the Australian climate, so they will
withstand the Australian climate better than pianos which are made in cold, wet countries.
5. Your Beale Piano will have a Better Touch.
We supply a better “ action ” —one of the most expensive parts of the instrument —in Beale Pianos than you
find in any other pianos of similar price. We supply the recognised best action in the world in our 70-guinea
Beale Pianos, whereas you must pay not less than for an imported piano containing the same action-
system. It provides that perfect touch demanded by master-pianists.
6. You gain by the Unique Experience of probably the Greatest Piano Expert in the World.
Associated with the founder of this Company (Mr. O. C. Beale) is Mr. George D. Rose, for many years Technical
Director of Messrs. John Broadwood & Sons, London, now acting exclusively as our Technical Expert and
Factory Manager. Mr. Rose is the third generation in his family to devote a lifetime to high-grade piano
making. He has had a unique experience in all branches of the art in the best piano factories of England,
Germany, France and America, and is recognised in Europe, as probably the greatest expert in tone-
production in the world. He has invented epoch-making improvements in pianos. He was for over twelve
years President of the British Piano Makers’ Association.
7. You get a Written Guarantee from us —the MAKERS —Tor twenty=five years.
No other maker guarantees his pianos for such a lengthy period ten years is the usual thing. But we know
how Beale Pianos are constructed, and we know they will last longer than any others. In any case, if you
buy an imported piano you will find that the guarantee (if any) thereon will not be of much value to you, as
the maker is in Europe, twelve thousand miles distant from you ; whereas we are here, m Australia, with a 1
our interests in Australia, ready to support our guarantee on the spot.
8. Only Beale Pianos have quite Reliable Bass=strings. .
You know that in all pianos the strings (steel wires) in the bass portion are wound with copper or other wire
to make them heavier, and thus afford the necessary slow vibrations. In Beale Pianos these steel wires are
six-sided, instead of round, and thus afford a firm grip for the copper winding. Consequently no Beale Piano
ever develops buzzing noises caused by loose binding of the bass-strings. Other Pianos have roan steel wire m
the bass, affording no secure grip for the copper. Six-sided steel wire costs more than twice as much as the round.
9.
You get a Piano which is built only of Tested Parts. , A . , .
Every part—every detail—of every Beale Piano is tested and scrupulously examined before it is embodied m
the instrument. The slightest flaw in material or workmanship and the part is entirely rejected.
10 Your Beale Piano is Guaranteed Against Damage in Transit.
We take the entire risk of damage in delivery of the Beale Piano right up to your door : you take no risk,
If it should be damaged by any mischance, we make it good, or supply another piano of equal quality.
1 In case of Damage while in use, your Beale Piano is Easily Repaired. .
We are the makers of the Beale Pianos, and our factory is here m Australia. Therefore, if any part of your
Beale Piano-should get damaged through accident or carelessness we can always supply new parts at low cost,
and the piano is restored as good as new. How could you obtain new parts for a foreign piano . The agents
do not carry stocks of spare parts, and they have no factory facilities for making them, either. We can make
or renew any part of any Beale Piano at any time.
If you make a careful study of such advantages as other firms offer you, and compare them with the foregoing, you are sure
to come to the conclusion that you will be getting most benefit by obtaining a Beale Piano. Study what we have said above
—think it all over—and you will say that it is just simple common-sense reasoning.
