DRINKING WITH MEALS C AUSES
INDIGESTION.
“You should never drink with meals if you
are troubled with indigestion,” I was told at
a time when I could hardly eat a meal with-
out suffering afterwards. I followed the
advice rigidly, and, although I was somewhat
relieved, still suffered a good deal. I tried
several patent medicines, but without any
beneficial effect until a medical friend recom-
mended taking about half a teaspoonful ot
ordinary bisurated magnesia in half a cup-
ful of warm water after each meal and at
bedtime. The result was wonderful. The
bisurated magnesia entirely counteracted the
acidity in the stomach, which causes dyspep-
sia ; relief came almost immediately and
I am now able to eat, drink and enjoy mv
meals without bad effects. Drinking with
my meals does not worry me now. In case
any readers would like to try this excellent
remedy, which I understand relieves all
kinds of indigestion just as effectually as it
did mine, I may add that it is obtainable
from any chemist, but I strongly advise
anyone trying it to make perfectly sure they
get the bisurated magnesia, other forms not
being suitable for this purpose.—E.T.G.
“Overseer” : Re “Gil Gunnia's” par. about
the poisoning of fish by the Mitakoodi blacks in
Queensland (B. 21/8/’l3). This method of catch-
ing a breakfast is in common use amongst the
Fijian natives. At low tide, when the reef is
above water, the women get to work in the numer-
ous lagoons where the fish have been left captive.
They use hand nets in the shallow places, but
where that is impracticable they use their anesthe-
tic. The pounded leaves of a native vine are em-
ployed for the purpose. The vine turns the water
a reddish color, and the narcotic effect wears off
the fish ih a few minutes. Unlike the goods used
by Black Brother of Queensland, the Fijian decoc-
tion does not emit any smell. The women do
most, if not all, of the fishing in these tropic isles,
the men having given up work since the advent of
the missionary.
“Felix Holt”: These hot days (it’s 110°)
remind me of my first experience of an Australian
summer. It was in 1877, and I was travelling with
my then boss along the road to Gundagai. About
three o’clock we passed a man with a flock of sheep.
He was rounding them up himself, and appeared
to have no dog. “Good-day, shepherd,” said the
boss. “Good-dav,” replied the old fellow, as he
wiped his streaming countenance with a red “bundle”
handkerchief. “But you’re making a mistake,” he
explained. “I’m the dog. There’s the blanky shep-
herd,” Pointing to his dog, which lay under the
shade of a bush with its tongue lolling out of its
mouth.
“Dob Wilkins” : Bill was driving a bullock-
team into Wagga, and after crossing the river bridge
he pulled up at the first pub for a reviver. The
team, having stopped, was not ' disposed to start
again. Bill walloped and swore, but it was no go.
He thought hard while he had another “taste.” Then
he strode up to the leaders. “There, yer vermilion
cows,” he shouted, shaking a dirty waybill in their
faces, “look fer yerselves, an’ yer’ll see yer only
carryin’ two ton. Get ehrp, yer heliotrope blighters.”
Whereat the cattle all pulled together, and the
waggon creaked on its dusty way.
“Kodak” : I only know the headliners of
the bird world, and my ignorance of the others and
their habits is immense. But I want to record a
curious instance of bird-daring that I discovered
last week in a poet’s garden, at Roseville, Sydney.
Whilst pruning an overgrown rose tree, the poet
came upon a small nest about the size of an egg-
cup, containing two or three half-fledged infants.
Originally the nest must have been a nicely hidden
private residence; but by the time the poet had
finished sub-editing the tree, it was about as conspicuous
as a bed would be in the middle of Pitt-street. In-
stead of abandoning the family in disgust, as might
have been expected, the married couple bravely stuck
to their blinking encumbrances and fed them daily
in full view of all comers. We were all collected
round the bush when the pair arrived home from
the Old Worm and Moth, and they kicked up a
twittering fuss until we all retired to a distance of
five yards or so, when one of the heads of the
family fluttered to the nest, sat on the family and
stared at us thoughtfully. Now let no bird-lover
ask me what sort of bird it was. All I know is
that it was small and quite unlike a hen or an emu.

“Condamine”: I’ve been patting simple
questions on Australian geography to advanced
school children, and I’m amazed at the amount of
information they haven’t got. Any one of them can
tell me how long the Mississippi and the Amazon
are, but the longest Australian stream is a blank to
them. And it’s no slouch, either. Start near War-
wick (Q.) with the Condamine, and follow it as the
Culgoa, the Balonne, the Darling, and finally the
Murray, and you have a tidy streak; but I’ll bet
that not one kid in 50 can tell you of it.

“A. Field”: “J.J.C.” (B. 27/11/T3) is
wrong; our good Australian brother the platypus is
a native of the Barron River district of North Queens-
land, and is also to be found in the creeks and rivers
as far south as the Johnstone. Further down the
map than that I cannot vouch for.
“Snell” : Devlin’s hut had taken fire. As
Devlin had returned home two hours earlier, carry-
ing a grievous burden of brewer’s small goods, he
was in some danger of being roasted whole; but a
despondent “beat,” passing in that vicinity, ventured
in amongst the smoke and flame, and finding Devlin
sprawling upon a bunk, full as a fresh keg, took him
by the heels and towed him into the glorious moon-
light, more from an instinctive desire to save the
beer than to do Devlin a kind action. When Devlin
returned to a somewhat rational frame of mind, he
was lying near the ashes of his hut, wearing nothing
but a shirt. Devlin had a
distinct recollection of
going to bunk in his
clothes, and as. soon as he
could provide himself with
decent covering he
charged about Pingoot, in-
quiring for the “cow what
saved my life.” Eventu-
ally he came upon a gin-
ger waif wearing his
clothes and boots, and
within half an hour the
party in illegal possession
of Devlin's garments was
lodged in Pingoot lockup
charged with entering and
stealing. At his trial all
the hero could plead was.
“Lor’ lumme! wasn’t a
bloke t’ get nothin’ fer
savin’ his life?” The sym-
pathetic magistrate who
sentenced accused said:
“Next time you find Dev-
lin in a burning hut you
had better leave him
there.” “No,” said the
prisoner regretfully. “Where I made the mistake,
ver Worship, was in not chuckin’ him back after I'd
finished with him.”
“N. or M.”: The man who lays poison
for rabbits on his run has had many clods thrown
at him of late, and Tassy’s Lower House turned a
thoughtful eye on the matter the other day. Not
only is the native fauna being ruthlessly sacrificed,
but also, if the story is to be believed, the fish in
some rivers are being afflicted through poisoned or
poison-dusted grasshoppers which fall into the water.
I don’t answer for the truth of the last, though it
is a current belief; but the magpies, ringtails, etc.,
lying dead along a poison furrow answer the other
part of the question. At the same time if the
farmer is not to poison his rabbits, Heaven knows
how he is to get rid of the nest. The obvious
remedy for rabbits as for a lot of other ills is
closer settlement. But, meantime—well, it is the
meantime that is worrying us.
“Machete” : “Pick” is evidently in need of
re-pointing when he asserts (B. 4/12/’l3) that the
platypus does not suckle its young, but disgorges
digested food as provender for them. The queer
beast, like his cousin, the echidna, or “porcupine,”
is a true mammal, though zoologists put them in a
class by themselves, the monotremes. Both lay eggs
and suckle the family until the latter can forage for
itself. Plat, lays several eggs in a nest, while
“porky” carries round one only, in a little pouch
which develops for the occasion. The milk is
squeezed from the mammary glands by special
muscles, as in the marsupials, and the little Tins
mop it off the gland surfaces as it comes along. The
method of feeding the kids imagined by “Pick” is
only possible among certain species of birds which
are fitted with apparatus for the purpose.
“L.N.” : The Papuans west of Yule Island
are as reticent about their secret ceremonies as any
society in our civilisation. Weird performances are
enacted in the mysterious, gloomy dubus—immense
structures where the female may enter only under
penalty of death. When boys are approaching
puberty they are taken to their dubu and kept in it
for a long period, and they would be eternally dis-
graced jf they were even seen by a woman during
this period. If it was imperative for one to leave
the dubu —say in case of illness or fire —he would
so swathe himself in leaf garments as to be abso-
lutely invisible. I was at Motu-Motu once when
the village was en fete on account of several
youths who had completed their probation.
They were led out in front of the dubu,
glistening with oil, wearing high, conical hats,
and such native ornaments as each possessed.
They could scarcely stand, having chewed
betelnut till they were silly. Their relatives brought
loads of food to feast the village. Bananas, yams,
sugarcane, taro and such were there in tons. Many
pigs were a-cooking beneath piles of leaves on
heated stones. Eligible girls cavorted in their friski-
est mood and scantiest ramies, looking unutterable
ecstasies in anticipation of a honeymoon in the
cocognut grove. But we’d better not intrude on
them there.
"S.H.G.”: Promise the man outback a
decent funeral and he will die happy. I was trying
to persuade a pub-keeper outside Temora (N.S.W.)
who has made his pile to chuck the dust and heat an !
sweat and come to Sydney. “No good t’ me,” he
said. “I dunno anyone in Sydney, an’ if I was to
die there no one would come to my funeral. But if
I was to die out here, everyone in the district would
be at it.”
“Gouger": What would be an unspeak-
able boon on many a dry track down south is pro-
vided by the flanges of an enormous fig tree growing
on Mt. Hartley (N.Q.). The flanges have become so
twisted and intergrown as to form a huge tub, 3ft.
wide and as many deep. On the slightest shower the
drops from the leaves fill the natural reservoir, and tin-
scratchers, bandicoots and such pests quench their
thirst in the liquid for weeks afterwards. But I
suppose if the tub was down south you blokes would
growl because it wasn’t full of beer.
* * * *
“A. Field”: Can dingoes climb? “Pick”
says they can’t. Well, then, the dingo of these parts
(Barron River, N.Q.) must be of a totally different
breed to any he has experienced. Twenty yards
away from my hut I have a horse yard, two-railed,
top rail 6ft. from ground ; and I have seen a dingo
clambering on top, a yard away from the post, and
he was a lame one at that. In proof, I will forward
either yard or dingo to “Pick” on receipt of stamped
addressed envelope. P.S.—lf dingo required, enclose
3d. in stamps for disinfectant.
THE WAY OF ESCAPE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
N.S.W
The tree-insect sizzled awhile on his bough,
He sizzled and crackled and whirred.
Oh, it was a heated and maddening row,
And more than a trifle absurd.
He screamed with his friends like an outrush of
steam,
Till the bush was a-boil with the racket;
Then he wearied and died,
And his spirit untied
The back part of his blouse—or his jacket.
From that celluloid shape
Lo ! it made its escape
Through the vent in the back of the jacket.
The womenfolk chatter all day in the home,
And never get off a new wheeze;
The men hear them crying, wherever they go:
“We want you to do us up, please!”
Their clothes are all built on the same silly plan,
With the buttons put where they can’t reach ’em.
But they never desert
Through the back of the shirt—
A good trick that the insect might teach ’em.
Would you join hook and eye,
If you thought she might fly ?
If you like —! Bear in mind, she can’t reach ’em!
SILENCE.
“Eddyson” : Herbert Sell succumbed to a
great temptation. As it was Sell’s invariable pr ac
tice to to small temptations, what chance
had he aganisF a* great one? Let us think of that
when judging him. Herbert went through Reekie's
fried fish hell on the night of the 23rd, and dis
covered a large, plump, nude turkey suspended bv
the heels in the back verandah. The bird was there
holding out shameless allurement to a susceptible
stranger; a small flour bag was there, and on the
window-sill there was a stout cord. Here were all
the means to do ill deeds. Herbert Sell is prompt
in wickedness ; in pursuit of good works you couldn’t
hit him off the mark with a battering ram. He cut
down the turkey, he put it in the bag, he tied the
bag with the cord, he hung the bag over Reekie’s
back fence, and he rejoined the convivial party gnaw-
ing holes in slim flounders in Reekie's front room
Herbert had determined not to remain to supper
He begged to be excused ; he had to go and have his
appendix removed. Would everybody excuse him?
Herbert walked out at the front door, strolled round
the square, sauntered up the back lane, unhitched
the bag of decollete Christmas feed, and started for
the home of his beloved. He walked warily, like
those who go in darkness. When approaching the
exit from the right-of-way Mr. Sell heard a sharp
voice back in the gloom. The voice said “Hi!”
Only that. If you have ever stolen down a dark lane
with a sack of dead meat purloined from a treacher-
ous Dago, and a voice from out the black past has
snapped “Hi!” at you, you will understand the
vitalising effect of “Hi!” Herbert Sell moved. He
received an involuntary start, and projected himself
across the open road with the celerity of a sensitive
man who expects presently to have a set of teeth
inserted where teeth are least desirable, and to feel
a large, powerful dog of the sect of the Great Dane
pulling handfuls of material out of the back of his
trousers. It is not wise to tear across a well-lit
highway, carrying a bag, but Sell had his reasons.
He heard another voice cry “Hi!” as he precipitated
himself into another segment of the right-of-wav.
Is it necessary to remind you that Herbert Sell was
running some, as we say in Pottsville, Penn., U.S.A.?
Looking back, the unhappy young man saw the shine
on helmets bobbing in the night. To resume: 11.
Sell ran 300yds. in lsec. over the record, threw
himself through a 3-inch gap in a paling fence, and
was about to hide in Mrs. Smith’s wash-house when
Mrs. Smith herself came forth, and screamed at him.
Herbert did not wait to hear from Mr. Smith. He
went through the Smith villa, and came out in
Wickin-street, still running. In Wickin-street a voice
yelled “Hi!” and Herbert took round the next cor-
ner, and yearned for dim distance, hitting the earth
only once in every four yards. No sprinter can do
justice to himself over a long distance carrying a
three-stone turkey, and Herbert clung to that de-
funct gallinaceous biped with the determination of a
criminal knowing what was due to himself. He went
through three suburbs, invaded five private houses,
burst into the privacy of a prayer meeting, sneaked
in and out of sundry pubs, and everywhere he went
shrill, unfeeling voices cried “Hi!” In the course
of that great struggle for turkey, Herbert Sell
learned that a roan may safely carry a bag of fat
provisions in the street if he is content to walk, but
the moment he runs with it he invites vulgar sus-
picion and the enmity of men. Mr. Sell finally
evaded pursuers by hiding for two hours in a barrel
in a butcher’s stable, and when he reached the home
of his beloved he was all out. He went in by the
back way, and left the bag in the hands of the
ginger domestic. “It’s for Miss Jess,” he said. “A
present from me. Tell her it is for the Christmas
dinner, and I will come to help the family eat it.”
Herbert called at the front door of the home of his
beloved on Christmas Day. The reception he got
was not encouraging. In fact, Miss Jess’s father
gave him 10 seconds to reach the street, offering to
kick him with due severity if he seemed to need ex-
ternal impulse. Herbert’s unpopularity with the
family could not be overlooked. Jess’s large brother
came out while the poor young man was still in a
state of great mental perplexity, and hit him in the
chin. It was understood the engagement was broken
off. Herbert learned the reason some months later.
M hen the bag was opened it was found to contain
the carcase of a portly terrier. Evidently Reekie
had been advised in some way of Herbert’s doings
with his turkey, and —well, he had intended to kill
that terrier, anyway.

“A Female of the Species”.; After 18
months spent in British Columbia, 1 feel
qualified to say that the Australian who goes
to Canada expecting to see better than he
can find at home will receive a horrible
shock. Australia hears a lot about Canada s
immigration methods. But as almost every
building has a “No Men Wanted,” or simply
a “No Men” notice posted over it, and as
there is practically no small farming carried
on within at least 30 miles of Vancouver
(almost the whole butter supply for B.C.
comes from Maoriland, and is 40 cents per
lb.), the agriculturally-inclined immigrant
is not likely to burst with an excess of hap-
piness. I knew two educated, willing, able-
bodied Englishmen in Victoria (Vancouver
Island), who had been beguiled by Canada’s
alluring advertisements to “Come west and
wear diamonds.” Alter 10 months of weari-
ness, during part of which they slept in
parks and envied the well-fed swans, they
got work carrying mortar for a new build
ing and had to toil from 7 a.m. till 6 at
night six days a week (no Saturday half-
holiday there). For this they got! five
dollars a week! One of these men had been
a member of the London Stock Exchange
(he had gone smash) ; and although lumping
mortar was plain Hades to him, yet he was
afraid to leave because he well knew the
awful scarcity of payable jobs —and the
long, long waits between drinks. - Hundreds
of disappointed Britishers could be per-
suaded to come to Australia (which would
seem like Heaven to them after bleak and
dreary 8.C.) if the Commonwealth only
had a decent Immigration Office on the other
side. I stumbled across what we have in
the way of an office one day. It was tucked
up in a dingy side-street, and in the win-
dow was a small card, about the size of a
“Mangling done here” notice, which con-
veyed the information that this was the
official headquarters of the Commonwealth
of Australia. It looked like a home
for indigent piano-tuners! What we want
is a live immigration depot on Main-street,
with good photographs in the window and a
periodic display of picture films showing
the agricultural advantages of this country,
and an explanation of the facilities which
are offered to intending immigrants. Canada
is all right for the moneyed tourist, hut for
the ungilded immigrant it is Sheol—a Sheol
of snow, and shivering, and wild cursing,
and hope deferred.
“O’Phimerty” : I propose that a course of
Roman History be prescribed for all the
schools in Australia. There needn t be rnuch
of it. Just enough to show how like to Aus-
tralia Rome was in the fifth century. To
the north was a country swarming with
alien hordes, unable to find elbow room in
their own territories. At home was a people
fancying itself immune from northern at-
tack. “The Roman world,” writes Gwatkm,
of Cambridge, “was too well pleased with
itself and too contemptuous of barbarian
migrations to notice the first stirrings of the
whirlwind from the north.’ When the
whirlwind broke in the sixth century Rome
and all its western territories fell as a spoil
to the fierce northerners, who did their work
so savagely that the terrified Romans named
their leader the Scourge of God. lhe
American professor, Myers, puts the case
clearly: “All enthusiasm for the military
life and the military virtues had been lost.”
And Gibbon: “The vigor of the military
government was relaxed, and the Roman
world was overwhelmed by a deluge of bar-
barians.” History is trying hard to teach
us a few things; but it is mostly drowned
by the voice of two politicians calling one
another swine.
From a book-taster in London :
Two more books about King Edward VII. are
just out here. One is by Lord Suffield, the father-in-law
of one-time Governor Carrington, of Mt. Rennie and
thereabouts; the other by Edward Legge, a member
of a family of court flunkeys. Suffield, now 83,
started his connection with royalty in boyhood. He
lived in Norfolk ; and when the estate adjoining his
—-Sandringham—was acquired by the Prince of Wales
in 1801, they became intimate friends and neigh-
bors. lie accompanied the Prince on a tour through
India, in which Baron Bob Carrington and Charlie
Beresford also took part. Suffield is discreet in his
stories about Edward, telling only those which
illustrate the “tact and geniality” of the late mon-
arch. He makes up for this reticence by showing
the absurdities and indiscretions of the Shah of
Persia and other foreign potentates who visited
London from time to time. Legge’s book is his
second on the (Subject. He is not quite so discreet
as Suffield. For instance, this about Queen Alexan-
dra: “It is conceivable that she has had more to
put up with since her beloved Consort s untimely
death than during his life.” It is fairly well known
that Queen Alexandra and her daughter-in-law, Queen
Mary, have never been on very cordial terms; for
Alexandra was deeply pained and shocked at the
rapidity with which the volatile Princess Mary
transferred her affections from the recently-deceased
Clarence to his brother, York, the new heir apparent
to the throne. In regard to that still unsolved mys-
tery, the stealing of the Crown Jewels from Dublin
Castle, Legge tells us that Edward was furious when
the news reached him. He had a most stormy inter-
view with Lord Aberdeen, the Irish Vl <-eroy, at
whom he “thundered out his wrath.’ Legge hints that
he could spot the thief, and the reason, he says
why there has been no prosecution is that tne
evidence would have a direct bearing upon the
reputation of persons not m any way implicated in
the robbery of the jewels’—an observation that will
only add to the mystery.
The late A. W. Craven-r—M.L.A. for
Benambra (Vic.) for 24 years, and Chair-
man of Committees for nine—held his seat
almost entirely on his personal popularity.
It was while he was working at his job as
land and mining surveyor in the vast
Benambra district that he astonished him-
self, and everybody else, by first contesting
the seat against Billson. He was a fresh-
faced, school-boyish youngster, genial and
good-natured, and from the first day of his
election was always prepared to act as a
constituent’s city agent—not with an eye
to a vote, but simply because he hadn't the
heart to be disobliging. However, he got his
reward in many walk-overs —a great saving
of expense for such a constituency. He was
never regarded seriously in politics; but
professionally he had a fine reputation.
Also, he had a mania for working on featur-
day afternoons and Sundays and at night-
time fixing up correspondence, and devising
svstems for filing and pigeon-holing stuff
which ought to have been dumped into the
w.p.b. After he had passed 40 he went to
the Working Men’s College to learn short-
hand, and obtained a mild proficiency at it.
In the Great Beyond he’ll be a handy man
altogether —a superfine surveyor, a photo-
graplier in. the top class of the amateurs, a
lair shorthander, and the owner of a vast
mass of precedents, all nicely indexed, as
to the conduct of angels’ debates.
“Chasuble”: The cable tells us of the
death of Oreglia, the last of the Cardinals
appointed by Pius IX. Oreglia was the
most implacable of the “Irreconcilables.
Not then red-lmtted, he was Lisbon Nuncio
in 187 U, when Victor Emmanuel took Rome.
Oreglia was so bitter that, at the .instance
of Queen Pia, Victor Emmanuel’s daughter,
he was recalled from Portugal. The next
event in which he figured was the
election of Pope, 1878. Oreglia would have
got it but for the Portuguese King. Simi-
larly the Austrian Emperor baulked Pam-
pulla in 1903. Oreglia was almost ferocious
in his antagonism to Leo in 1878, and is
probably the last Cardinal to stub-
bornly insist on the Temporal power of the
Popes. They had more or less of that power
for a thousand years. The Italian Parlia-
ment in 1871 voted £120,000 sterling annu-
ally to the Vatican, as a sort of solace; but
Pius IX., Leo XIII. and Pius X. have
sternly refused to touch the money, and
maintain their dignity on Peter’s Pence,
which tot up to about £250,000 a year.
One very useful measure, brought in by
the Watt Government and hung up by the
fantastic politics of the period, was the
Opticians’ Bill, which had passed its second
reading. It aimed chiefly at the rascally
humbugs who prey upon the housewives of
the suburbs and the country, and inci-
dentally spoil gallons of eyes which they pre-
tend to relieve. The Vic. Opticians’ Associa-
tion chortled when the Bill was first men-
tioned ; but at the possibility of a clause
demanding that all opticians, whether re-
putable members of the Association or not,
must pass an exam., many members looped
askance —which led an eye specialist to
declare that, if it came to a test, not 25 per
cent, of practising “opticians” could pass an
examination of a fair English or American
standard.
From an old Maoriland gallery-liand :
Maoriland’s Parliament is the most serious in Aus-
tralasia. The House of Reps, prides itself that it is a
miniature House of Commons, and laboriously acts
the part. Undignified language is sternly suppressed,
and any member who applied such epithets to another
as “body,snatcber,” “condemned mongrel,” and “dirty
rat” would be warned off the precincts for life, and
the Prirtie Minister would move the adjournment
while eau-de-Cologne was sprinkled by the Usher of
the Moral Code. There was an unlucky contretemps
the other night when a goaded member described
another hon. gentleman’s remarks as a “lot of non-
sense.” The House was in Committee, and the
Chairman was staggered, the more so because he
took the insult as referring to himself. He de-
manded an apology. None being forthcoming, he sent
for the Speaker, in a silence like that which descends
on an infant class when Miss Pupil Teacher sends for
the headmaster to taw Willie. The Speaker rustled
to the chair, the Mace was reverently laid on the
table, the offender was formally arraigned and called
upon to plead. He pleaded not guilty, urging that
when he used the words, “a lot of nonsense,” he did
so as an aside, and they were in no way directed at
the Chairman. Evidence was given for and against
this plea; the Prime Minister, the Minister of Cus-
toms, Marine, Machinery Inspection, Advertising,
Printing and Stationery, Legislative, State Fire In-
surance, Life and Accident Insurance, Electoral,
National Provident Fund. Friendly Societies and Pen-
sions Department, also the. Leader of the Opposition
and 16 others, gravely debated the possibility of a
I misunderstanding; and the Speaker gave judgment,
lie “thought the hon. member was to blame, but as
the honorable gentleman assures me on his word of
honor that the words were not addressed to the
Chairman, I must rule that they did not afiect the
Chairman, and therefore there was no disrespect to
the Chair.” 15ut it was a d —d narrow squeak l
George Cann hasn’t the political luck of
his brother, the N.S.W. Labor Treasurer.
Twenty-two years ago J. 11. Gann was put
in the Broken llill seat as representative of
the handful of local miners; he happened to
be president of the local branch-union at
the time, lie still sits for Broken Hill, has
never had a serious contest, generally
has been unopnosed. Brother George, on
the other hand, though by no means the
green politician that the other was when
he was first elected, had one term in the
House of lieps., was then defeated by a mere
fistful of votes, and now lias failed to win
ex-Spcaker Willis’s seat for Labor.
The S. M. Herald, in its remarks about
the various political candidates, published be-
fore the elections, told the yarn of Pat
McGarry’s association with Murrumbidgee
upside down. As a matter of fact,
McGarry had been selected as the
Labor banner-carrier at Goulburn; but
news having reached P.L.L. headquarters
that there \yas a prospect of win-
ning the seat with a strong candidate, a top-
notcher was sent down to do the winning, and
rough-and-ready McGarry was consoled by a
chance of fighting a hopeless contest at Mur-
rumbidgee. The carefully-selected champion
went down solidly in the good-chance elector-
ate; and the energetic McGarry came back
triumphant from his allegedly hopeless con-
stituency.
From a Westralian Laborite who is get-
ting tired: —
Despite Premier Jack Scaddan’s three-year-old
boastful promise that he would down all monopo-
lists, Westralian timber combines still laugh at law
and equity. The cos. which supply fuel to the Kal*
goorlie mines and those dealing with the jarrah and
karri forests in the South-West, hold licenses to cut
timber over certain areas and construct tram-lines. In
addition, the Sou’-West people have agreed to re-
plant part of the denuded areas. These are the main
features of the agreements so far as they are known
to the public. In effect, the cos. spend their time
in a greedy evasion of their responsibilities. They
run the general stores along the tram-lines and’
charge such extortionate prices that Justice Burnside
the other day said in an Arbitration case that it lie
had his way he would sweep them off the eartli;
and he referrred to their “pawnbrokers rates.
Anyone is, of course, at liberty to open a shop,.but
the invader is soon crushed out, as the co. carries its
competitors’ goods and decides what they shall pay
for freight. So if the cos. pay fair rates for labor,
as thev say thev do, the money is hooked back in
the stores. It 'is doubtful, moreover, if the jarrah
and karri cos. have replanted an acre of land, lhe
firewood combine claims that it actually
only a few men. An applicant for toil, if he be
wanted, is told that he can chop away if he likes,
and that his wood will be bought from him. Thus
all liability for accidents is evaded. The fuel co. is
usually composed of only a few shareholders, and
each receives his whack of profits by way of salaiv
for “service rendered” ; so the State gets no divi-
dend taxes. Scaddan is well aware of all this. Most
likely he knows a lot more. Before he became
Premier he used to frequently denounce the iniqui-
ties, and promise what he would do. He has been
nearly three years in office, and up to this hour has
done nothing that the naked eye can see. Surely
the time is nearlv ripe Even a Labor Government
can’t expect us to live for ever on promissory notes.

Records for December
and Christmas.
This Christmas thousands of people will enjoy some delightful
music, including beautiful vocal selections, entertaining humor
and thrilling band and orchestral music. The New Edison
Blue Amberol Records for December are now ready, and some
of the most popular will probably be an Orchestral Selection
entitled “The Idol’s Eye,” excellently rendered by Victor Her-
bert and his own Orchestra. Peter Dawson is heard in “The,
Toreador’s Song” and the “Miner’s Dream of Home.” The
seductive strains of the ever popular “Blue Danube” Waltz, ren-
dered by the American Standard Orchestra, _ will delight all.
Other worthy records are “Always take a girl named Daisy,”
“Down at Finnegan’s Jamboree,” “Lead, Kindly Light.” etc.

THE CHOICE OF CONNOISSEURS.
POLITICAL
POINTS
The old foxes of the N.S.W. Labor party,
of course, dropped to the inherent viciousness
of Second Ballots. The second lot of con-
tests were in neglected country districts all
country districts are neglected, or think they
are. So the old foxes didn’t talk politics
when they went electioneering. They simply
put it this way: “We’ve got a majority, so
there’s bound to be a Labor Government.
You want a culvert. Now what’s the use of
sending a Liberal to get a culvert out of a
Labor Government?”
Headlines above an article in the S. M.
Herald last week :
MEAT TRUST.
Gaining Ground in Australia.
Well, of course. Isn’t that what the S. M.
Herald wanted? Anyway, a few months
ago, it fought the Referenda proposals for
combating Trusts, and if a man declines to
carry an umbrella when rain is threatening,
it’s a fair thing to assume that he really
enjoys a wetting.
Sydney had a seven-storey fire one night
last week, and enjoyed it hugely. On the
word of one of the morning papers which
do their best for the Congestion policy, and
the skyscraper of which it is part, “the fire-
men had a hopeless task.” About the only
thing they could do was to nurse the high
brick walls that enclosed the flames, so that
they would not crack and spill hundreds of
tons of hot bricks on to the adjacent two-
and three-storey dwarfs. Luckily, that fine
old incendiary, the wind, happened to sleep
right through the entertainment, so the golden
showers of sparks were not wafted round
the district, to give Sydney a taste of another
of the blessings of the Congestion policy.
The Senate had an exhilarating time the
week before it adjourned. It filled two days
with questions, arguments and casual amend-
ments ; then it brought up the report of a
speech by Joe Cook, in which he criticised
the language used by Senators. Poor Millen,
leading his microscopic minority, was asked
whether he had read the report, what he
thought of it, whether he approved of Cook’s
remarks, and what he meant to do about it.
His replies jbeing unsatisfactory, he was
rebuked, admonished, and finally instructed
to go and see liis Boss and get a replv of
some sort. The House was adjourned
till S p.m. to enable him to do so.
The Boss’s answer bc-ng even less
satisfactory than Millen s, the Opposi-
tion majority censured him, and then pro-
ceeded to reject the Preference Abolition Bill,
amend the Public Accounts Committee Bill,
and insist on its previous amendments to the
Audit Bill. Next day it w T as as sweet as
honey and as mild as milk, and Millen flat-
tered himself he would get his little Defence
Bill through without demur. The Opposition
smiled, debated the measure sweetly, and then
knocked a hole through the bottom of it.
After which it firmly but gently insisted on
its amendments to the Loan Bill, and then
courteously assured Millen that it proposed
to adjourn till Monday. As Millen “leads”
a House which is strong enough to go
whithersoever it lists and take the “leader”
with it, Millen had to accept the proposal.
By the end of another such session, he will
perhaps hai r e some doubts regarding the
sanity of our system of Party government.
THE FEDERAL GOVERNMENT
AND DEFENCE.
Of course Elmslie wasn’t able to persuade
Lieut.-Governor Madden that a dissolution
should come his way in Victoria. In fact, it
is doubtful whether any Governor ought ever
again to grant anybody a dissolution before
a Parliament’s three years are up. The
Governor’s job seems to be to say in effect;
“You tell me you can’t carry on because par-
ties are too nearly equal. Parties? What
are parties? I don’t know anything about
them. Will you swear members are evenly
divided about everything that wants doing?
No? Very well —then go and do a few of
the thousands of things that you are not
evenly divided over. The missus and I are
evenly divided on the question of scrambled
eggs. She loves and I hate them. But we
don’t quit housekeeping on that account. And
I’m not going to let you, either.”
Jokook’s pet term of contempt ought to be
registered as the war-cry of Liberalism for
electioneering purposes—“ Tripe !” Jack
Murray applied it 1 ist Thursday afternoon
to the "minute which the luckless Vic. Labor
Ministry was preparing for Waxcellency
Madden. Murray assumed that the docu-
ment would “prove to be merely tripe.”
“Trine,” seemingly, has come to stay in the
vocabulary of Liberalism. It stands for
caustic comment or crushing argument. It
has all the descriptive force of “hogwash”
and the gentle irony of “go-hon.” The
Labor party is doomed unless it can come
up to the scratch with a suitable rejoinder.
How would “Sausages!” do?
A SAFE SEAT.
The largest land ballot held in Australia took
place at Wyalong in connection with the Woollon-
gough and Ungarle homestead farms. For 35 farms
there were 2962 applicants, from all parts of Aus-
tralia.—S. M. HERALD, 8/12/’l3.
THE BIG HOLDER: “Beautiful! Beautiful!
I'll have to put up the price of my little
lot another pound an acre."
From an old political hand: —
Party Government was always a sorry makeshift;
but, at any rate, before the east-iron two-party sys-
tem came in, people could sometimes get a look in
at their own business concerns through the agency
of an Independent member or two. And the fear of
the Independent was often a great check on Parlia-
mentary corruption. But now that party government
has degenerated into faction fighting, the people never
get a look in at all. All the grabbing, jobbery and
robbery are pre-arranged by the two caucuses ; and
because every man-jack of the politician tribe is
afraid of being hoofed out of the party if he dares
to criticise its policy or Government, politicians have
degenerated into a race of Brer Rabbits, so far as
their own side is concerned, while their business is,
of course, to lie and misrepresent so far as the other
side is concerned. So we practically get none of the
truth: no man dares to tell the truth about his own
party if it goes wrong ; and it’s not his business to
tell the truth about the other party if it goes right.
Westralian Premier Scaddan
has been struck by a new
trouble from an unexpected
quarter. Before last election,
while he and his mates were
soliciting votes, they promised
that, if they were placed in
power, the railway service
would receive a substantial rise
in pay. The promise was
promptly and almost faithfully
redeemed. Now. with another
general election showing up on
the horizon, and a good wheat
harvest just ready for trans-
port, the same railway men
make a peremptory demand for
more. They simply give notice
that if their wishes are not
met within 14 days, the bottom
will be knocked out of things.
And Scaddan knows that his
alleged friends have put him in
a cleft stick. If he hands over
the required coin thousands of
voters will say that it isn’t safe
to put a Labor Government in
power because it encourages
public servants to become bush-
rangers ; and if he doesn’t
ante-up, and the bottom is knocked out of
things, the farmers will rage furiously, and
Scaddan will be knocked on the head as a
bungler.
In attempting to justify its
attitude towards the Vic. Labor
Ministry, Liberalism was some-
what unfortunate. The labori-
ously Liberal Age provided one
reason —to the effect that Labor
ought to be thrown out forth-
with, lest the young Adminis-
tration should raise railway-
men’s wages without referring
the matter to Parliament. Willy
Watt himself evolved another
reason —that the “electoral in-
tentions” of Chief Secretary
Prendergast were a departure
from “general principles every-
one acknowledged.” Consider-
ing that (1) what Prendergast
proposed was simply to enrol
large numbers of people who
ought to be enrolled, and (2)
for years Victorian railway
men have been how-ling for jus-
tice and getting considerable
sympathy from the public and
nothing else, it looks as though Liberalism
has presented its opponents with two excel-
lent helps to win popular approval.
In the Labor-selection ballot for the late
A. W. Craven’s Benambra seat in the Vic.
Assembly still another farmer was top
scorer. His name is John Moyle, and he
separates the sheep from the oats in the
Tallangatta region.
One of Willie Watt’s most
implacable opponents in
these days is ancient
“Jimmy” Mirams, who was
writing politico-financial ar-
ticles for the Age 50 years
ago, and quarter of a cen-
tury later was the scapegoat
when the boom burst and
somebody had to be punished.
The ancient has just issued
a leaflet, “The Ass in the
Lion’s Skin,” in which he
snorts at Willie’s claim to
be a Liberal and a Protec-
tionist, and denounces
Willie’s recent redistribu-
tion scheme as merely one
to diddle the Laborites bv
giving the Conservatives of
the country extra represen-
tation.
HIS MOTHER'S SON.
As far as important legislative achievement is con-
cerned it will probably have been the most barren
session of any Parliament since the establishment of
Federation. This is not an unmixed evil, for both in
the Federal and State spheres we have had a surfeit
of legislation.— S. M. HERALD.
GRANNY: “Well, Joseph Cook , I'm very phased
to see that you have got through the
session without passing any of our Liberal
programme .”
THE SLUM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Disclosures with regard to slum conditions in Mel-
bourne are shocking. Whole streets of hovels belong
to prominent city councillors. — Recent press item.
When halls of high Swellgravia are aflood with light
and song,
And floors are all a-quiver with a merry, dancing
throng ;
When heirs of half a city to their partners whisper
low,
And soft, entrancing melodies swim dreamlike to
and fro,
You sometimes catch a calling, like a smothered
undertone,
A muttered incantation with a cadence all its own ;
From haunts of hidden misery its voices seem to
come.
It swells a gruesome portent—’tis the murmur of the
slum.
In foetid hut and hovel, lo! the city children lie
When earth is all a-swelter ’neath a lid of brassy
sky;
So thick they press together in the pinched and
narrow room
’Twere hard to crowd them closer did you shut them
in the tomb ;
A gutter foul goes trickling by the entrance to the
lair,
And blends a keen malodor with the sultry morning
air;
And in that blighted atmosphere the soul is stricken
dumb —- • *
For soul and body dwindle in the Hades of the
slum.
Yet up in high Swellgravia the babes are fat and
fair,
With eyes of laughing love-light, and with lustre
in their hair;
And at their lightest wishes do the lackeys speed
away
To win the toy that gives a joy to gilded youth at
play;
And as they sport with boyish prank and winning,
girlish wile,
The happy parents watch the game with soft, com-
placent smile;
For all have health and all have wealth, and all are
glad—and some
Are lords of rods of alleys in the Hades of the slum.
The villas of Swellgravia in their spotless splendor
show;
The furtive fires of fever lick the hovels of the row.
The lords of many tenements are lapt in linen fine;
The serfs who do them homage in their squalor peak
and pine.
The hut is built of matchwood that the manse may
be of stone,
And toil is half-requited to put purple on the drone.
And everywhere the winter air bites little bodies
numb,
Because the rag is all the flag that floats above the
slum.
And over ev’ry anthem high and under ev’ry hymn
You seem to catch the swelling of a murmur low
and grim,
Admonishing the heedless, giving counsel to the wise:
Beware of building lordly States on sand of rotten
lies!
In human breasts and human weal alone lies human
wealth,
And not in lordly palaces built up of subtle stealth.
In ev’ry land, at Hell’s command, when Ruin smote
her drum,
’Twas always beat to call the feet of rebels from the
slum!
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.
KNOCKING DOWN MA’S CHEQUE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Premier’s Triumph—Uproarious Welcome—Crowd
at Railway Station. — Sydney newspaper headlinet.
Bill ’Olman come ter town ter-day. You orter ’eard
’em yell
When ole Bill dropped ’is swag inside th’ Boom an’
Bust ’Otel.
“Come on, yous blokes!” yells Bill, an’ shies ’is
cheque acrost th’ bar.
“There’s plenty more where that come from! Three
cheers, yous blokes, fer Ma!”
’lp, ’ip, ’ooray! ! Th’ cheers they give near brought
th’ bottles down.
Then ev’ry bummer crowded round and welkimed
Bill ter town.
While Cohen sweated at th’ pump an’ pulled th’
pints o’ Joy,
Ole Bill stood smilin’ proudly like th’ Wild Ker-
lonyerl Boy.
An’ when each bloke ’ad put three down, an’ ’ad a
fourth in reach,
Ole Joe ’e says, “I call erpon Bill ’Olman fer a
speech! ”
We cheered an’ stamped till Bill said “Right!” an’
jumped up on th’ bar.
“Now, lads,” says Bill, “I’ll tell yer wot me plans
an’ platform are!
Remember wot I said when I was in this bar before?’’
(’E ’ad ter wait until they stopped from bangin’ on
th’ floor.)
“You ain’t fergot wot I said then?” (My oath!”
“No blanky fear!”)
“Drunk as I am,” says I, “I’ll be a d — d sight
worse next year !”
You orter ’eard ’em yell an’ stamp when ole Bill
tole ’em that!
But ’ow did they go on when Bill throws down ’is
blanky ’at,
An’ yells, “I’ll teach yous blokes ter booze! I’ve
got th’ money too,
So ev’ry bloke in town will ’ave more beer than ’e
can chew!
’Oo says that I ain’t right ter booze?” (“Good on
yer, Bill! ’Ooray !”)
That’s ’ow th’ lads received th’ noose when Bill
come in ter-day.
Lorblimy, Missus Noo South Wales (that’s Bill’s
step-mother) she
Must love ’er boy ter give ’im such a boshter cheque
ter spree! ”

SPORTING
NOTIONS
It runs in families. Two of the best bats
amongst the youngsters playing this season for
Sydney Grammar School are Trumper and Bardsley.
Judging by the results of the first test
match in S’Africa, the home side is particularly
weak. Most of the leading players, including Faulk-
ner and Schwarz, have disappeared ; and of the team
that visited Australia A. D. Nourse alone seems to
be in anything like form. On paper the Australian
team going to S’Africa next year will have an easy
time.
Judging by the poor attendance at Sydney
Cricket Ground on Saturday to watch Macartney
smite Satan out of the South Aus. bowling, cricket’s
power to draw a crowd is as good as dead. In the
most perfect weather, with men like Trumper,
Macai'tney and Barbour in the team, an inter-State
game failed to draw more than 6000 people to about
the best ground in the world. The game evidently
needs revising or improving somewhere!
Maoriland Cricket XI fled northwards till
it met Queensland at Brisbane, and in the ensuing
struggle on a sodden wicket the Bananaland team
smashed up the Kiwis for 89. It was a sad display,
the top score being the 19 of Hickmott. Only two
others (Hemus 14 and Patrick 10) reached double
figures; even Sundries accounted for only four.
The bowlers were Barstow, who killed five for 47 ;
and Ironmonger, who shot four moas for 38. When
Queensland went to the sticks it made a depressing
start, the first three batsmen accumulating only
eight between them. But the fourth man, Marshal,
made the score of the day, 42, before he was
bowled. Prout made 14 and Evans obliged with
30, so, in spite of some ducks, the score, largely
helped by 24 sundries, soared to 124. The Maoriland
wicketkeeping was far from brilliant. Maoriland
started on its second effort 35 to the bad, and at the
close of Saturday’s play had lost four wickets for 64.
The first match for the Sheffield Shield
between N.S.W. and South Aus., started at Sydney
Cricket Ground on Friday. South won the toss and
decided to bat while the sun shone. Mayne and
Chamberlain opened, and made five and 28 respec-
tively. The top score was Crawford’s 91 ; Pellew
backed him with 57, but excepting the two 28’s
made by Mayne and Steele, and the 27 by Sundries,
he was' not well supported otherwise. The pile of
Sundries was a disgrace to the Ma State; yet, in
spite of it, the side was dismissed for a very moderate
281. Kelleway and Bardsley rushed to the sticks
for Ma’s sake.” Bardsley came to a sudden and in-
glorious end, being caught by Mayne before he had
time to score. Kelleway put up a modest 21, and
then hied away, and Macartney and Trumper were
together. It looked a terrific combination. But
Macartney was the dangerous one, and in 75min.
he had smacked and bashed a neat 100, which he
afterwards increased to 104, and carried his bat at
close of day. Trumper made a mere 19. When
stumps were drawn N.S.AV. had only two wickets
down for 149 runs. On Saturday morning they
broke out with fury. But Macartney added only
13 and Trumper only one. Barbour got 52, and
Andrews 43. Reliable Folkard smacked 25 and was
the not-out man. The southern wieketkeeping was
far better than Ma’s, and sundries were only five,
leaving 307 on the board. South went to bat once
more, 26 in arrears. Mayne and Chamberlain again
took strike. This time Chamberlain was bowled for
a duck. Mayne, however, bashed 68 before McKew
stumped him off Mailey. Moyes and Crawford made
41 and 40 respectively, and Pellew put on 23. When
the light gave out, seven wickets were down for
183. In South’s first effort Kelleway was the star
bowler, his three wickets costing 51 runs. Folkard s
three were worth 63 runs and Massie’s three 80.
Crawford was the strong hurler of the South Aus-
tralians, taking five of Ma’s wickets for 89 runs.
Kirkwood got three, for 76.
The spectacled Jim Crawford, of South
Aus., is the lion of the Wheat State’s XI, and at
time of writing is giving Ala a taste of his form. In
two innings he piled up 93 and 40, and also gathered
five wickets for 89 runs. He howled 36 overs at a
cost of 84, and by sending down seven maidens was
the only man to shine in that department. The ex-
cellence of Crawford’s bowling is proved by the fact
that the other four bowk-s could gather only four
wickets for 209 between them. #
Old Sundries is playing high jinks against
the Ala State in this season’s inter-State cricket
matches. Bananaland pocketed 50 out of 232 and 18
out of 211 a week ago. Then South Aus. received 27
out of 281 in the first innings. In each match the N.S.AV.
selectors placed a new man behind the sticks, and as
both exhibitions were decidedly low in quality, it
looks as though another search will have to be made.
AVhat is wanted is a stumper capable of helping his
own side. South Aus., on the other hand, had a
wicket-keeper of brilliant ray in L. E. AVinser, a re-
cent arrival from Bull Land. Although the South
Aus bowling was particularly varied, only nine runs
were added to Ala’s tally by sundries. On his show-
ing Winser should hold first place among the hopes
for the Australian XI.
Swelled head is always an affliction, but
when it hits a surfer it is horrifying to all hands ex-
cept the adjacent shark. There were several swelled
heads surfing last week, and the sharks, especially at
Coogee, came in very close to give the vainglorious
ones a welcome. Owing, however, to the vigilance
of the watchers the Sydney press missed a sensational
headline or two. Disobeying the order of those in
charge of the beach should be made an offence worth
£2O or two months, for the shark-baiter endangers
valuable lives as well as the one he apparently places
no value on.
At Penrith (N.S.W.) on Saturday, Syd-
ney topnotch swimmer W. Longworth put up a
fine performance in the fresh water of the Nepean
River, against a crowd of 29 metropolitan swimmers.
The distance was 1320yd5., and Longworth was on
scratch, with the limit men 6min. 15sec. away in
front when he kicked off. The breeze was strong,
and blew right in their faces, raising a very ugly
jobble. Right from the jump Longworth made pace
1— he had to, considering the handicaps he conceded.
One after another he passed them all, except Good-
win, of Sydney, and Taylor, of North Shore, who had
clawed their way to the front and were swimming
in a style that suggested they had the ability to
stay there. For 100 awful yards Longworth, though
fully extended, could make no impression . on their
lead. Goodwin, in fact, seemed to he increasing
his pace. But the strain told, and they began to
fade; and despite their gallant struggles Longworth
Crept remorselessly nearer. At last he was level,
and then he forged to the front. They came at him
again. But it was a spent effort. They could not
overtake him, neither could he shake them right
off; and for all he knew they might have a sprint
for the finish concealed about them. Taylor was the
first to fall back slightly. Goodwin hung on des-
perately ; but he had no final spring about him,
and Longworth finished three seconds to the good.
Goodwin was a good second, any way; with Taylor
third. Longworth’s time was 19min. 51sec., which,
under the disadvantageous circumstances, was a
mighty deed.
When the U.S.A. athletic team struck
Dunedin (M.L.) they met the wild Otago Cale-
donian on his native swamp, and things became
strenuous. In the 100 Yards Handicap Parker
(U.S.A.) was beaten in his heat. Putting the 161 b.
shot saw Reg Caughey, one of the visitors, win by
3ft. lin. from Reid, of Otago, who beat Yankee
Parker. In the 880 Yards Race, the Yankee dropped
out. But he came again in the Running Broad
Jump. In the 120 Yards Hurdles, Templeton (U.S.)
won his heat, but, finding himself up against
Maorilander Keddle, who had twice previously beaten
him, he did not face the final. The Mile Handicap
also saw J. A. Power (U.S.) retire after laps;
and in the Running High Jump Templeton was
beaten by A. S. Reid. Altogether the visitors
struck an evil day.
The A.J.C. opened its Christmas Meeting
at Randwick in bright sunshine pleasantly tempered
with a breeze that was like a long, cool drink, grateful
and refreshing. From the punter’s point of view the
day had only one drawback —the wrong horses won.
A big proportion of the winners went out at 12’s, a
sure sign that they carried no great swag of coin.
The Grand Stand Stakes was about the best race
from the public’s financial standpoint, the winner,
Alured, starting at 7to 4 against. The gilded plum
of the afternoon was, of course, the Villiers Stakes, a
£lO sweepstakes with £lOOO added. A field of 23
faced the starter, and the public proceeded to pile on
Golden Hop in great quantities at sixes. But Michael
Judastein did not long maintain even that price,
although he certainly continued to bawl B’s against
First Principle and Gigandra, the second favorites.
The first to show out was the 33-to-one-against chance
Malchester, with the favorite in hot pursuit. Then
Popinjay blew along, but was immediately headed by
Royal Laddie. Then Popinjay again. Golden Hop at the
six-furlong post was running about third last, and hope
began to wither in the punter’s bosom. At the bend
First Principle put his nose level with Popinjay’s
girths, Malchester looked as though he had designs
on First Principle’s tail, and Royal Laddie was hang-
ing to him. As Popinjay thundered into the straight
he was pursued in that order. At the distance First
Principle came at the leader; but as he came he
swerved, losing ground by running the loop, so to
speak, and Popinjay, dashing straight for the win-
ning post, won by two lengths, with Royal Laddie
third, about four lengths behind First Principle. The
time, lmin. 38isec., was Isee. worse than last year’s.

“Hermes”: Some nice little dividends
handed out by the Tote at Belmont (Perth) on
Saturday; First-class Plate, £5 14s. (for 10s.) ;
Second-class Plate, £lO 12s. ; Christmas Handicap,
£l3; and Welter Handicap, £l2. By the way,
isn’t the advocate of the Tote in N.S.W. barking up
the wrong tree in asking Parliament to introduce
the Tote? Is there any law against the use of the
Tote on a registered racecourse? Betting is allowed
on such courses, and Tote betting is far more genuine
wagering than gambling with bookmakers. All that
is required is to convert the racing clubs, not
Parliament.
Adelaide does not go racing to excess—
never except on Saturday, barring a Cup Day; not
more than once a fortnight, on the year’s average,
for the whole metropolitan area ; and less than once
a fortnight when the crowded autumn campaign is
over. But it likes to be comfortable. The S.A.J.C.
at Morphettville and the Adelaide Racing Club at
Victoria Park (within the city boundaries) have
made themselves luxurious courses, and the Port Ade-
laide club, the only suburban one, is working up
to their standard. It has just thrown open its
£12,000 improvements, including a railway siding
near the stands, a hoist for the flat, Flemington
style, and a new Hodsdon (Queensland) totalisator,
which is of course claimed to be the best in Aus-
tralia. It is the Tote, as a matter of fact, which
has made these luxuries possible, as it does every-
where it is used. Port Adelaide, by the way, has
made an innovation by allowing well-known patrons
to deposit cheques with the secretary and buy tickets
against the amount. It will be a convenience for a
few heavy bettors, but for the “machine” to depart
from the strict cash principle that has always been
its boast seems to be like asking for trouble.
* * * *
No less than 78 motor cyclists have entered
for the Continental Reliability Test between Sydney
and Melbourne. This procession will rage across the
landscape of two States from December 29 till Janu-
ary 1. The first day it will puff and snort to Goul-
burn, 132 miles, and indulge in a flexibility test on
Governor’s Hill before entering control. The second
day will take it over Jugiong Hill —1408 yards, and
250 feet high—on to Gundagai, 120 miles. So far
the running' will have been fairly good. But on the
third day will come the awful roads between Gun-
dagai and Wangaratta, 164 f miles. Over that wil-
derness points will be lost wholesale and probably
some machines smashed up. The final run will be
the 148 miles between Wangaratta and Melbourne,
and will include a non-stop from Seymour to Craigie-
burn. The first and chief reward for all this will be
a motor-bike worth £65, which will be awarded to
the rider who loses the least number of points.
Separate prizes will be dealt out for the various
competitions en route, and then 78 weary men will
sit down and think hard if the game is worth it.
However, the affair will throw some light on the
question whether seven or eight h.p. is necessary
for road work. There are 10 seven- and eight-
powered bikes competing, and they are up against one
of 2i, a swarm of 2i and 2f, a still larger swarm of
3ps, one of 4 and 13 sixers. Thus practically all
powers are represented.
* * * *
On the beautiful roads of Yorke’s Peninsula
(S.A.) George Ramsay has knocked the 24hours’ motor-
cycle record into shivers. A 34-mile lap was taken,
southward of the Wallaroo copper-mine, and he flew
round it once an hour, doing a bit better than that
towards the end of the day. He started and finished
at 4 p.m., and raised the record from 722 miles to
836. The only question is whether the length of the
course can be guaranteed.
Just out in time for the Christmas tours,
“Broadbent’s Motor Route, Melbourne to Sydney,” one
of the best half-crown’s worth of earefull.y-compiled
information ever printed. Broadbent takes the road
by sections and gives a most minute description of
everything, from V-drains to XXX bars.
The New York Six Days’ Race, an annual
cycling struggle of much importance, has just re-
sulted in a win for ex-Australian Goullett and J. Fogler,
of the U.S.A. Another Australian, It. J. McNamara,
finished third, with Root, of New York. Grenda and
A. J. Clark had to retire owing to falls.
The distance covered is not stated, but the previous
American best was the 2737 miles put up by Mac-
Farland and Moran at N.Y. in 1908. The best Aus-
tralian run was that in Sydney in 1912, when Goul-
lett and Hehir did 2085 miles and 1680 yards. Goul-
lett has been very lucky at this sort of grinding;
since he and Hehir won the Sydney and Melbourne
double, he and Fogler scooped the big Paris event, with
the world’s record run of 2775 J miles in April last
year. Fogler won the New York race last December
also, but on that occasion his mate was Rutt, the
German champion.
Sydney’s Pennant bowls on Saturday was
scarcely a game for weak hearts. Consider the ex-
citement when Victoria Park just squeezed Randwick
out of it by a majority of one (95 to 94), and Bur-
wood upset Petersham’s applecart by six. Balmain
■did better against Woonoona, and had seven to spare.
City clouted Red fern by eight, and Mosman downed
Hunter’s Hill with the same majority. There were a
few other victories somewhat less arduous, but, on
the whole, things were very strenuous. Fortunately,
in most cases, stimulants were handy.
Judging by statistics recently published in
the United States, baseball is one of the most dan-
gerous games on earth. During the season just
closed in Yankeeland no less than 15 players have
been killed outright, while the non-fatal but serious
injuries run to hundreds.
Summer baseball was not exactly sus-
pended in Sydney on Saturday—it was merely re-
moved. The teams went to Windsor and played a
couple of matches in aid of the local hospital, and
had a good time. The first match Was between the
Reds and Blues. The Reds started badly, and scored
only three in four sessions. The Blues in the same
time put up 11. Things looked blue for the Reds.
But they struggled on, and eventually won by 18
to 11. Then a combined team was chosen from
these teams, and went up against Leichhardt B. It
had no hope. It was utterly overthrown and stamped
flat, and Leichhardt raged out winners by 15 to nine.
Johnson got into the ring last Friday at
Paris, opposed to another nigger called Jim John-
son—whether a brother or a cousin or merely a son
of Ham was not stated in the cable. The place was
thronged with women, and they made a terrific uproar
when it became apparent that the niggers were
anxious to avoid hurting one another. The scrap
ended in the fifth round, and was declared a draw;
after which the audience, led by the women, furi-
ously demanded their money hack. Whether they got
it the cable forgot to tell. But as Jack Johnson had
allegedly broken an arm the nasty remarks may have
been undeserved.
Waldemar Heiberg’s bad luck found him
out again in Melbourne on Saturday night. He was
fighting Alf Goodwin, who wanted to hug him like a
bear. Holberg fought him off, and piled up points
galore. Had the fight gone the full distance Holberg
must have won. But getting irritated at the con-
stant clinching, Holberg bashed at the break-away,
and referee Fullalove cautioned him. Fullalove has
a beastly habit of meaning what he says, and when
the forgetful Holberg repeated the offence several
times in the 14th round, the referee stopped the
fight and gave it to Goodwin on a foul. The most
surprised wight in the place was Goodwin; but he
soon recovered sufficiently to wander round and
collect the large of the fighters’ share.
That much-talked-of scrap between Lang-
ford and Jeannette, at New York, back in October,
seems to have been very much over the odds. Lang-
ford in condition should weigh 12st. 21b., but he
cheerfully admitted that he was 14st. 31b., whereat
everyone marvelled where he hid the weight of
black flesh. Jeannette walked off a Boston vaudeville
stage, where he had been giving a turn, jumped into
a train and got to New York just in time to keep
faith. Faith as regards showing up, that is. Other-
wise the only faith was on the part of the public
who put up' the splosh. Langford’s stalling was
terrific —he would hurl in vast punches and pull them
back just before they made impact. In one case
Jeannette made a mistake, and landed Langford a
beauty on the nose that made it bleed considerably,
and Langford’s look of surprise and Jeannette’s ex-
pression of horror were a sight for the gods, who
rose up and hooted fiercely. The referee begged
them to fight and give the people something for their
money, but they ignored the request, and went on
killing time with little sparring and much hugging.
Probably the greatest fight of the year will
be the disturbance between Harry Stone and Herbert
McCoy, at Sydney Stadium on Friday night. On
paper Stone should beat McCoy from the jump;
but paper form counts for nothing in boxing.
Judging by the speed McCoy showed in his fight
with Pal Brown, it is just possible that he may be
able to catch the elusive Hebrew as he comes in, or
as he backs off. McCoy has the faculty of taking
punishment and slogging like a Trojan all through.
His quickness of return as we saw in the fight with
Brown is phenomenal, and heavy, too. He seems to
know bv intuition where to hit, and if he does
beat Stone it will be one of those sudden rights that
shook Brown so much that will wing the dancing
Menhisto—and Stone without speed is a dead bird
and a gone coon.

was at Oaldands, California, and the object of his
butts was Joe Azvedo, strongly suspected of being
a Spaniard or some other Dago. He was
all over Moran at the time, and the latter 7,1"
ently reckoned it was better to Jose on a foul P th

The fight between Englishman Arthur
Evernden and Jeremiah Jerome at Sydney sula;.
on Saturday night was a strenuous affair but
from exciting. It is said that when Jerry Ilk
really hard he is no good ; and there is little do, ht
that for this fight he was badly
roast pig and other luxuries. Jerry’s condition w as
so hopeless that he could not follow up his advan
tages when he succeeded in scoring them No m,
ever -expects to see him win on points. He is not
a pointsman, nor anything else connected with »
railway. He wins with a sudden, hurricane act Hp
goes baresark, showers a sudden blizzard of siedsre
hammer blows with lightning-like rapidity and
when the dust has cleared the other is down or
out, or both. In Saturday night’s riot Evernden
scored in the first round, but got knocked down in
the second and all but outed. Then, had Jerry been
in nick, he could have unwound his hurricane and
ended it. But he didn’t. The fight degenerated
into a scrambling business in which, bent almost
double, they stood head to head or shoulder to
shoulder and sought for a chance to uppercut. Jerrv
acted the giddy ox to a considerable extent and
allowed Evernden to pile up a small mountain of
points. In the 17th he caught the Englishman a
fearful left swing on the right eye, and cut it
very badly. Jerry did follow up oh that occasion
but failed to accomplish anything very serious’
That eye troubled Evernden right through the rest
of the fight, but he fought on with dogged persist-
ence, and piled up points very grimlv. As time
went on the aborigine became anxious and waded
in for a knock-out. But Evernden smothered his
attempts to cut loose, and Jerome was not in con-
dition for speed. Another few ounces of the rapidity
he has so often shown, and the Australian would
have won hands down. As it was. Evernden- fought
a remarkably gallant battle, after the cutting of the
eye, and deserved his victory. All the same in
proper condition, Jeremiah would sing the hymn
of victory while Evernden wrote his lamentations.
“M.P.A.”: Clarence Weber, Melbourne’s
most beauteous athlete, decisively vanquished William
Maynard, an American wrestler, in a catch-as-catch-
can at Melbourne Athletic Club. Weber had the
advantage of height, weight and strength. Against
this, Maynard was said to have a whole bag of tricks
up his sleeve; but the clean-built, hefty-looking
stranger didn’t get a chance to reveal one of them.
Clarence rushed his man hard to the ropes, laid
violent hands upon him, and scored a flying fall. In
the second bout Weber again tried the same trick—
a body-hold well up under the armpits, a lift and a
side heave. But Maynard turned quickly as Weber
brought him down and spreadeagled on the defensive.
Clarence next seized the Yank’s foot in a vyee-like
grip, and clung to it for some minutes, working on
the leg as a lever to upset Maynard’s equilibrium. He
eventually forsook the leg attack for a chance that
enabled him to gain the second fall with something
in the nature of a half-Nelson and further arm-hold.
This bout lasted Bmin., and finished the match. Weber
has met a fair string of wrestlers since old Buttan
Singh gave him such a doing in Wirths’ Circus tent
in Melbourne years ago ; but turkey-legged old Singh
is still the toughest and most crafty of the lot.
Weber beat him for the title with plain rough-up
methods. When Singh went to England some few
years ago he proved himself equal to, if not better
than, any of the middleweights thereabouts ; so if one
draws a line on Singh’s form in England, it would
place Weber about top dog in such company. But
amongst the men of tonnage he would have no chance.
Evidently the boxing ring at the M.A.C. is no place
for a wrestling contest, in any case. The rusher with
the height and weight has all the advantage. Pinned
against the ropes, a smaller man hasn’t a ghost of a
chance, and at this game Weber succeeds every time.
* * * *
Reg Baker has a Chinese boxer coming
on, and declares he will make a champion of him.
This is awful! Mclnstoush brought his black pugs
out here, and for about two years the world groaned
under the fear of the Dark. When that passed it
was the day of the Jew. Jews are absolutely on
top of the game just now in every class in which
they are represented. Perhaps it is the strategic
side of Baker’s brain that suggested to him the
training of the Chow to beat the Chosen. But imagine
the feelings of the average Australian boxer when he
steps into the ring w r ith the Yellow Agony!
J. J. Miller’s neat little diary and date
book for 1914 is on the Melbourne market, and can
be now procur’ed by post from the Miller Printing
Co.’s office. As a pocket companion for daily use
and frequent reference it is the best of its sort.
Insuring the car against destruction by
fire is a ticklish business in Michigan (U.S.A.),
where a law has just decreed 25 years imprisonment
for burning a car to collect the insurance. The in-
surance cos., of course, pulled the strings. Probably
when the premiums ease off they will be sorry they
overdid it.

The Inky Way.
Anybody who desires better value than two nips
for one shilling will find it in Copy, the Christmas
number of the Australian Journalists’ Association’s
trade paper, The Journalist. It contains 114 pages
of articles, stories, verses and illustrations
by most of the best writers and black-
and-white artists in the Commonwealth, in-
cluding Bedford, David McKee Wright, John
Sandes, W. M. Hughes, “Dryblower” Murphy, P. J.
Nolan, C. A. Jeffries, B. S. B. Cook, “Kodak,” Lewis
Deer, A. L. Brewer, H. M. Green, Ruby Davies, J. H.
Ramsay, Farmer Whyte, Isabelle Ramsay, “Steele
Rudd,” “Hop,” D. H. Souter, Claude Marquet, Will
Donald, Ure Smith, Lionel Lindsay, Harry Julius and
Frank Jessup. As a scheme for giving the journal-
istic and artistic school in Australia a chance to
make holiday in print it is a notable start. *
W. M. Hughes, ex-Atcorney-General, writes the
story of the first Labor newspaper he had anything
to do with in N. S. Wales (the story is in Copy, the
Christmas publication of the Australian Journalists
Association). “Put briefly,” says Hughes, “the idea
was to combat and, peradventure, overthrow the
hordes of Capitalism, spread enlightenment through-
out the Universe, awaken the slumbering masses, and
substitute for the effete and rotten existing social
system a new order... .Nobody on the paper was
ever paid. It was a most extraordinary and most
disquieting fact, but current coin of the realm, or
any other negotiable coin, was rarely ever seen in our
office. In the early days of the journal this excited
little comment, but as the circulation bounded up,
and still gold and silver avoided us as though we had
the pestilence, a thick gloom settled heavily upon us
all One of She staff was an Italian. He was an
Anarchist, and bombs with him were the one remedy
for all evils. He was the most kindly-natured soul
I have ever met, and I am persuaded he would not
have known a bomb from a banana, and he would
not have hurt a fly.” With no money coming in, the
new apostles took payment in kind. “By the process
of exhaustion, corn beef and damaged apples fell to
our lot. We ate both when we could get them, but,
believe me, they make a rotten diet for a man who
has to hurl himself against the massed forces of
Capitalism,,,. The myrmidons of Capital began to
assail us after about the sixth issue. A base caitiff
sought to seize our poor plant, his pretext for this
dastardly attempt being that our bill for paper was
overdue. Hard on his heels came another, cackling
about ink supplied and not paid for.” When the
end came, “the editor never quite recovered from
the shock, and even now edits a highly-respectable
and law-abiding journal. Some of the staff, driven
by despair, stood for Parliament, and were returned.”
Amongst them were Hughes and W, A, Holman.
From a reporters’ room in London: —When public
men have said something they are sorry for some
of them have a habit of alleging that they have “not
been accurately reported” ; and the English National
Union of Journalists has got tired of it. So,
when Lord Wolmer, M.P., did it, the secretary of
the Union wrote to him and pointed out that two
experienced reporters, unknown to each other and
acting independently, took down the disputed pas-
sage, and their printed renorts were literally iden-
tical. “The curious result follows that if your
Lordship is right, the reporters not only omitted
important words, but actually turned a plural pro-
noun into a singular one three times over. Such an
aberration would be hard to explain if confined to
one reporter. Shared by two reporters acting inde-
pendently of each other, it cannot be explained at
all, except on the assumption that it never hap-
pened.” The upshot of this correspondence was
that Lord Wolmer confessed that it did not happen.
The sec. of the N.U.J. calls this too-common sub-
terfuge “a mean and shabby practice,” and adds,
“The Union is at present considering whether it is
not possible to secure some legal protection.”
Meantime, whenever occasion arises, it is going to
take the same steps that it took in this case to
nail down a particularly contemptible sort of lie.
“Melb ”: Apropos the early days of the Herald.
after the ex-bricklayer had bought it from David
Syme (B. 4/12/’l3). The editor, Dr. Hickson, was
a- curiosity. Besides being a man of overpowering
dignity, he was full of fads. Among other things
he was a ferocious teetotaller, and issued a pamphlet
“Will the Coming Man Drink Wine?” He proved
conclusively that by this year 1913 no alcohol
would be consumed on earth. Evidently he made
no converts at home, for his wife and two daughters
kept the Chancery Lane Hotel, Melbourne, after his
death. The daughters also appeared for a brief
period at the Theatre Royal. Graham Berry was
hard up at the time when Hickson was editor of
the Herald and Forsyth chief proprietor, and he
did some wbrk for them. He even edited the paper
for a little while —after he had performed the same
service for Tait’s Collingwood Observer. But, like
Gambetta and nearly all eminent noliticians or
authors, Berry had not the journalist’s instinct.
Even as proprietor of the Geelong Advertiser he was
a nuisance to the staff. Oratory doe's not chime
in with writing. Greeley, of the New York
Tribune, was inferior as a news man to Ben-
nett, of the N. Y. Herald. Even a Carlyle or
Macaulay could not write up a newspaper. And
Dickens failed. Berry’s idea was that the Melbourne
Herald depended on a large number of short politi-
cal articles, nossiblv 13, which he would rip off
every day. Meanwhile there was no news: and there
is nothing else of much value to a newspaper.
A TALE OF TOUTIHE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Testimonials for these pills had to be put in a
literary style. If they were put in the illiterate
way they were given, people would not read them.
—Sydney law report.
I read —and, as I read, the tears
Splashed on the printed sheet.
To think you’d spent ten frightful years
Just dying on your feet....
The picture showed you bent with pain—
One hand pressed to your head ;
You seemed too wasted to regain
The health that long had fled.
I read your symptoms one by one,
And saw your ills increase,
Until I longed to get a gun
And give you lasting peace.
I read and sighed as you were racked
For nearly half a page ;
By everything you were attacked—
It put me in a rage.
It seemed insanely wrong that you
Should ache and throb and twitch.
I swore when parts of you went blue
And when you got the itch.
But when you fell to ruins quite,
And doctors gathered round
To gaze upon the awful sight,
My grief I could not sound....
Then suddenly, athwart the dark,
A ray of hope !.... A gleam !
A twinkling advertising spark—
“ Try Pudger’s Purple Cream 1”
You tried a pot—a little one
Upon your azure leg.
It did you good! The fight begun,
You went and bought a keg.
That was the end of grief and pain.
Life was a rose-lit dream
When you became a man again
Through “Pudger’s Purple Cream.”
’Twas but an ordinary “case,”
But, told with Milton’s art,
It streaked with scalding tears my face
And nearly broke my heart.

KLAXON
“&he. Public Safety Signal
The note of the Klaxon is purposely harsh and unpleasant.
It is a WARNING signal. It expresses DANGER— to insure SAFETY.
Previous to the advent of the Klaxon,
scientific analysis of the sound properties nec-
essary to produce a note of warning, had never
been considered.
Makers of so called warning signals had ig-
nored entirely the psychological fad; that all
true warning sounds—-while they may differ
widely in other respects—must possess one es-
sential characteristic: they must be harsh, un-
tuneful, unpleasant—repellant, rather than at-
tractive.
Nature’s warning cry—since the world be-
gan—has been a discord. The warning growl
of a dog, the cry of a fowl that voices danger,
the warning snarl of a wild beast, are not pleas-
ant nor musical.
All humans—from babyhood on—all ani-
mals use and understand this same discordant
warning.
Try it on the dog; try to call him to you by
shouting in a loud, harsh, warning voice; try
to send him away by employing sweet musical
sounds.
A musical sound made by an automobile tells
a falsehood to a pedestrian whom it is intend"
ed to warn. It indicates that there is no danger,
when danger is most imminent.
The note of the Klaxon is a true warning
note. It is not pleasant to hear; but it at once
expresses—and is instinctively understood as
meaning—DAN GER.
The note of the Klaxon cuts through every
other noise. It is heard and is heeded—with"
out confusion, without hesitation. It warns on
the instant-—in time.
The KLAXONET and Klaxet are smaller
Klaxons, opertaing on the same principle and
producing notes higher in pitch, lower in vo"
ume. Designed for small cars and runabouts.
An
times
adequate warning signal is necessary to insure safety; but its use should be restricted to
when it is necessary—as a warning of danger. The law should stop unnecessary noise.

Factory, Newarß, N.J*
