The Cult of Beauty
A RARE COLLECTION OF BEAUTY
HINTS FROM NEAR AND FAR.—
HOME RECIPES.
How to Have Thick and
Pretty Hair.
“Home Talents.”
Soaps and artificial shampoos ruin many beautiful
heads of hair. Pew people know that a teaspoonful
of good stallax dissolved in a cup of hot water has
a natural affinity for the hair and makes the most
delightful shampoo imaginable. It leaves the hair
brilliant, soft and wavy, cleanses the scalp com-
pletely and greatly stimulates the hair growth. The
only drawback is that stallax seems rather expensive.
It comes to the chemist only in sealed packages,
which retail at half a crown. However, as this is
sufficient for 25 or 30 shampoos, it really works out
very cheaply in the end. *** For an actual hair-grower
nothing equals pure boranium. It is quite harmless,
and sets the hair roots tingling with new life. *** The
use of rouge is almost always obvious, but powdered
colliandum gives a perfectly natural color and defies
detection.
To Kill Roots of Superfluous
Hair.
“Home Science.”
Women annoyed with disfiguring growths of super-
fluous hair wish to know not merely how to tem-
porarily remove the hair, but how to kill the hair
roots permanently. For this purpose pure powdered
pheminol may be applied directly to the objectionable
hair growth. The recommended treatment is designed
not only to instantly remove the hair, but also to
actually kill the roots so that the growth will not
return. About an ounce of pheminol, obtainable
from the chemist, should be sufficient. *** For com-
plexions inclined to be oily many smart women are
now using just the natural allacite of orange blos-
soms. It is perfectly harmless, makes a capital
greaseless cream which holds the powder perfectly
and does not encouraee the growth of hair. *** Dis-
agreeable body odors can be instantly neutralised by
the occasional application of a little powdered
(white) pergol to the affected surfaces.
Using Oxygen for the
Complexion.
“Chemistry at Home.”
The well-known characteristics of oxygen may be
effectively applied to the renovation of the com-
plexion. Mercolised wax, such as may be found at
any chemists, contains oxygen which is released
directly the wax comes in contact with the skin.
Oxygen consumes waste matter in the body, but does
not affect healthy tissues at all. Therefore, it
attacks and removes the deadened waste accumula-
tions on the skin which show in the disfiguring form
of sallowness, moth patches and a general appear-
ance of lifelessness. The fresh young skin which
has been obscured by this disfiguring veil of waste
matter is thus allowed to show forth in all its
healthy beauty. The mercolised wax is absolutely
harmless, and indeed very beneficial to the skin. *** To
bring a natural red color to the lips, rub them with
a soft stick of prolactum. t *** For tired, hot or per-
spiring feet use a teaspoonful of powdered onalite in
a foot bath.
Emergency Complexions.
“Novel Recipes.”
Even a complexion which is hopelessly dull or
greasy can be made beautiful in a moment by a
perfectly harmless home recipe. If you have no
cleminite in the house, get about an ounce from
your chemist, and add only enough water to dis-
solve it. Apply a little to the face with the finger
tips. The process defies detection, and the result
is an immediate appearance of velvety, youthful
“bloom,” which every woman desires. The effect
will last all day under the most trying conditions,
indoors or out, and renders powdering quite un-
necessary. This simple substance is also very good
for the skin, and no possible harm can come from its
regular use. *** To make the eyelashes grow long, dark
and curling, apply a little mennaline with the finger
tips occasionally." It is absolutely harmless, and
beautifies the eyebrows as well. *** Pilenta soap is the
most satisfactory for all complexions. It even works
well in cold or hard water.

I help you to live on time
You’ll call me an alarm clock, but
people who have used me call me
“ TJte Big Watch," for I keep watch-
time. My bell really isn’t an “alarm”
either, for it doesn’t startle you. -
I’ve a mellow, deep-toned, cheerful
voice. It arouses you promptly but
gradually with a “ Good morning,
it's breakfast-time" sort of sound.
I am really two alarms in one, a
long alarm ringing for 5 minutes
without interruption, an intermittent
alarm ringing for 10 minutes at half-
minute intervals; you can shut me
off whenever you please.
I must be watch-accurate as a
time-keeper or they won’t let me
leave the factory. They make me
run six solid days and nights with-
out varying two minutes. If I do
vary —back I go to the adjusters.
I look lilpe a watch, act like a
watch, and in many respects am
built like a watch.
The heart of your watch is the
escapement —the part that ticks and
on which all its timekeeping quality
depends. Look at your watch. Its
escapement is governed by the bal-
ance wheel and the hairspring in
exactly the same way as my escape-
ment is governed.
That’s why I’m a timekeeper.
Most alarm clocks tick slowly and
heavily. I tick lightly, evenly, and
fast —like a watch.
An inner casing of steel makes me
strong and keeps out the dust. That’s
why I stay a timekeeper for years.
I am 7 inches tall, I'm massive,
well poised and triple plated. My
face is frank, open, easy to read— my
keys large, strong, easy to wind. If
you have me oiled every other year
there is no telling how long I will last.
I am made in La Salle, Illinois,
U.S.A., by Westclox. My price is
12/6, postage 1/- extra. If you can-
not find me in your town, send
a money order to my makers’
Australian office, Westclox, 63 Pitt
Street, Sydney, and they will
have the nearest jeweller forward
me to you at once.

The Secret of the “ Swan's " Popularity lies in this part—
THE WORKING END.
E. J. Carroll tells how Graham Moffatt got his
chance with “Bunty Pulls the Strings” in London.
Carroll, who will tour the “Bunty” co. through
Australasia, had the information at first-hand from
the author and producer when he (Carroll) was re-
cently in London. Moffatt, prior to 1911, was play-
ing sketches in Scotland, and trying hard to get a
chance with his since-successfuE piece. He pestered
the London managers until Cyril Maude agreed to
help him. “I don’t think this dialect thing will
go,” said Maude: “but as you are so sanguine about
it, I’ll let you have the theatre for a matinCe, and
I’ll get all my friends and the metropolitan press-
gang to come along. You get your cast and be ready.”
Moffatt had little money, but he got some people and
borrowed some wardrobe. What followed is old his-
tory now. To date Moffatt is said to have made
£150,000 out of the piece.
“Maeko”: The attention paid to' the comfort of
the people on the stage nowadays strikes one who
was a humble performer in the ’Bo’s. In the Theatre
Royal, Melbourne, I was one of the shivering fairies
in the original production of “lolanthe.” The time
was midwinter, and our dress a miserable wisp
of liberty silk, and a scanty underskirt which we
were not allowed to supplement with an extra petti-
coat. Occasionally we braved the wrath of Emmie
Nathan, and got into a bit of flannel, but it meant
a fine if we were discovered. I can’t remember if
jaeger was invented in those days; at any rate, it
would have been impossible to wear much of it, as
our bodices were extremely decollete, and one leg
was partly shown in the silk stocking. Unforgettable
was the vast stage, and the wind whistling round it,
and tearing through from an enormous property-
room, where on the hottest summer day it was as
cool as a vault. I think it had a door, but I never
saw it closed. When Mrs. Brough (the Fairy Queen)
delivered the line, “And your legs shall be our
peculiar care,” our business was to stand on one
limb with our wands extended towards Strephon.
Often we only managed to keep the poise by touch-
ing our immediate neighbor. There were no fires
or stoves in the drpSteing-rooms, but the gaslighting
kept the apartments so hot that when we “went on” or
stood round in the. draughty entrances waiting for
our cue we shivered. One consolation —the principals
had to rough it with us. Even our Nellie Stewart had
an ill-ventilated room, without a fireplace. How
we used to pray for an opera with lots of march-
ing, dancing and movement to enable us keep our
chattering teeth still! “lolanthe” was so quiet
that we never got warm. A few months ago “Within
the Law” was on, and I had business on the stage
of the old theatre. The contrast ! Warmth and
comfort .everywhere. Electric radiators round the
stage. Doors within doors, shutting out any possi-
ble draught, and swinging and sliding noiselessly,
from the same old property-room, exits and stage
boxes. And bright, warm dressing-rooms. Under
these conditions the present-day pro. should be con-
tent with half the salary we got—though, as a
matter of fact, he (or she) get a great deal more.
“Gazer”: The blatant A.N.A. in Victoria an-
nounces the judges for its coming competitions. As
for years past, the music adjudicators are a German
and a genial money lender, also exotic, The latter
gentleman is an amateur. Music is his little diver-
sion. In giving the job to a man outside the
musical ranks, is the A.N.A. aware that within the
past 12 months two sterling musicians have been
driven to leave this city through inability to earn a
living wage?
The ordinary merry-go-round with its jangling
barrel-organ can move on for the prairie now that
Sydney had seen the “caroussel,” which is United
States French for the gorgeous rideabout at the
White City. C. Spencer (the picture man), who
holds a wad of shares in the new playground, had
the machine made at a cost of £6OOO ( £lOOO of it
for the organ) and there is not another so elaborate
in the world, unless the Lost Tribes have set it up.
It is fitted with something like 60 or 70 horses and
a number of chariots, the nags by an ingenious con-
trivance imitating a gallop as the contraption whizzes
round. The lighting decorations are a feature in
themselves. There are 13,000 electric lamps and
candelabra in the scheme, and these make the “car-
oussel” look the star item in the park. In fact, it
looks a whole constellation.
“J.F.H.” : London Daily Telegraph extends a
cordial greeting to Oscar Asche and Lily Brayton
“after a year and a half’s sojourning in foreign
parts.” The D.T. is a bad Imperialist. Australia
is not a foreign part. It is a theatrical suburb of
London. When Oscar left London 18 months ago
he “had reason to hope that by the time of his
return he would be the possessor of his own theatre;
but that expectation has not been realised.” So
Oscar and Lily are to take lodgings again, either at
the Queen’s or the Globe, where early in January
they will revive “Kismet.”
“K.C.”: Because he had had two or three songs of
his composition published, the girls in the boarding-
house thought he was the Whole Cake. So did he
until Henderson came. Henderson had never heard
of the song-maker, but the astounded girls soon put
that to rights. “Seen my new piece?” smirked the
insufferable composer across the table to Henderson
one evening at dinner. “No,” says the brute,
“where does she work?” The girls are not speaking
to Henderson yet.
“Locusr’: Saw “Hindle Wakes” in England lately.
It is mainly a series of conversations on the sex
question, not from the subtle problem standpoint, but
from the side of down-right utility. A factory girl
has a “week end” with her employer’s son, a young
man engaged to be married to another manufacturer’s
daughter. She is found out through pitching a
weak tale to account for her whereabouts; and her
scandalised parents say the young trifler must marry
her. His rich father, who had been a workman with
her dad, agrees; so does the young man ; so does
his fiancCe; so does everyone -except the “victim.”
She flatly refuses—says she doesn’t permanently love
him, and that she ain’t no victim ; that he was only
“just someone to have a bit of fun with.” Of course,
the young man is horrified, when she tells him that
he was for her merely what the average young man
thinks a casual girl is for him. “You’re a man, and
I was your little fancy,” she says. “Well, I’m a
woman, and you were my little fancy. You wouldn’t
prevent a woman enjoying herself as well as a man
if she takes it into her head?” He is terribly
shocked, and says: “It sounds so jolly immoral. I
never thought of a girl looking on a chap just like
that.” The girl is kicked out by her parents, re-
fuses aid from the rich manufacturer, and goes out
into the world with a declaration of independence
that she’s a Lancashire Lass, that as long as there
are weaving sheds in Lancashire she can earn enough
“brass” to keep her going, and that “so long as she
has to live her own life she doesn’t see why she
shouldn’t choose what it’s to be.” Shades of “East
Lynne” and “The Sign of the Cross”! What’s the
English stage coming to? Arp they going to bring
this to Australia? What do we keep Percy Hunter
in London for, I aek!
A FOOTLIGHT FRAGRANCE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. W. N. Miller, of Ohio, as the result of long
experiments in onion hybridisation, has succeeded in
raising an onion that is all flavor and no odor. It
has the flavor of the Bermuda onion and the hardi-
ness of the golden yellow, without a trace of the
odor of either.— Melb. meekly.
’Twas Tot who reeked of patchouli,
While Maisie is recalled by musk;
Frangipani brings to me
Kate and her kisses in the dusk!
But lo! the gentle onion means
Much more indeed of vanished bliss,
Recalling each full-flavored kiss
From damsels snatched behind the scenes i
I think of footlight charmers cute
Whene’er of onions I partake,
Each chorus girl the fragrant fruit
Devours, for her complexion’s sake.
But Science in its stern advance
The onion’s pungent smell would slay;
And, in this manner, take away
The scent of many an old romance!
VICTORIA.

IN BANANA LAND
Pathetic “Missing Friends” advertisement
in the Brisbane Courier (13/12/’l3) :
George.—Come back again. Never mind work;
I II keep you.-—B.
If George resists this, he is adamant indeed.
Out of 1137 applications for loans granted
under the Workers’' Dwellings Act last year
Brisbane scooped in 713, or two-thirds of
the total. Maryborough actually secured
ien. But this was a special concession to a
town returning two staunch Liberals.
Cooktown Independent tells a yarn about
a local carpet snake, 20ft. long, that hunted a
sitting lien off her nest, and swallowed the
eggs. Afterwards, while sleepily attempting
digestion, the reptile was killed and the eggs
were extracted, Replaced under the hen, and
uuly hatched out. Well, if the rescued eggs had
each brought out a young carpet snake or a
juvenile croc., Cooktown might be proud of
itself. But hatching chicks out of hen’s eggs
—there’s nothing in it.
Finding that sharks were at work, and
-that, on account of the outrageous prices the
men had to pay, they would probably come
at him for higher wages, Railway Commis-
sioner Evans lately established socialistic
stores on the Mount Cuthbert and Mount'
Mulligan railway works. He reports that
both are selling goods of better quality and
at lower prices than the private shanties,
and still show profits; so he intends to do
more in the same way. Which puts Denham
and his supporters in a hole, as they don’t
know whether to pat themselves on the back
for the successful enterprise, or tell Evans
to give over his foolish idea before the
marriage tie is busted and the sanctity of
the home is destroyed for ever.
Queensland is slowly coming into its own.
A few years back the Calliope Valley and
the Mt. Larcom scrub lands, both in the
Port Curtis district, were looked on as quite
useless, and settlers passed them by accord-
ingly. A few foolhardy optimists, however,
decided to try the libel, and last week Rail-
way Minister Paget opened his eyes
to the result. In the Mt. Larcom
scrub lands 16,000 acres have been
taken up under 200 miners’ homestead
leases, and 14,000 acres by German set-
tlers, all within three years. It is lime-
stone country, and will, seemingly, grow any-
thing, but it has such a preference for sugar
that if there were a handy mill the scrubs
would soon become canefields. In the Calliope
Valley there are already dozens of dairy
farms, and the country is so “useless”
that one patch of lucerne was cut nine times
last year. When Bananaland’s population
moves on to the land in earnest, it will not
starve for lack of suitable accommodation.
P. W. Shannon, who goes to the Land
Court, replaces the deceased Judge Scott
with an official trained in his own school and
possessed of the same devotion to official
duty. Shannon is a comparatively young
man (47), keen, alert and vigilant. He will
probably be succeeded as Under-Secretary
by Gordon Graham, an encyclopaedia of
Lands Office minutiae.
The contractor is having a cold time in
Queensland. The records of the Railway
Department show that, where there is proper
supervision, the State itself is by far the
cheapest and best contractor. Treasurer
Barnes, though he admitted it was against
his grain to say so, told a deputation the
other day that they had saved as much' as
half the ‘cost by the present system. Even
the Church is finding it out. The treasurer
of the Pittsworth Anglican body has written
to the papers to say that they built their
church for £3OOO, while the lowest tender
was £5OOO. When you tell Brisbane people
that contractors really live on the losses, they
point to one man who made so much out of
one Government job in Brisbane that he
never had to worry about the wolf again.
NOT ELIGIBLE.
KITTY: "Why ain't you goin' with Willy now?"
SISSY : “ Couldn't waste me lime. He ain't in no position to buy peanuts!"
Bananaland Labor party got what it
wanted—“Bishop’’ Barnes is Acting-Premier.
The Tory press barracked for Jimmy Blair.
The Tory press hits sharp snags in these
days.
Townsville papers show that, in the local
tenders for hospital supplies, meat, which
was available at 2d. per lb. last year, is
offered at nothing less than sevenpcnce for
next year. Has the beneficent Trust got to
work' 1 so soon?
It is remarked in Brisbane that O. C.
Roberts bids fair to effect a revolution in
the prickly-pear business. A man with the
name of Oliver Cromwell ought to be at
home in making revolutions.
Young Row, who gets the next year’s
Rhodes scholarship, is a son of the Uunwich
medical officer, and comes from the N.S.W.
family which got some fame for an embro-
cation that farmers spill on stock to mend
them. Curiously, two other Roes, of another
family, have won the Queensland scholarships
previously. Perhaps they all deserved the
lift; still it becomes clearer that the unidenti-
fied genius has very little chance if he is not
in close touch with the University.
Gospels, Old and New.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
If you suffer ev’ry evil
With a patient kind of groan ;
If you lift your cross like heroes,
Never heaving sigh or moan ;
If you take your black reverses
As a tonic-thing in curses,
Rest assured, my suff’ring brothers,
You shall stand before all others
In a circle round the Throne ;
You shall enter Highest Heaven-
Yea, the loftiest of the seven.
(Now the whereabouts of Heaven
Isn’t known.)
If you bear your little troubles
With a discontented groan ;
If you smash vour cross to flinders
And your fathers’ faith disown ;
If you tell the devils in you :
“I would like to scalp and skin you/
Rest assured, my brother sinful.
You will have your little skinful
When Apollyon gets his own —
Gets his own on you in Hades
Where the grill in constant trade is.
(Now the whereabouts of Hades
Isn’t known.)
Take, my friend, your joy and sorrow
As a thing that soon is flown ;
As a bird that’s gone to-morrow
With the breeze that’s overblown ;
Bide you, friend, by this' evangel:
I mvself am curse or angel;
I can keep my mind a palace ;
I can poison every chalice,
Make or mar the anthem-tone ;
But my Hades has its birth here
And my Heav’n’s upon this earth here,
(Now the whereabouts of earth here
Well is known!)
QUEENSLAND.
PHILANDER F.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, December 20, 1913.
My Dear Thargomindaii.—
The Talbot aide, who “severed his con-
nection” with your sample of imported Gov-
ernors, will arrive here about the first week
in January, as gentleman-help to our sub-
royal personage. His picturesque uniform
is expected to be a new treat for the girls.
The Foxton aide will take over the private
secretaryship to his Scotchellency. 1 rom
what that untruthful jade Rumor says about
the Strickland menage, Captain Talbot will
feel in his new billet something like a lion
in a den of Daniels.
Premier Denham was entertained at din-
ner at Parliament House last night. Robert
Philp was in the chair, and besides the
local talent and beauty who were grouped
round the feeding board were Uncle Sam’s
Commissioners, Adams and Stallsmith. who
are up here on business connected with the
Panama Canal Exposition. The chair-
gentleman proposed Digby’s health and
Digby thanked him at length. Then “Bishop”
Barnes proposed the American Commission-
ers’ health and theyi separately related their
.thanks. Next Philp presented to Digby a
pair of field-glasses (presumably to assist
him in picking out likely financiers) and
Digby replied with some more thanks.
Finally, as many of the crowd as could
manage it shook old Dig. by the hand and
wished him a pleasant trip, and he thanked
them kindly. It was a great thanksgiving
meeting.
To lots of people it was more than a sur-
prise when “Bishop” Barnes beat Jimmy
Blair for the acting-Premiership. So far
weights, odds and time have not been pub-
lished, to the dissatisfaction of a large sec-
tion of the betting public; but it is whis-
pered that the race was very close,
and that the Bishop won by a suddenly-
extended whisker.
Railway Commissioner Evans is to be
sent to America to see how they manage
their railways over there, especially that
part of the business which has to do with
the packing, transit, etc., of frozen meat,
fruit, etc. Other matters, too, will be con-
sidered. The Railway Department could do
with a few lessons on other matters. For in-
stance, tickets arp now given up at a barrier
at the Central Station. The sad suburban-
ite considered that he would thus save the
time that trains were detained at Bruns-
wick or Roma streets, while yelling
porters gathered up the cardboards. To his
despair he finds that the horde of porters
has faded away, and that on arrival at the
Central he has to join a long and snakelike
queue, which slowly wriggles through one
half of a gate. All other exits are barred.
Perhaps.in America Commissioner Eva ns will
learn some method by which all the gates
may be open at once, and a porter supplied
at each opening. Even a railway commis-
sioner ought to be able to learn a little for
the convenience of the travelling public.
Freda Bage, M.Se., F.L.S., of Melbourne,
has been appointed head of the Residential
College for Women. Freda is a smart
little woman, who has won a Mcßain
Research Scholarship, and the Victorian
-Government Research Scholarship. In be-
tween she demonstrated at Melbourne
University. Afterwards, she travelled
Europe for two years, and on her return to
Australia was appointed acting-lecturer in
biology to the Queensland University. She
is a nice, sociable scientist, and should do
very well at the Women’s College.
Miner James Alford was handed a medal
yesterday for helping to rescue two children
from a burning house at Mount Chalmers.
James Hill, who aided and abetted, will get
his at Rockhampton next Tuesday. George
Y.’s locum tenens passed over the bit of
bronze, and mentioned that these knick-
knacks were only given when they were
thoroughly deserved. Alford went back a
thiru time after the two children were hauled
out, thinking that still another infant was on
the premises, and got pretty badly burnt.
II is final exit was made about 30 seconds
before the building ,
Mrs. Elkington, wife of the well-liked Fed-
eral Health Officer, has evolved an idea for
which she deserves a well-nourished wreath
of bays. She and her husband .are building
a house on their land at Sherwood, and until
it is finished they are to reside on the
scenery in a large, cool tent. Mrs. Elking-
ton knows what this sort of existence is like,
for in India she often accompanied her hus-
band on his travels beyond the tourist tracks.
T Will not, of course, be an ordinary tent.
It will, as a matter of fact, be two tents,
and will have a board floor and such luxuries
as telephone, ice-chest, gas-stove, etc. It is
quite time someone with some initiative
started a scheme like this. In these days of
timber rings and lack of domestic help a tent
seems ideal, and now that a recognised society
leader has begun it, the revolution may
spread. Better a tent where simplicity reigns
than a large mansion replete with all modern
inconveniences except domestics. It is quite
likely that another six months will see the
suburbs bursting into canvas.
Popular medico Hopkins was fed and
otherwise entertained at Eschenhagen’s yes-
terday. Carl Zoeller occupied the head perch,
and about a dozen other well-known busi-
ness and medical men sat around the festive
board. Carl Zoeller mentioned how glad they
were to see Dr. Hopkins so improved in
health, and marked the occasion with an illu-
minated address.
The city kindergartens have been enter-
taining their pupils at Christmas trees.
Ithaca. Ipswich-road and the Valley have
all had a turn, and the infants appeared to
have the time of their young lives.
These kindergartens are fine things.
Each one is right in the heart of a slummified
part of the town, yet one never sees a poorly
clad or unhappy-looking youngster in the
lesson ring. All the mothers have an approv-
ing word for the teachers and the schools,
which is another good sign.
May the New Year find all your bills
receipted.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
Governor Macgregor, at the Brisbane
Grammar School break-up, expressed his
delight that precedence was still given to
Latin and Greek. A few minutes later, he
lamented that neither French, German nor
English was half as thoroughly studied in
our academies as in those of the Continent.
Perhaps the dead languages, to which the
Macgregor is so partial, get in the way of
the living ones.
At Ohillagoe (N.Q.) recently one man
called another a “scab,” and reported him as
such to the Union. He was fined £3, with
£5 13s. 4d. costs. A few days later Warwick
doctors agreed that the Friendly Societies’
doctor was also an animal of the same breed,
and refused to work with him. But no fine
has followed in this case, of course. Offen-
sive language doesn’t consist so much of
what you say as the sort of clothes you say
it in.
“Itangan” : Bananaland threatens to break
the bonds of one foolish convention —the
building of almost air-tight schools. A new
open-air factory has just been put up at Too-
woomba, and Under-Sec. Story, while lauding
its other advantages, praised also its virtue
of cheapness. Queensland’s old-style schools
cost on an average £8 per head. (Inited
States and Great Britain pay £4O a head to
build theirs.) But the new open-air type
cost only £3 a head. Incidentally, what s
wrong with open-air churches? Banana-
land’s tabernacles are even more air-tight
than its schools, and who wants to’ go to
church to literally sweat out his sins?
From one of the faithful: —
It is 50 years since Dr. Dunne, the little, mild-
eyed, self-effacing Archbishop of Brisbane, sailed
from sweet Tipperary to help Queensland grow big-
Queensland was then a parochial little spot, and it
wanted some forcing. Bishop Quinn was pegging
away at the immigration question. He had discov-
ered a process whereby he could bring out immi-
grants entitled to land orders, and he invited nr.
Dunne to back him up. Although some of the lana
orders went to the Church, hundreds of farms abo
Warwick and Toowoomba went to Tipperary, ana
Wicklow immigrants and settlement grew rapid).
Dr. Dunne was an enthusiast in stocking and crop-
ping.- He took little interest in politics; tilling tne
vacant spaces of the Downs with the sons and g
ters of Erin wholly absorbed him. He had a repu
tation for scholastic accomplishments, as he had pee.
for a few years a seminary professor; but an ag
tural gazette was dearer to him than a v .
doctrinal controversy. And at 82 he preserv
same mild-eyed placidity.

Inquirer: The Oldest Inhabitant tells us that, theie
is no record of any of the Teeth having been in the
retail beer trade: the “family fortunes” certainly
were not founded that way. In 1836 Newenham and
Tooth founded the Kent Brewery. In 1843 Robert and
Edwin Tooth —the latter the father of the baronet—
came from Cranbrook, Kent, to take over the man-
agement, and “R. and E. Tooth” was the firm until
1854, when Robert, Frederick and Edwin Tooth were
the partners. And so it was in 1858, when Robert
Tooth represented Sydney in the second Leg. Assem-
blv. In the mid ’6o’s the firm consisted of William
Butler Tooth, Frederick Tooth and J. S. Mitchell;
W. B. Tooth had been squatting in t'-e Moreton Bay-
district, and had represented that territory in the
second Parliament. In the first ’7o’s the firm com-
prised Frederick and Robert Lucas Tooth (the now
baronet). A year after that Frederick left and J. S.
Mitchell came back. Tooth and Co., Limited, was
formed in 1888 Snatcho: Heat sketch might make

....Vantage: That doesn’t follow. F. \V. Holder,

Professor Cherry, who instructs the youth
of Melbourne in the art of turnip-raising and
the allied branches of rural industry, is a
short, energetic, husky person with
whiskers like a bunch of mattress-stuffing,
lie lives at Glen Iris, where signs of his
labors are to be seen all over the landscape.
Being a cow-professor, he keeps a herd of
all-sorts, which are mostly the envy of the
local rural-producer, the exceptions being a
bare-ribbed Ruby or two and a shrunk-
■ shanked 'Doll, presumably retained for ex-
perimental purposes. Besides tearing a con-
siderable amount of stock-sustenance out of
the soil around his own homestead, the
academic fodderist leases land in the most
unexpected places, and makes two tons of
maize grow where only 5cwt. of weeds grew
before. For some time he was Director of
Agriculture; but there were disagreements as
to the cow-policy of the country, and a
University professorship looked better than
a directorship enduring perpetual criticism
from all quarters. Victoria'certainly needed
an agricultural professor at least-as much as
it needed an imported professor of litera-
ture, and this one seems to fill the bill, if
it be possible to judge by the proofs of his
activity.

N THE SUPREME COURT OF
QUEENSLAND.
No. 354 of 1913.
Mr. Justice Chubb.
BETWEEN
ROBERT JOHN SIMPSON, Plaintiff,
and
WILLIAM SIMPSON, Defendant.
TO WILLIAM SIMPSON, formerly of
Currumbin, in the State of Queensland, in
the Commonwealth of Australia, Farmer, but
believed to be lately residing in the State of.
Western Australia.
TAKE NOTICE that Robert John Simp-
son, of Currumbin, aforesaid, Farmer, has
commenced an action against you, William
Simpson, in his Majesty’s Supreme Court aLi
Queensland, by a Writ of that Court dated j
the fourth day of December, One thousand
nine hundred and thirteen, which Writ is
endorsed as follows : ,
“THE PLAINTIFF’S CLAIM' is
against the defendant for partition of all
that piece or parcel of land containing
One hundred and sixty acres more or
less situated in the Parish of Tallebud-
gera, County of Ward, being Farm No.
13, Beenleigh District, Portion 19V, and
being the whole of the land described in
Deed of Grant No. 78463, Volume 630,
Folio 223, of which said land the plain-
tiff and the defendant are the registered
proprietors under the Real Property
Acts 1861 and 1877 for an estate in
fee simple as joint tenants, or in the
alternative for the sale of the said
piece or parcel of land in lieu of such
partition as aforesaid and the distribu-
tion of the proceeds of such sale.
“And for costs.”
AND-you are required within two months
after the receipt of this notice, inclusive of
the day of such receipt, to defend the said
action by causing an appearance to be
entered for you in the said Court to the said
action and in default of your so doing the
said Robert John Simpson may by leave of
the Court or a Judge proceed therein and,,
judgment may be given in your absence.
You may appear to the said Writ by
entering an appearance personally or by
your Solicitor at the Supreme Court Office
at Brisbane.
Dated this Eleventh day of December,
1903.
McCOWAN & LIGIITOLLER,
Solicitors for the Plaintiff, Robert John
Simpson,
New Zealand Chambers,
334 Queen Street,
Brisbane.
In the Supreme Court of New Zealand.
Wellington District.
IN THE MATTER of The Trustee
Act, 1908, and
IN THE MATTER of The Public
Trust Office Act, 1908 ; and
IN THE MATTER of the estate of
George Tuck well formerly of Wel-
lington in the Dominion of New
Zealand but latterly of Sydney and
Cobar in the State of New South
Wales in the Commonwealth of
Australia, Laborer, deceased.
PURSUANT to an Order made by His
II on or Mr. Justice Cooper under Sec-
tion 74 of the Trustee Act, 1908, on the
second day of August, 1913, in the matter of
the above estate all creditors and others
(INCLUDING THEREIN THOSE
CLAIMING AS NEXT OF KIN OF THE
SAID GEORGE TUCKWELL) having
claims against the estate of 'the said de-
ceased are required to send in their claims
to The Public Trustee, Wellington, New
Zealand, on or before the 31st May, 1914,
and in default of any such claims The
Public Trustee will on the last mentioned
date proceed to distribute the assets of the
said deceased having regard only to. the
claims of which he shall then have notice.
DATED at Wellington. New Zealand,
this 2nd day of August, 1913.
J. W. MACDONALD,
Solicitor to the Public Trust Office.
NOTE :—The said George Tuckwell was
by an Order of the said Court on the 23rd
March. 1911, presumed to be dead. He was
a native of Wellington and left to reside in
New South Wales. Claimants must 'for-
ward full particulars of the parents of the
said George Tuckwell, his brothers and
sisters, the date of his birth and a resume of
his life, which information will be checked
with the true facts in the possession of The
Public Trust Office.

OVER LITTLE GIRL
•
Could Not Stick Pin Between Sores.
As if Scalded. Broke and Formed
Sores. Pitiful to See. Cuticura
Soap and Ointment Cured Her.
•—
Italians, Via Ballarat, Vic.—“My little
girl, Violet, has suffered With eczema ever
since vaccination some five years ago. The
teczema Caine out all over her; you could not
stick a pin between the sores and more espe-
cially in her joints. It was like as if she
had been scalded, and the blisters used to
break and form sores which were full of
humour. It was pitiful to see her.
“I tried ointments and several other
remedies but derived no benefit, so I sent
an order for Cuticura Ointment, and am
pleased to say after using two tins and
bathing well with the Cuticura Soap she
is cured and the Cuticura Remedies have
cured her. I used to apply them in the
following manner: bathe with Cuticura
Soap morning and night and apply Cuti-
cura Ointment on linen after bathing.”
(Signed) Mrs. W. Peel, October, 1912.

plexions, red, rough hands, and dry, thin
and falling hair, as well as eczemas,

the world. Liberal sample of each mailed

* * * *
There ik a great scooping up of mud and ooze
ill the shallow channels about Melbourne just now.
All the dredges are hard at work, and the marine
slime of ages is sliding and splashing into the
hoppers, of which there are not enough. The clean-
up is being proceeded with vigorously, because the
channels have been allowed to silt up until the
actual depth is a couple of feet less than the
registered* ditto. A few inches more or less would
mean nothing much to a port like Hobart, but Mel-
bourne sees ruin in the distance when the mud is a
little thicker than usual. Even the coveted battle-
cruiser can’t get into the bay until it has fired away
some of its heavy ammunition, burnt a good part of
its coal, shaved off most of the bosun’s whiskers and
taken the superfluous flesh off the crew. Still
by dint of heroic mud-shifting, Melbourne is keeping
up, more or less, with the marine times; but the
taxpayer is bleeding for it, and the nerves of the
mariners who have to claw and skate their ships
through the gutters in the sea-bed are as taut as
banjo-strings.
Comparison of food prices and rents in Maori-
land’s four chief cities is slightly in favor of
Dunedin. Bread is one penny per 2lb. loaf cheaper
there than in Auckland, and a halfpenny cheaper
than in Wellington and Christchurch; 251 b. of flour
is quoted at 2s. 10d., as against 3s. 4d. in Auck-
land, 3s. in Wellington and 2s. lid. in Christchurch ;
i-ton of coal costs from ss. to 95., while in Christ-
church it costs from 7s. 6d. to 10s. 6d., in Auck-
land from Bs. 6d. to 9s. 6d., and in Wellington
from Bs. 3d. to 9s. 6d. ; and forequarter of mutton
is 3d. per lb. in Dunedin, 4d. in Auckland, and
in the other places. For house rents Welling-
ton is the most expensive. Four-roomed shacks there can
be had for 16s. to 18s. a week; in Auckland, 10s.
to 15s. ; Christchurch and Dunedin Bs. to 15s. A
six-roomed house in Wellington brings the rentlord
from 245. to 30s. ; in Auckland, 18s. to 275. 6d. ;
Christchurch and Dunedin 15s. to 235.

“Marloo”: A shire council recently received this
amongst its answers to an advt. for a surveyor and
sanitary inspector, at a salary of £BO: —
Dear Sir, —Herewith receive my application for
the dual positions of surveyor and sanitary inspector.
I am good looking, speak eight languages, and am
learning others. In addition to performing the duties
mentioned in the advt., I am willing (without extra
remuneration) to act as caretaker of municipal build-
ings and fireman, and assist in cleaning the streets
and collecting house refuse.
Should the soil be clayey, 1 will make bricks in my
spare time.
I have spent £6OO to qualify as a sanitary engi-
neer, but I think the position you offer would give
me scope to complete my education in municipal
work.
I attach only one condition to this application,
Viz., that permission be granted me to keep fowls
and a cow, so that I may provide the poor of the
district with eggs and milk free.
Somehow the councillors didn’t appoint him.
“Cash with Order”: The price of the “life study”
photo, of the kind liable to be seized by the Cus-
toms, is going to drop, owing to the simple fact
that the Customs won’t get a chance to rtab it. It
is being produced, and that most artistically, inside
the Commonwealth. My friend dropped on the
scent while out after rabbits. Novelists are fond of
calling a scene “indescribable”; this one was —in
print. He thinks it was because he was carrying a
gun that the man with the camera received him
without violence, though coldly, after the actors had
fled. Art Photography was the trade name of the
proceeding, and the demand was alleged to be good.
One young man, one older one, and three young
women (one of them little more than a child) were
the co., and they took it in turns to work the
camera. A lively imagination, and evidently a
considerable amount of classical education, com-
pleted the outfit. The discoverer won’t say where
the camp was, except that it was within 20 miles of
a G.P.O.
* * * *
“U.S.”: A New York Insurance concern talks of
putting up a 60-storey building, 901 ft. high. The
highest existing is the Woolworth, 756 ft., and 50
storevs —qver twice the height of St. Paul’s, London,
and 200 ft. loftier than the Great Pyramid. Next
comes the Metropolitan, 685 ft., 46 storeys; third,
the Singer, 612 ft., 42 storeys. There are in N’York
10 sky palaces, or “cliffs,” as they are locally
styled, with a total population of 73,000 in business'
hours, or more than 7000 apiece as an average. The
Metropolitan has the astounding number of 15,000.
Thus 10 roofs cover more people than Ballarat and
Bendigo together. New York land costs even more
than soil in London. A corner of Broadway and
Wall-street has been sold for £l2O sterling the
square foot, or over £5,000,000 sterling the acre.
The oldest existing record of sale shows that this
lot went for £l2O the acre. The rest is value
created by the community and collared by the in-
dividual. The building of skyscrapers in N’York is
due to the business part of the city being packed
into the tongue of land known as Manhattan —be-
tween the Hudson and East River. The erection of
such buildings in uncramped Chicago is only bravado ;
yet there was a time when Chicago capped New York
with the 33-storey Masonic Temple.
* * * *
The youth was teller in the bank at Smithsville,
and used £35 of the bank’s money. When the
manager checked the cash the ingenious youth would
hand the head a roll of~ (say) a hundred notes, and
when that was counted he would slip 35 notes from
the counted roll to another, and so it appeared that
he had £35 more than he really did have. One day
he received instructions to proceed at once to Brigh-
ton for a fortnight. That was awkward. But he
managed it. He went to the boss, represented
that his father was ill and needed £35 immediately,
and asked the chief to cash his cheque for that
amount on the Cross Roads branch. The manager
did so, whereupon the youth promptly paid the
coin into his cash, and so made good his shortage.
When he arrived at Brighton he took £35 out of
the cash there and wired it to Cross Roads to meet
his cheque. When his time was up at Brighton he
worked the same game on the boss there as he had
on his own manager. Arrived back at Smithsville,
he took £35 out of his cash again and wired it to
meet his second cheque at Cross Roads. Then the
inspector came. The ingenious youth swindled him
as he had the manager by slipping 35 notes from a
counted roll to another pile. The next morning the
inspector was leaving. On the platform of the rail-
way station he overheard someone remark what a
bad night the teller had had at cards. Result:
The inspector went back and counted the cash
again. While he was doing so the youth went across
to the local branch of the other bank, got 35 notes
“on the exchange book,” took them over to his own
office, and so had a balance. But in making up his
cash the night before he had included the £35 as
being in gold. Therefore he was now £35 short in
his gold and £35 over in his notes. Mr. Inspector
asked for an explanation. The youth said that the
other bank had come in on the previous evening,
after he had made up his cash, and asked for £35
in gold for 35 of their notes. “Well,” says Mr. In-
spector, “you don’t mind if I step over and verify
that?” He was stepping, when it dawned on the
youth that the game was up. Final result: An
ingenious youth saeked, the managers at three
branches carpeted, and an inspector put up one.

The Harvester.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
DRAWN from out the desert places,
Out of glooms and silent spaces,
Where the winds take form in dark-
r> an( * the ghostly vapors blur,
Ry the hill and through the heather,
Lareless of the hour or weather,
.through the town and through the meadow
journeyed Death, the Harvester.
Proud he rode, with bearing regal;
On his wrist the hooded eagle
fcwift to swoop and fast to follow —none
shall mark the sudden flight!
(aim was he, a horseman knightly,
JNaught ungainly nor unsightly;
4 Ins was Death, the Royal Huntsman,
I riend of All, the Splendid Knight!
Glossy black, liis charger stately
Avi .? sse f the trappings chased ornately,
While the ringing harness quarrelled, silver-
linked with cloth-of-gold ;
Bearing with majestic paces,
rm° ut of Scorns an d silent places,
that great overlord of Buddha, Christ, and
all the gods of old.
Blazoned on his armor golden
Shone his crest and ensign olden —-
Sword and sickle interwoven on a poppied
field of red ;
Through the wheatfields, ripening mellow
w ith their myriad flags of yellow,
Rode he with the shadowy escort of his
countless troops of dead.
Like the leaves of Autumn, drifting,
ddying, skurrying, whispering, lifting
With the gales that rake the forests, came
the motley hordes of ghosts
Drifting past in endless legions,
I rom their dim, tenebrous regions—
All the followers of the Reaper, all the
Pallid Captain’s hosts.
As he passed men ceased from weeping ;
Stopped their plaints, and fell to sleeping.
Each forgot his love or hatred, bowed his
head, and closed his eyes;
Knew no more his hope or sorrow ;
Yesterday had gone, to-morrow
would not ever be; no waking evermore to
earth and skies.
So he rides alone, eternal,
As a god with mien supernal;
Not in arrogance nor anger, quickened not
by any spur;
Bearing, through all shadows fretful,
Largess of a rest forgetful—
Peace alike to every spirit—Friend of All,
the Harvester!
VICTORIA.
C. G. A. COLLES.
The Passing of Inga Sosa.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I FIRST met old Pedro Sosa some 10
years ago. Pedro was the father of
Inga, and to understand what manner
of man the son became one must know
something of his revered parent. Allowing
for difference of race, and a rougher envir-
onment, father and son were examples of
two types not uncommon in the Australia of
20 years ago.
Pedro was a Paraguayan of the usual
campo breed, partly Spanish and mostly In-
dian. He was a small, wizened, monkey-
like man, tough as hickory, and cunning as
a fox. Two years ago, when he was nearly
80, I saw him race his famous mare —riding
bareback, as the fashion is here—on the
course, and, all but, pull off the event. The
mare lost, in spite of good riding, because
she had been underfed; which leads me nat-
urally to tell that Pedro was that'very rare
thing among Paraguayans—a skinflint. He
had a goodly slice of land lying about three
leagues from Oosme Colony, with probably
1000 head of cattle on it. Also, he was reck-
oned to possess a plant of money stowed
away in, or about, his rambling old home-
stead (no bursting banks for Pedro), but
he underfed everything belonging to him
which wanted more than camp grass to eat
—himself, his women, his children, his peons,
even, as 1 have said, his racing mare. Also
he underpaid and overreached —by means of
the law, or without it—-all who fiad dealings
with him.
A noted desperado, one Algerin, and his
band, had paid a surprise visit to the colony,
“ ’twixt the dawn and the day,” and lifted
two of our best horses, what time the colon-
ists were safely dreaming of The Regenera-
tion of Mankind, an Unlimited Meat Diet,
and other unlikely happenings. This being
the last of a long series of crimes committed
in the district, the Government got a move
on, and sent out a big force of soldiers. Al-
gerin, badly wounded, escaped owing to his
excellence as a rider and bushman. His
lieutenant, a gentleman credited with over
20 homicidios, was captured, unwounded, but
“died,” with great suddenness, on his way to
the local town of Caazapff, and the band
generally were either annihilated, or trans-
formed into soldiers after the thrifty Para-
guayan custom.
Some weeks after, we learnt that one of
our missing horses was in the keeping of
Pedro Sosa. How he got there nobody seemed
to know, but, armed with a portentous-look-
ing document, having many seals and signa-
tures, I was despatched to recover him.
Pedro invited me into his galpon, gave my
horse a generous feed of maize, and in a short
time set me in front of a mighty breakfast
of tender beef and trimmings. He also
offered me a second mate’s nip of fine old
cana. This, with a sigh, I passed, but ac-
cepted an excellent cigar, and suggested the
bringing up of the grey horse. The old man,
laying his hand affectionately on my shoul-
der, said that, owing to the respect which he
had for me and for all the Australian colon-
ists, he meant to give us something better
than an old grey horse. Taking me round
to his corral, he produced, for inspection, a
one-eyed, broken-kneed. melancholy-looking
mule, which he described, with much eloquent
blasphemy, as an untiring roadster, and an
animal which dearly loved plough work. Ulti-
mately I got away with the grey and out of
the sound of Pedro’s curses in Guarani.
About a couple of miles this side of the
estancia, the road winds round to escape a
labyrinth of dry watercourses, dead tree
trunks, washed down during floods,. and
matted scrub. Just as I arrived at this un-
lovely spot, a small mob of cattle entered it
- —on the run—from the opposite side. After
them came three horsemen. Tavo of them
promptly and prudently checked their horses,
and wheeled round the clear ground. The
third, who was young Inga Sosa, charged
through the mess-up at a gallop. Doubtless
his fine horse and he both knew the ground
well, but it was a pretty piece of horseman-
ship for all that, and the rider put a neat
finish to his work by dropping his lasso, at
an impossibly long range, over the horns of
one of the fat steers, when they had all
reached the fair ground. His horse stopped
dead, propped out his fore feet, bent his
hind legs till his haunches almost touched
the ground, and, when the shock came to
the tightened rawhide rope, the steer went
down with a crash. It was the best-done
piece of work of the kind I had seen in Para-
guay, and I examined the rider with inter-
est. He was little more than a lad, tall,
with extremely long legs (the sort of legs
that can get a grip all down, and under, a
horse’s. flanks), the promise of a fine chest
and pair of shoulders, an Indian face of the
well-bred type, thin lipped, with straight eye-
brows over eyes which held one with their
direct, unwinking gaze, half fierce, half
haughty.
I saw but little of Inga during the follow-
ing years, but I heard plenty. His father
and he hated each other cordially ; his mother
stuck to him. Absolutely without book edu-
cation—old Pedro didn’t believe in anyone
belonging to him losing time fooling around
school —able even to talk but little Spanish,
there was yet so much unspoiled force and
vigor in the man that he became a little tin
god in the neighborhood. He broke away
from the old he-parent, and raided the pater-
nal herds with a daring and cunning which
drove that worthy person frantic. The suc-
cessful manner of his wooing of women, and
the equally fortunate outcome of his fre-
quent fights with men, his skill and daring
at the rodea (cattle muster), and his untir-
ing grace at the dance, are not these known
to all men, from Santa Catalina to San An-
tonio?
Two years ago Paraguay struck troublous
times, and achieved four revolutions in 12
months. These settled nothing definite, but
paved the way for what may be called the
Old Man Revolution of 1912, which settled
many things, including the earthly careers
of some two to four thousand Paraguayans,
and those of a few odd foreigners whose luck
was out, or who pushed their heads into poli-
tics. The redoubtable Inga came to his own
at this time. After much three-cornered
fighting and intriguing, the struggle resolved
itself into one between the Liberales (who
were actively supported by foreign capital-
ists), and Colonel Jara, who had been suc-
cessively War Minister, President, Dictator
and exile. Jara is old bones now, but he
was a bonnie fighter and a boss intriguer a
year ago. The Liberales, well armed, well
fed, with an overflowing army chest, held
Asuncion and Encarnacion, and with them
the two ends of Paraguay’s one railway line.
Also, they held the rivers, which are Para-
guay’s outlet to the world, with some of the
“Mystery Ships” which the English press
was babbling about a couple of years ago.
In between was Jara, master of a portion of
the line, and with an uncertain hold on the
districts on either side. He had to live on
the country, or not at all. He preferred to
live, and Inga was one of the men who helped
him do it. He rounded up peons, cattle and
horses, looted stores for arms and clothing,
fought, and beat, small detachments of the
enemy wherever he found them. Inga could
find most things he was after.
At the head of his hard-bitten, sleepless
little.force he paid the colony a few visits,
just to borrow, in a friendly way, a fat horse
or two. A fine figure of a man he looked in
thigh riding-boots, khaki tunic, and flapping
sombrero; a mauser across his saddle-bow
and the best of automatics in his belt. A
fat Italian storekeeper in Caazap& sighed for
these lost treasures. During one of Inga’s
absences from that town, the bands of the
other side made a swoop on to it, capturing
old Pedro Sosa by the way. They failed to
find his hidden treasure, and, on arriving at
Caazapd, gave him a brief choice of death, or
ransom. _ The old pirate stood firm on the
sacred rights of Property, and coughed up,
with the last of his blood, a final curse on
all robbers. Inga and his men returned un-
expectedly the same night, and saw the old
man respectably buried, with quite a collec-
tion of strange corpses round him.
After some months of this, finding that
the Liberales refused to attack him, in spite
of their numbers and armament, Jara called
in his scattered outposts and marched on
Asuncion. A considerable portion of the
enemy had left the entrenched base at Para-
guari, a town some distance from the capital,
and was stationed on the line at Ibitimi,
about 24 miles nearer Jara. This force had
mounted a fine 4-inch gun on a railway
truck, and was camped close up on either
side of it. Learning this, Jara, a most in-
genious person, loaded up one of his locomo-
tives with dynamite and other explosives, got
a full head of steam on, and let her go. lie
would have brought oft this fine coup had it
not been for the alertness of a German in-
structor on the other side. Your soldier of
fortune of to-day is no longer the English-
man, but the German, and a mighty fine
adventurer he makes. He sucks in lan-
guages like mother’s milk, has a good head
for liquor, much stolid, enduring patience
in licking raw levies into shape, and never
reckons that he is “quite good enough” (as
I heard one crack shot sav) till nothing can
live within the range of his own rifle or re-
volver. The particular German of Ibitimi
planted a shell from his gun into the loco-
motive almost on sight. Engine, tender,
permanent way and the surrounding scenery
were blasted into nothingness in a twink-
ling.
Leaving a company or so to make a show-
ing, Jara secretly swung his force off the
line, and, travelling through dense forests by
old half-forgotten roads, endeavored to get
between the hostile armies, and attack Para-
guari before they could unite. He did the dis- •
tance in an incredibly short time, inspiring
his army to step out and be lively, partly
by shooting stragglers who suffered from
sore (or cold) fefet, and partly (so it is
said) by promising a three-days’ sack of
Asuncion, to the brave who lived to get
there. On arrival at Paraguari, however,
he found both armies joined. Probably
treachery had been at work; it usually is in
civil wars. Jara attacked with great vigor
and skill, and. in spite of all handicaps,
nearly pushed his way through. The artil-
lery against him was too heavy, however.
His army—what was left of it—rolled back.

DROPSY
Watery Swellings under the Eyes and in the Ankles, Limbs and Body.
The fluid you take with your food,
in a single month , weighs as much as
your entire body.*
Most of this fluid has to pass out
through the kidneys, bladder and
urinary system.
Imagine what would happen if the
outlets for this water were stopped up !
In a few hours the limbs and body
would swell to a great size with the
collected water.
That is what actually takes place in
dropsy.
Dropsy is the name given to the
soft, watery swellings which appear in
the ankles, limbs and body, under the
eyes pr in the eyelids, or on the backs
of the hands. If you press your finger
into the swollen flesh the pitting slowly
fills up with the water again.
If you are of average weight.)

DROPSICAL EYES.
DROPSY is not a disease in itself,
bnt it is serious because it indicates
serious disease.
It may show that the kidneys are
failing to separate the waste water from
the blood, or that there is something
obstructing the urinary canals and
preventing the waste water passing out ;
or it may be caused by defective aCtion
of the heart.
Tapping takes away the water from
under the skin, but more water soon
colleCts, because tapping cannot reach
the cause of the dropsy, and as long
as the cause remains the dropsy will
continue.
Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills have
a quick and diredl action on the kidneys
and bladder. They promote a free flow
from the system, washing out clogging
impurities from the passages, and
draining out the collected water through
the natural channels. They gently
lead the kidneys back to health and
activity, and thus reach the cause of
most cases of dropsy.
Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills have
no action on the heart, nor on the liver,
stomach or bowels ; they are solely for
the kidneys and urinary system, and
are, therefore, of the highest value in
dropsy, gravel, stone and all diseases
arising from kidney and bladder trouble.
Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills are sold by
all chemists and storekeepers at 2/9 per bott e
(6 bottles 15/3), or post free by Poster-
McClellan Co., 76 Pitt Street, Sydney.

Jara, wounded in the head and abdomen, the
last man on his last gun, checked a counter-
attack of the Liberales. The time had come
for a Forlorn Hope. Calling up his 4W
horsemen, “the flower of his army,” he bade
them ride up a protecting gully and charge
home on the enemy, while he gathered up
his footmen. Through some error —his own
or another’s, I know not —the mounted men
broke cover and rode over a rise on which
was planted a 'fine battery of machine and
field guns. “ Considerably more than half of
the fbl’ce wds a huddled heap of dead and
wounded iPefi and horses in a few minutes;
The tail disappeared. 1
The 1 battery had’ ceased firing, when out
froin the dust ahd the reek, a horseman
canie gallofnng obliquely tbwards it! When
som£ 200 yards off, 1 he seemed to turn his
liorse slightly and mpke straight for its
centre. A soldier, anxious for distinction,
covered the rider with liis mpuser, but Re-
ceived a rap op the head frpm the butt of
the revofver of p German officer. Tfiis sol-
dier of fortune had never seen ope man
charge a battery before, and wanted to
fearp how it was to be done. A fine sight
he made coming up that rise. The great,
rawboned, fiddle-headed roan, his chest
streaming blood from a shell wound, and
the tall rider, Mauser across his saddle-bow
and flapping sombrero, seemed cut out of
one block. Within ten yards of the centre
gun lay a big boulder. Too spent to rise to
it, the roan struck heavily and came down,
a thing all legs and head. The rider sailed
over the rock and lay quiet on his face. The
German ran out and turned him on his back.
He was dead. A rifle, or machine gun, bul-
let had caught him near the heart. With
the last instinct of a rider and a soldier he
had got a final grip with his long legs round
the horse’s flanks, jamming the spurs into
them, and, convulsively grasping his gun,
had, with darkening sight, made for the
middle of what lay in front of him.
So passed Inga Sosa.
D. R. STEVENSON.
The Mulgaland Emporium.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WHEN you go into a remote back-
block store it is no use being
fastidious, and setting your mind
on any particular brand or style of goods.
Your tastes must conform to the_ limitations
of the warehouse. You will go in to buy a
black suit, and you will come out with a
grey one —which may be either a size too
small or two sizes too large. Bushmen are
seldom neatly dressed; there is always a
“slop” air about them, their clothes being
too skimpy or far too big. Not infre-
quently, too, the trousers, coat and vest of
the best suit are composed of different
materials and of a strikingly loud pattern.
Dress counts for little outback, and the men
who live there are not particular. Still,
they dress as well as they can, which means
as well as the backblock store will permit
them to.
The storekeeper doesn’t go to the expense
of keeping a buyer, or travelling down to the
city to choose for himself, and commercial
travellers are rarely seen in the far interior.
He depends on price lists and samples by
post, but mostly on the price, buying cheap
goods, and retailing them at a profit of
about 300 per cent.
The prevailing fashions are little thought
of. Fashions, anyhow, are apt to change
while the goods are in transit; for the teams
are often six months, and occasionally
twelve months, on the road. That is
another reason why the backblock folk are
so easily picked out when they visit the
city. They are decades behind the times.
Many of them get their clothes by parcels
post—which is a great source of complaint
to the storekeeper. Some of them get
samples and send their measurements to a
tailor; but as the measurements are often
taken per aid of a piece of string and the
station blacksmith’s rule, as likely as not the
resultant fit makes the would-be well-dressed
boundary-rider look a guy. So most of
them trust to ready-mades when they dress
by post. They do so for cheapness. In all
other respects they have to take pot luck-
in the same way as the stores, many of
which are made a dumping ground for rub-
bish, for last year’s materials, and goods
that have been more or less a drug on the
market. The farther out the store is situ-
ated, and the more inexperienced the pro-
prietor, the more he is imposed on in this
way.
Sometimes the proprietor is a navvy, or a
blacksmith who puts down his tools of
trade to serve a passing traveller with tea
and sugar. Sometimes he is a baker, who
comes in covered with flour and dough to
sell a prospective bridegroom a starched
shirt. Again he is a butcher and a JP-,
who dispenses sausages, softgoods, hard-
ware, groceries and law as required. Again,
the “general store” is sometimes run in con-
junction with an hotel business, and per-
haps with a butchery and bakery in addi-
tion, in which case the customer frequently
departs with empty pockets, and a big swag
of things he has no use for, and for the
purchasing of which he severely censures
himself next morning.
* * * *
Johnny Allsorts’ Mulgaland Emporium
was a conglomeration of many things. In
the first place it was the post office and the
terminus of the mail-coach, the mails beyond
being carried on horseback. He had, there-
fore, many callers on mail day—which was
his busy day—but as many of these got their
light goods by parcels post, and he had to
sort out the packages and deliver them to
the owners, acting thus as a sort of ser-
vant for his rivals down below, they were
not all friends of his. He was not an
amiable man, and he nursed a bitter grudge
against anybody who did not patronise local
industry.
“Help your district by keeping the money
in it,” was Johnny’s motto.
His charges were calculated to keep the
money all under one roof. He was more
than usually acrimonious when the mail
contained tin dishes, perambulators, blan-
kets and such bulky materials. They em-
phasised what Mulgaland was losing, and
the disgraceful lack of pride in the district.
The Emporium was a ramshackle struc-
ture, one storey high, with an iron roof. The
rear half was built of packing cases. From
front to back it was crowded with a won-
derful assortment of things—-mostly things
that nobody ever wanted to buy all mixed
up in a higgledy-piggledy fashion. inere
was scarcely room for more than one to
wriggle into the shop at a time, and bareJj
space enough on the counter to roil up a
parcel. When a customer asked tor any-
thing that was not in frequent request, he
would give Johnny a hand to find it and
thus put in the time. There was not a great
variety of brands, but in boxes behind the
counter there was a fine assortment ot spare
labels, designed—with the help of a paste
brush —to suit all tastes and requirements.
Adelaide preserves were changed to nest
English” in the twinkle of an eye, and Eng-
lish, American and Australian boots were
hauled out of the one case, and priced in
accordance with what the purchaser desired
to call them! . ,
His most profitable line was clothing, tie
was always well-dressed himself that is, he
always had new clothes on. Rarely did he
wear a suit more than a week. Before the
creases had time to disappear he would roll
it up carefully, and replace it on the shelves
for sale. Then he would take a few days
out of another one. Thus, though he wore
as many suits in a year as the greatest
dandy in the land, lie never paid anything
for that kind of dress. Besides affording
him the pleasure of dressing well on nothing
a vear, he considered it an excellent way ot
showing his wares. With a cigar in his
mouth, he strutted about where lie could dis-
play them to the best advantage, and it
they caught somebody’s eye, or provoked a
remark, he plunged at once into business.
It was the most comfortable and best-wear-
ing suit he had ever put on. Had just one
more left —dirt cheap, at so much.
Perhaps the other party would decide to
have a look at it, and a sale would result.
Perhaps Johnny would have only the one
outfit of the sort, in which case lie would
slip into a back room and change. Hats and
caps were treated in the same way; so
were overcoats in winter, and waterproofs
and ginghams in wet weather. When articles
were wanted that were not in stock in the
store, and Johnny said that he had some at
his house, it could be taken for granted that
the family had been making good use of
them. Johnny’s women-folk were accus-
tomed to partially undress at almost any
hour. . , , at
Opposite the Emporium was an hotel. Mr.
Allsorts kept a close eye on that establish-
ment through his window. He noted all the
arrivals, and whenever it seemed worth while
he sauntered across and joined them in a
drink, and afterwards a game of cards, or a
game of billiards. He was always sober,
but nevertheless a good customer to the
publican. He knew the failings of the men
who dwelt in the lonely backblocks. Many
of them came in to do shopping, but left
their shopping till the last, and often made
their purchases when they were too muddled
to know what they were doing. Mr. All-
sorts was an expert at catching the cheque
man at the psychological moment. If he
made no direct allusion to wanting any-
thing, something would be adroitly intro-
duced into the conversation at cards that
would necessitate a visit to the store.
Once in that seclusion, Mr. Allsorts would
warn him as a wellwisher to look out for
the party who ran the hotel. The publican
was a sharper who would take him down if
he got the least chance. The visitor would
be advised in a friendly way, if he had
much money on him, to leave it at the store
for safe custody. This he would probably
do, for it is common for a bushman to hand
his money to someone he knows to look after
for him when he is drinking.
Mr. Allsorts was also a wine and spirit
merchant. His license permitted him to
sell liquor only in wholesale quantities, but
that did not prevent him from giving it away
in nobblers as frequently as he liked. He
was liberal with it to his customers. The
cheque man received a nip as soon as he had
been rescued from the hotel. Then Mr. All-
sorts would be more inclined to talk than to
do business. He would want to know all
the newrn from farther-out, and about all
the people he knew who lived there. In the
course of this chat the bottle would be intro-
duced a couple more times. Then Johnny
would get down to real hard work, and sell
until the cheque was exhausted. In the
account that the customer got when leaving
towm, numerous small sums were set down
as cash, which, it was explained, had been
given to him from time to time and spent at
the hotel. In this way Johnny demonstrated
that lambing down could be accomplished
just as well in a store as in a roadside pub.
Like other backblock storekeepers, lie did
not depend wholly for custom on those who
came to the shop. He travelled round the
stations and sheds periodically with a
hawker’s van, carrying only what he had a
good chance of selling, and taking orders for
anything else. He wanted his van empty
for the return trip, for he bought up sheep-
skins at the sheds. These he stored at the
back of his own premises until he bad
enough to load a waggon. Then with his
own team he took them himself to the near-
est railway town, and brought back a load
of store goods. In the meantime his wife
looked after the Emporium. She did not
take as much in six months as he did in six
weeks. Mrs. Allsorts was too honest. Still
her tenure of office helped to clear the
moral atmosphere.
In his travels he visited all deserted
camps and huts, gathering up anything he
could find that “might come in useful.” lie
never passed a horseshoe or any bit of old
iron on the road. He also purchased old
tools and other second-hand things. His
back premises were choked up with a mis-
cellaneous pile of old lumber—“his forty
years’ gathering.” The main objection
people had to Johnny Allsorts was in regard
to his “shinplasters.” These were simply
“promises to pay”—flimsy notes of the face
value of 10s. and 20s. —but they were not
drawn on any bank, and there was no sound
guarantee behind them ; so they were no use
to anybody outside the district. He loved
to give them in exchange for cheques and
good money, but did not like it a little bit if
he was tendered them in payment for goods.
If any got lost or destroyed in circulation,
that was the issuer’s profit, and Johnny
wanted his notes to be out taking risks—not
lying safely at home.
EDWARD S. SORENSON.

The “Aster”
For Precise Work.
The “Aster” is not a big lathe. It has been built for
the man who turns out a great deal of small, precise
work. Its accuracy is really remarkable —enabling
one to work to almost the thickness of a shadow. It
has sin. centres, runs in outside spring conic,al
boxes, which do not alter the axial position of the
spindles by adjustment. The spindle is provided with
face ball-bearings. Whitworth and Metric pitches
may be cut on the “ASTER” as well as gaspipe
threads.
